Definition of al-Khassa (Proper Accident)
Author defines the fourth universal (al-khassa) and gives its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the four components of the definition of al-khassa (proper accident) and apply them to determine whether a given universal is a proper accident.

Core Idea
The proper accident is a non-essential predicate exclusive to a single species.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth universal, al-khassa, as defined by the author. The definition has several parts, each crucial for distinguishing it from other universals like the differentia or common accident. We will walk through the definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find where the author states 'al-khassa' and note that the definition follows immediately after the colon.
2
Extract the genus (al-kharij 'an al-mahiyya)
The first part says 'al-kharij 'an al-mahiyya' meaning external to the essence. This is the broader category: attributes not part of the quiddity.
This excludes necessary concomitants that are part of the essence, like the differentia.
3
Extract the differentia (maqul 'ala ma tahta haqiqatin wahidatan faqat)
The definition specifies that this predicate is applied only to individuals under a single reality (one species). This distinguishes it from accidental predicates that apply to multiple species.
The word 'fakat' (only) is crucial to exclude common accidents.
4
Note the qualifier 'qawlan 'aradiyyan'
The definition ends with 'as an accidental predication'. This emphasizes that the property is not essential, even if it is exclusive.
There is debate about whether this is redundant given the first part; we will address that in the next lesson.
5
Test the definition with an example
Consider 'laughing' (al-dahik). It is external to the human essence (humans are not defined by laughing), applies only to humans, and is accidental. So it is a proper accident.
Try to think of other examples like 'writing' (al-kitaba) – also exclusive to humans in its true sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات [الخاصة] (الرَّابع) من الكلِّيَّات : (الخَاصَّةُ : وَهُوَ الخَارِجُ عَنِ المَاهِيَّةِ المقُوْلُ عَلَى مَا تَحْتَ حَقِيقَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ فَقَطْ قَوْلاً عَرَضِيًّا ). وفي العبارة بحثٌ؛ لأنَّ قولَه : «الخارج» الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : الْمَقُوْلُ) أي : المحمول حمل مواطأة، ولو من غير سؤال، كأن يُقال: زيد ضاحك ، عمرو ضاحك ... إلخ ، أو يقال: زيد، وبكر، وعمرو شيءٍ يميّزُهُم حالة كونه من عرضيَّاتهم؟، فيقال: ضاحك. (قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى مَا :أي: على أفراد تحتَ حقيقةٍ واحدة، ولَمَّا كان هذا لا يُنافي أنَّه يُقالُ على أفرادِ حقيقةٍ أُخرى ؛ زادَ لفظ : (فقط) ؛ لأجل أَنْ يُفيدَ أَنَّهُ لَا يُقالُ على أفراد حقيقة أخرى. العطار (قَوْلُهُ : الخَاصَّةُ) تاؤها للنقل مِنَ الوصفيَّة إلى الاسمية، قال في شرح المطالع : وَهِيَ مَقولةٌ بِالاشتراكِ على معنيين : أحدُهَا : ما يخصُّ الشَّيءَ بِالقياس إلى كُلِّ مغايرةٍ وتُسمَّى خاصَّةٌ مُطلقةً، وَهِيَ الَّتي عُدَّتْ مِنَ الخمسة، وثانيهما : ما يخص الشَّيءَ بِالقياس إلى بعض مَا يُغايرُهُ ، وتُسمَّى خاصَّةً إضافيَّةً ا . هـ (قَوْلُهُ: وَهُوَ أي : الخاصَّةُ والتَّذكير باعتبار الخبر وهو قوله : (الخارج). (قَوْلُهُ : وَفِي العِبَارَةِ بَحْثُ مُحصِّلُهُ استدراكُ قَوْلِهِ : (قولاً عرضيًّا)؛ بناءً على أنَّهُ ثابت في نسخ، والذي شرح عليه شيخُ الإسلام وغيره إسقاطه، واعلَمْ أَنَّ التَّعريف الواقع وهو في عبارة غيرِهِ هكذا : وهو المقولُ عَلَى مَا تحتَ حقيقةٍ واحدةٍ فقط قولاً عرضيًّا أو قولاً غير ذاتي والمصنِّفُ جمع بين قولِهِ الخارج عن الماهيةِ وقَولاً عرضيا، فحكم الشارح بزيادةِ قَوْلِهِ : (قولاً عرضيًّا ) لِتَقديم قوله : الخارج عَنِ الماهية عنه ، وَبِهِ تمَّ التَّعريفُ ؛ إلَّا أَنَّهُ جعلهُ بِمنزلة الجنسِ، وَمَا بعدَهُ بمنزلة الفصل، وكأنَّهُ جرى على القول بوجوب تقديم الجنس على الفصل، وقد نقل الدَّوَّانِيُّ في فصل المعرفاتِ عن الشَّيخ في بعض تعليقاتِهِ : ناطق حيوان حد تام ، إلَّا أنَّ الأَوْلَى تقديم الأعمّ لِشهرَتِهِ وظهورِهِ، نعم ؛ لَا بُدَّ مِن تقييد أحدِهِمَا بِالآخَرِ حَتَّى تحصل صورة
Section: On the principles of concepts. (The fourth) of the universals: (al-Khassa: it is what is external to the essence, predicated upon what is under a single reality only, as an accidental predication.) And in the expression there is a discussion because of his saying 'al-kharij'. Al-Dasuqi (his saying: al-maqul) i.e., the predicate with a predication of identity, even without a question, like it is said: Zaid is laughing, Amr is laughing, etc., or it is said: Zaid, Bakr, and Amr are something that distinguishes them while being among their accidents, so it is said: laughing. (His saying: 'ala ma) i.e., upon individuals under a single reality. And since this does not negate that it is said upon individuals of another reality, he added the word 'only' in order to convey that it is not said upon individuals of another reality. Al-Atar (His saying: al-khassa) its ta is for transference from adjectival to nominal. He said in Sharh al-Mutali': It is homonymously applied to two meanings: one of them is what is specific to a thing in relation to everything else, called absolute khassa (al-khassa al-mutlaqa), and it is the one counted among the five; and the second is what is specific to a thing in relation to some of what differs from it, called relative khassa (al-khassa al-idafiyya). (His saying: wa-huwa i.e., al-khassa and the masculine [pronoun] is due to considering the predicate, which is his saying 'al-kharij'.) (His saying: wa fi al-ibara bahth) its summary is the addition of his saying 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' based on it being present in some copies, but the one upon which Shaykh al-Islam and others commented omits it. Know that the actual definition in the wording of others is: it is what is predicated upon what is under a single reality only, as an accidental predication or as a non-essential predication. And the author combined his saying 'al-kharij 'an al-mahiyya' and 'qawlan 'aradiyyan'. So the commentator judged that his saying 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' is additional because he preceded his saying 'al-kharij 'an al-mahiyya' to it, and with it the definition is complete. However, he made it like the genus and what follows like the differentia, as if he followed the view of obligating the precedence of the genus over the differentia. And al-Dawwani narrated in the chapter of definitions from the Shaykh in some of his comments: 'natiq hayawan hadd tam', except that it is better to precede the more general due to its fame and clarity. Yes, it is necessary to restrict one with the other until the form is obtained.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth universal, al-khassa (the proper accident), and then comments on a textual debate about its definition. The author states: 'Al-Khassa is the [predicate] external to the essence, predicated on what falls under a single reality only, as an accidental predication.' The definition contains four parts: (1) 'external to the essence' means it is not part of the quiddity; (2) 'predicated on what is under a single reality' means it applies only to one species, e.g., 'laughing' (al-dahik) is predicated only on humans; (3) 'only' excludes accidental predicates that apply to more than one species; (4) 'as an accidental predication' clarifies that the predication is accidental, not essential. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that 'maqul' means 'predicate with a predication of identity', and he gives an example: 'Zaid is laughing, Amr is laughing'—laughter is a proper accident of humans. He notes that without 'only', the definition would allow the predicate to apply to another species, so it is necessary. Al-Atar then clarifies that the word 'khassa' is a transferred noun (from adjective to substantive) and lists two meanings: absolute khassa (specific to one thing in relation to all others) and relative khassa (specific in relation to some). The text ends with a discussion about whether the phrase 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' is redundant given the earlier 'kharij 'an al-mahiyya'. The commentator notes that some copies include it, others omit it. The author combined both 'kharij 'an al-mahiyya' and 'qawlan 'aradiyyan', leading to a debate: if 'kharij' already implies accidental, then 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' is superfluous. However, the commentator treats 'kharij' as the genus and 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' as the differentia, following the principle that the genus should precede the differentia. Al-Dawwani is quoted as saying that 'natiq hayawan' is a complete definition even though the genus ('hayawan') is stated after the differentia ('natiq'), indicating that precedence is not obligatory but preferable for clarity. The main point is that the proper accident is a non-essential attribute that belongs exclusively to one species.

الخَارِجُ عَنِ المَاهِيَّة
مَقُول
قَوْلاً عَرَضِيًّا

Checkpoint

A) 'Hayawan' (animal) predicated of humans and horses
B) 'Dahik' (laughing) predicated only of humans
C) 'Natiq' (rational) as a differentia of humans
D) 'Abyad' (white) predicated of cotton and snow

Key Takeaway
The proper accident is a non-essential predicate that belongs exclusively to one species, defined by being external to the essence and predicated only on a single reality with accidental predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the universal 'al-istihala' (metamorphosis). Is it a proper accident of humans? Use the definition of khassa to argue that it is not, because it applies to many species (e.g., caterpillars) and is not exclusive to humans.
?
Provide an example from Quranic sciences: 'al-tanzil' (the act of revealing) is often considered a proper accident of Jibreel (Angel Gabriel). Test whether it meets the definition of khassa.

Textual Debate on the Definition of al-Khassa
Author examines a textual debate about the wording of al-khassa's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the textual debate about the redundancy of 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' and the precedence of the genus over the differentia in definitions.

Core Idea
Redundancy in definitions is a matter of precision; the order of components is conventional, not logical.

The definition of al-khassa given by the author includes the phrase 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' (accidental predication). Some scholars argue this is redundant because 'kharij 'an al-mahiyya' already implies accidental. Others add it to emphasize the distinction from the differentia. Additionally, there is a discussion about whether the genus must precede the differentia. We will explore these debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic phrase
The author says 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' at the end of the definition. The commentator notes that some copies omit this phrase, and the definition by others only includes up to 'fakat'.
2
Understand the argument for redundancy
If 'kharij 'an al-mahiyya' already means the attribute is not part of the essence, then it implies it is accidental. So adding 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' would be unnecessary.
This view is not explicitly stated but implied by the commentator's mention of omission.
3
Examine the counterargument
The commentator treats 'kharij' as the genus and 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' as the differentia. He follows the rule that genus must precede differentia, so the inclusion of both is justified to complete the definition.
But the rule of precedence is not obligatory, as shown by al-Dawwani's quote.
4
Analyze the issue of order
Al-Dawwani quotes the Shaykh saying 'natiq hayawan' is a complete definition, showing that differentia can precede genus. So precedence is for clarity, not logical necessity.
The commentator's reasoning may be based on a preference for conventional order.
5
Evaluate the robustness of the definition
Considering both points, the definition with or without 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' is logically sound. The extra phrase ensures no confusion with the differentia, which is also external but essential. So it is useful for pedagogical clarity.
The debate shows the meticulousness of logicians in refining definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات [الخاصة] (الرَّابع) من الكلِّيَّات : (الخَاصَّةُ : وَهُوَ الخَارِجُ عَنِ المَاهِيَّةِ المقُوْلُ عَلَى مَا تَحْتَ حَقِيقَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ فَقَطْ قَوْلاً عَرَضِيًّا ). وفي العبارة بحثٌ؛ لأنَّ قولَه : «الخارج» الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : الْمَقُوْلُ) أي : المحمول حمل مواطأة، ولو من غير سؤال، كأن يُقال: زيد ضاحك ، عمرو ضاحك ... إلخ ، أو يقال: زيد، وبكر، وعمرو شيءٍ يميّزُهُم حالة كونه من عرضيَّاتهم؟، فيقال: ضاحك. (قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى مَا :أي: على أفراد تحتَ حقيقةٍ واحدة، ولَمَّا كان هذا لا يُنافي أنَّه يُقالُ على أفرادِ حقيقةٍ أُخرى ؛ زادَ لفظ : (فقط) ؛ لأجل أَنْ يُفيدَ أَنَّهُ لَا يُقالُ على أفراد حقيقة أخرى. العطار (قَوْلُهُ : الخَاصَّةُ) تاؤها للنقل مِنَ الوصفيَّة إلى الاسمية، قال في شرح المطالع : وَهِيَ مَقولةٌ بِالاشتراكِ على معنيين : أحدُهَا : ما يخصُّ الشَّيءَ بِالقياس إلى كُلِّ مغايرةٍ وتُسمَّى خاصَّةٌ مُطلقةً، وَهِيَ الَّتي عُدَّتْ مِنَ الخمسة، وثانيهما : ما يخص الشَّيءَ بِالقياس إلى بعض مَا يُغايرُهُ ، وتُسمَّى خاصَّةً إضافيَّةً ا . هـ (قَوْلُهُ: وَهُوَ أي : الخاصَّةُ والتَّذكير باعتبار الخبر وهو قوله : (الخارج). (قَوْلُهُ : وَفِي العِبَارَةِ بَحْثُ مُحصِّلُهُ استدراكُ قَوْلِهِ : (قولاً عرضيًّا)؛ بناءً على أنَّهُ ثابت في نسخ، والذي شرح عليه شيخُ الإسلام وغيره إسقاطه، واعلَمْ أَنَّ التَّعريف الواقع وهو في عبارة غيرِهِ هكذا : وهو المقولُ عَلَى مَا تحتَ حقيقةٍ واحدةٍ فقط قولاً عرضيًّا أو قولاً غير ذاتي والمصنِّفُ جمع بين قولِهِ الخارج عن الماهيةِ وقَولاً عرضيا، فحكم الشارح بزيادةِ قَوْلِهِ : (قولاً عرضيًّا ) لِتَقديم قوله : الخارج عَنِ الماهية عنه ، وَبِهِ تمَّ التَّعريفُ ؛ إلَّا أَنَّهُ جعلهُ بِمنزلة الجنسِ، وَمَا بعدَهُ بمنزلة الفصل، وكأنَّهُ جرى على القول بوجوب تقديم الجنس على الفصل، وقد نقل الدَّوَّانِيُّ في فصل المعرفاتِ عن الشَّيخ في بعض تعليقاتِهِ : ناطق حيوان حد تام ، إلَّا أنَّ الأَوْلَى تقديم الأعمّ لِشهرَتِهِ وظهورِهِ، نعم ؛ لَا بُدَّ مِن تقييد أحدِهِمَا بِالآخَرِ حَتَّى تحصل صورة
Section: On the principles of concepts. (The fourth) of the universals: (al-Khassa: it is what is external to the essence, predicated upon what is under a single reality only, as an accidental predication.) And in the expression there is a discussion because of his saying 'al-kharij'. Al-Dasuqi (his saying: al-maqul) i.e., the predicate with a predication of identity, even without a question, like it is said: Zaid is laughing, Amr is laughing, etc., or it is said: Zaid, Bakr, and Amr are something that distinguishes them while being among their accidents, so it is said: laughing. (His saying: 'ala ma) i.e., upon individuals under a single reality. And since this does not negate that it is said upon individuals of another reality, he added the word 'only' in order to convey that it is not said upon individuals of another reality. Al-Atar (His saying: al-khassa) its ta is for transference from adjectival to nominal. He said in Sharh al-Mutali': It is homonymously applied to two meanings: one of them is what is specific to a thing in relation to everything else, called absolute khassa (al-khassa al-mutlaqa), and it is the one counted among the five; and the second is what is specific to a thing in relation to some of what differs from it, called relative khassa (al-khassa al-idafiyya). (His saying: wa-huwa i.e., al-khassa and the masculine [pronoun] is due to considering the predicate, which is his saying 'al-kharij'.) (His saying: wa fi al-ibara bahth) its summary is the addition of his saying 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' based on it being present in some copies, but the one upon which Shaykh al-Islam and others commented omits it. Know that the actual definition in the wording of others is: it is what is predicated upon what is under a single reality only, as an accidental predication or as a non-essential predication. And the author combined his saying 'al-kharij 'an al-mahiyya' and 'qawlan 'aradiyyan'. So the commentator judged that his saying 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' is additional because he preceded his saying 'al-kharij 'an al-mahiyya' to it, and with it the definition is complete. However, he made it like the genus and what follows like the differentia, as if he followed the view of obligating the precedence of the genus over the differentia. And al-Dawwani narrated in the chapter of definitions from the Shaykh in some of his comments: 'natiq hayawan hadd tam', except that it is better to precede the more general due to its fame and clarity. Yes, it is necessary to restrict one with the other until the form is obtained.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth universal, al-khassa (the proper accident), and then comments on a textual debate about its definition. The author states: 'Al-Khassa is the [predicate] external to the essence, predicated on what falls under a single reality only, as an accidental predication.' The definition contains four parts: (1) 'external to the essence' means it is not part of the quiddity; (2) 'predicated on what is under a single reality' means it applies only to one species, e.g., 'laughing' (al-dahik) is predicated only on humans; (3) 'only' excludes accidental predicates that apply to more than one species; (4) 'as an accidental predication' clarifies that the predication is accidental, not essential. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that 'maqul' means 'predicate with a predication of identity', and he gives an example: 'Zaid is laughing, Amr is laughing'—laughter is a proper accident of humans. He notes that without 'only', the definition would allow the predicate to apply to another species, so it is necessary. Al-Atar then clarifies that the word 'khassa' is a transferred noun (from adjective to substantive) and lists two meanings: absolute khassa (specific to one thing in relation to all others) and relative khassa (specific in relation to some). The text ends with a discussion about whether the phrase 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' is redundant given the earlier 'kharij 'an al-mahiyya'. The commentator notes that some copies include it, others omit it. The author combined both 'kharij 'an al-mahiyya' and 'qawlan 'aradiyyan', leading to a debate: if 'kharij' already implies accidental, then 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' is superfluous. However, the commentator treats 'kharij' as the genus and 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' as the differentia, following the principle that the genus should precede the differentia. Al-Dawwani is quoted as saying that 'natiq hayawan' is a complete definition even though the genus ('hayawan') is stated after the differentia ('natiq'), indicating that precedence is not obligatory but preferable for clarity. The main point is that the proper accident is a non-essential attribute that belongs exclusively to one species.

القول العرضي
البحث
الترتيب

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the commentator includes 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' despite the presence of 'kharij 'an al-mahiyya'?
A) He considers it necessary to avoid ambiguity with the differentia.
B) He copied it from an older manuscript without checking.
C) He thinks it changes the definition's meaning completely.
D) He wants to make the definition longer for memorization.

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' in the definition of al-khassa is a point of debate, but it serves to emphasize the accidental nature, and the order of genus and differentia is a matter of clarity, not logical necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look up a definition from a Usul al-Fiqh text (e.g., the definition of 'amm' (general) as 'lafz istaghraq jami' afrad jinsihi bi bad al-dalala'). Is there any redundancy? Discuss using the same principles from this lesson.
?
Consider the definition of 'al-wajib' (obligatory) as 'ma yuthabu 'ala fihi wa yu'aqaba 'ala tarkihi'. Some scholars add 'bi shar'ir' (by Shari'a). Is this addition redundant? Argue both sides.

Defining al-Khassah (Property) and Its Logical Parts
Definition of al-khassah with critical commentary on its wording and ordering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each component of the definition of al-khassah and explain how it excludes other universals (genus, differentia, species, common accident).

Core Idea
Every part of the definition of property is a gatekeeper: it lets in only what belongs.

In the fifth universal, the author gives a compact definition of al-khassah (property). But a definition is only as good as its ability to exclude what it should not include. The commentators dissect each phrase, showing how 'outside the essence' eliminates essential predicates, 'predicated on one essence only' removes common accident, and 'accidentally' blocks essential definitions. Understanding these exclusions is key to applying the five-universals framework in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum and the definition
In the Arabic passage, find the term being defined (al-khassah) and the full definition: 'al-kharij 'an al-mahiyyah al-maqul 'ala ma tahta haqiqah wahidah faqat qawlan 'aradiyyan'. Write down each component phrase.
2
Identify what each component excludes
Work through each phrase: 'outside the essence' excludes genus, differentia, and species (since these are part of or identical to the essence). 'Predicated on one essence only' excludes common accident. 'Accidentally' ensures that only accidental predicates count as property, not essential ones.
Al-Dasuqi shows this by example: 'laughing' is accidental to humans.
3
Analyze the dispute about the ordering
The commentator criticized the ordering, arguing that 'al-maqul' should be the genus and 'ala ma tahta haqiqah wahidah' the differentia, not the reverse. Understand why: 'al-kharij' is not a proper genus because it is a negation, and ordering matters for logical definitions.
Al-Dawwani's quote about 'natiq hayawan' shows that even complete definitions may benefit from proper ordering.
4
Assess the necessity of 'qawlan 'aradiyyan'
Some manuscripts omit this phrase. The passage argues it is necessary because without it, the definition might include essential predicates. Check: if we drop 'accidentally', could property be confused with differentia?
The conclusion: 'qawlan 'aradiyyan' secures the definition against essentiality.
5
Verify that only absolute property is defined
The passage ends by noting that relative property is not covered, but that is fine because only absolute property is among the five universals. Confirm that the definition does not accidentally exclude absolute property or include relative.
Reflect on why this is not a flaw.

التَّعريف خاصَّةُ ذاتِ الواجب؛ لأنَّ المراد مِنَ الحقيقة أعم مِنَ المفهوم الاسمي والماهية الحقيقيَّة، وخاصة ذاتِ الواجب لازمةٌ لمفهوم الواجب والقديم ونحو ذلك، وأما عدم تناولِ التَّعريف الخاصة الإضافية؛ فَغَيْرُ مُضَرٌ لِمَا عَلِمْتَ مِمَّا نقلْنَاهُ سابقاً عن شرح المطالع ؛ أنَّ الَّذِي عُدَّ مِنَ الكُلِّيَّاتِ الخمسِ هُوَ الخاصَّةُ المطلقةُ، فَلَوْ تناولَ التّعريفُ الإضافيَّةَ؛ كَانَ غَيرَ مانع؛ تَأَمَّلْ.
Chapter: On the principles of concepts. The fourth of the universals: al-Khassah: It is that which is outside the essence, predicated on what is under one essence only, as an accidental expression. And in the expression there is a discussion, because his saying: 'al-kharij'... al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'al-maqul' means the predicate in a predicative relation, even without a question, such as saying: Zayd is laughing, Amr is laughing... etc., or it is said: Zayd, and Bakr, and Amr are something that distinguishes them in the state of being among their accidents, so it is said: laughing. His saying: 'ala ma: i.e., on individuals under one essence, and since this does not negate that it is predicated on individuals of another essence, he added the word 'only' so that it indicates that it is not predicated on individuals of another essence. al-`Attar: His saying: 'al-khassah', its ta is for transference from adjectival to nominal. He said in Sharh al-Matali': It is predicated by homonymy on two meanings: one of them: what pertains to the thing in relation to every other, and it is called absolute property, and it is the one counted among the five; and the second: what pertains to the thing in relation to some of what differs from it, and it is called relative property. End. (His saying: 'and it' means the property, and the masculine is due to the predicate which is his saying: 'al-kharij'. His saying: 'And in the expression there is a discussion', its summary is the objection to his saying: 'as an accidental expression', based on it being established in some manuscripts, while that which Shaykh al-Islam and others commented on is its omission. Know that the actual definition in the expression of others is: It is predicated on what is under one essence only, as an accidental expression or as a non-essential expression. The author combined his saying 'outside the essence' and 'as an accidental expression'. So the commentator judged that his saying 'as an accidental expression' is additional due to the precedence of his saying 'outside the essence' over it, and with it the definition is complete, except that he made it in the position of genus, and what follows it in the position of differentia, as if he followed the view of the necessity of giving precedence to genus over differentia. Al-Dawwani narrated in the chapter on definitions from the shaykh in some of his comments: 'Natiq hayawan' is a complete definition, except that it is better to give precedence to the more general due to its familiarity and clarity. Yes, it is necessary to restrict one of them by the other so that the form excludes other than the common accident from genus and differentia and species, because they are not outside the essence. And by his saying 'only', the common accident is excluded, because it is predicated on individuals of one essence. al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'Excludes other than the common accident from genus and differentia' means because they are two parts, and his saying 'and species' means because it is the whole essence, so it is not described as entering or exiting. Then if the commentator makes 'outside the essence' an outlet for other than the common accident, it requires it to be a differentia. But there is that: its precedence over the genus, which is 'al-maqul', is prohibited upon investigation. So it is better for the commentator to make 'al-maqul' the genus, and his saying 'on what is under one essence' a differentia excluding the genus. And his saying 'only' excludes the common accident. al-`Attar: Correspondence to the defined, and that does not require a second motion. End. Then, permissibility, the definition becomes: It is predicated on what is under one essence only, outside the essence, as an accidental expression. Thus 'predicated on what is under one essence' is a genus for the five universals, and that does not contradict the condition of oneness, because predication on one essence does not negate predication on multiple even if the two predications differ. And restrictions only exclude what contradicts them, not what differs from them. There is no incompatibility between predication on what is under one essence and predication on what is under multiple, as was established in the discussion of species. And it occurred to Shaykh al-Islam in his commentary on Isaghuji that he said: There is no need for his saying 'only' after 'one', but it is not as he said, because the restriction 'only' excludes the genus and its differentia and the common accident. And his saying 'outside' is a restriction that excludes the species and its differentia. Then the definition is not affected by the property of the Necessary Being, because what is meant by 'essence' is more general than the nominal concept and the real essence. The property of the Necessary Being is necessary for the concept of 'the Necessary' and 'the Eternal' and the like. As for the definition not covering the relative property, it is not harmful, because you know from what we narrated earlier from Sharh al-Matali' that which is counted among the five universals is the absolute property. So if the definition covered the relative one, it would not be exclusive. Reflect.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-khassah' (the property) as one of the five universals. The definition states: 'It is that which is outside the essence, predicated on what is under one essence only, as an accidental expression.' The commentator then explains each part. The phrase 'outside the essence' (al-kharij 'an al-mahiyyah) excludes genus, differentia, and species, since these are part of or identical to the essence. The phrase 'predicated on what is under one essence only' (al-maqul 'ala ma tahta haqiqah wahidah faqat) excludes the common accident ('arad 'amm), which is predicated on multiple essences. The final phrase 'as an accidental expression' (qawlan 'aradiyyan) emphasizes that the predication is accidental, not essential. 

Al-Dasuqi clarifies that 'al-maqul' here means the predicate in a predicative relation, giving examples like 'Zayd is laughing' to show how property is predicated accidentally. Al-`Attar then discusses a dispute about the wording: some manuscripts include 'qawlan 'aradiyyan', while others omit it. The commentator argues that this phrase is necessary to ensure the definition is complete, serving as a differentia to 'al-kharij' which acts as genus. However, he critiques the ordering, stating that 'al-maqul' should be the genus and 'ala ma tahta haqiqah wahidah' the differentia, since 'al-kharij' is not a proper genus. 

Further, al-`Attar addresses an objection: the definition seems to exclude the relative property (khassah idafiyyah), but only the absolute property (khassah mutlaqah) is counted among the five universals, so it is acceptable. He also refutes Shaykh al-Islam's claim that 'only' is unnecessary, showing it excludes genus and differentia as well as common accident. The passage thus refines the definition of property, ensuring it precisely distinguishes this universal from others.

The Five Universals
intermediate

Definition of Property (Khassa) and the Role of 'Only'
Commentators critique the wording of the definition of 'property' among the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the definition of 'property' (khassa) and explain why the word 'only' is necessary to exclude genus, differentia, and common accident.

Core Idea
The proper definition of property requires 'only' to exclude everything that is not coextensive with a single reality.

In this passage, commentators examine the precise wording used to define one of the five universals: the specific property (khassa). They debate whether each element of the definition is necessary and sufficient. The key dispute revolves around the word 'only' (fakat) and its function in excluding other universals. Let's walk through their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term is 'khassa' (خاصة), the specific property among the five universals. Note its role: it is a universal that is coextensive with a species but not part of its essence.
Distinguish khassa from other universals: it is neither genus, differentia, nor species.
2
Extract the suggested definition
The definition proposed is: 'the predicate upon what is under one reality only, external to the essence by accidental predication.' Break it into parts: genus = 'predicate'; differentia = 'upon what is under one reality only'; and the qualifiers 'external to the essence' and 'by accidental predication'.
The definition aims to capture the unique property of a species without including other universals.
3
Understand the function of 'only'
The word 'only' (fakat) restricts predication to a single reality. According to the commentators, it excludes genus (predicated on multiple realities), differentia (which distinguishes within a genus), and common accident (predicated on many realities). Without 'only', the definition would be too broad.
Shaykh al-Islam argued 'only' is unnecessary, but the commentators refute this.
4
Analyze the objection and response
Shaykh al-Islam claimed that 'one reality' already implies singularity. The commentators respond that 'only' explicitly excludes genus and differentia, which are also predicated on one reality in a different sense. Thus 'only' is essential for exclusivity.
The debate shows the importance of careful wording in definitions.
5
Evaluate the role of 'accidental predication'
The phrase 'by accidental predication' (qawlan 'aradiyyan) is debated. Some argue it is superfluous because 'external to the essence' already excludes species and differentia. Others keep it to emphasize that property is an accident, not part of the essence.
Al-Dassuqi suggests deleting 'external to the essence' instead, relying on 'accidental predication' alone.

فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات واحدة، وعلى غيرها كما سيجيء .

It excludes other than the common accident from genus, differentia, and species because they are not external to the essence. And by his saying 'only' he excludes the common accident because it is predicated of individuals of a reality. Al-Dassuqi: His saying: 'He excludes other than the common accident from genus and differentia' means because they are two parts, and his saying 'and species' means because it is the entirety of the essence, so it is not described as entering or exiting. Then if the commentator makes 'that which is external to the essence' a means of excluding other than the common accident, it implies that it is a differentia. But in it: that its being placed before genus, which is the predicate, is disallowed upon verification. So the better for the commentator is to make 'the predicate' the genus, and his saying 'upon what is under one reality' is a differentia excluding the genus. And his saying 'only' is an excluder of the common accident. Al-'Attar: correspondence with the defined, and that does not need a second movement. End. So upon the permissibility, the definition becomes: it is the predicate upon what is under one reality only, that which is external to the essence by accidental predication. So 'the predicate upon what is under one reality' is a genus for the five universals, and that does not contradict the restriction of oneness because predication upon one reality does not contradict predication upon many even if the two predications differ. And restrictions only exclude what contradicts them, not what differs from them. And there is no contradiction between predication upon what is under one reality and predication upon what is under many, as was presented in the discussion of species. And it occurred to Shaykh al-Islam in his commentary on Isaghuji that he said: there is no need for his saying 'only' after 'one', and it is not as he said because the restriction 'only' excludes genus, its differentia, and the common accident. And his saying 'external' is a restriction by which species and its differentia are excluded. Then the specific property of the Necessary Being does not refute the definition because by 'reality' is meant more general than the nominal concept and the real essence, and the specific property of the Necessary Being is necessary for the concept of 'Necessary' and 'Eternal' and the like. And as for the definition not covering the relative specific property, it is not harmful because of what you know from what we previously transmitted from Sharh al-Matali': that which is counted among the five universals is the absolute specific property. So if the definition covered the relative one, it would not be exclusive; reflect.

So what is other than the specific property from the universals exits from the definition, and the definition applies to them, so the restriction 'accidental predication' becomes superfluous, unless it is taken that it was mentioned after the completion of the definition to clarify the reality, as an elucidation and following the scholars, not for exclusion. And the correct is to delete it.
Al-Dassuqi: And his saying 'by accidental predication' — meaning the state of that predicate being accidental to the essence of those individuals — is an excluder of species and differentia. And as for his saying 'external to the essence', the better is to delete it, dispensing with it by his saying 'accidental'. And the conclusion: that the better for the commentator is to make his objection related to deleting his saying 'external to the essence', dispensing with it by his saying 'accidental predication', because it occurs in its proper rank, which is after the genus, not to delete 'accidental predication', dispensing with it by his saying 'external to the essence', because of the necessary consequence of putting the differentia before the genus, which is not permissible upon verification. It cannot be said: 'external to the essence' is a genus, and 'predicate ... etc.' is a differentia, and if between the genus and differentia there is generality and particularity in some respect, it is permissible to exclude by it what is included in the generality of its differentia, because we say: there is no generality here (1), and that the context of the previous definitions indicates that the genus is 'predicate', not 'external to the essence'; so reflect. His saying: 'restriction: predication' — the addition is explicative; so reflect. His saying: 'superfluous' — meaning there is no benefit in it. That his saying: 'and the correct is to delete it' — the expression 'correct' is not appropriate for his saying 'unless it is taken ... etc.' because after the answer, correction is not appropriate, so it is better for him to say: 'the appropriate is to delete it.'
Al-'Attar: His saying 'and the correct is to delete it' in his expression 'correct' along with taking it as what he mentioned before has consideration.
(1) His saying 'there is no generality here' — therein is that the generality and particularity in some respect is realized between them; they combine in 'dahik', and 'external' is unique in the common accident like 'walking', and 'predicate upon what is under a reality only' is unique in species and its differentia like 'human' and 'dahik'. So the correct is to restrict the refutation to what comes after it, or to say: the exclusion by the genus of what falls under the differentia is not known. End. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered commentary on the definition of 'specific property' (khassa) as one of the five universals. The discussion begins with a remark that the phrase 'only' (fakat) in the definition serves to exclude the common accident ('arad 'amm), genus (jins), and differentia (fasl), but not species (naw') because species is the entire essence and is not 'external' to it. The commentator al-Dassuqi then explains that the statement 'He excludes other than the common accident from genus and differentia' implies that genus and differentia are parts of the essence, while species is the whole. He criticizes the suggestion that 'that which is external to the essence' could serve as a differentia, arguing that it should be placed after the genus in the definition. He proposes that the genus should be 'predicate' (maqul) and the differentia 'upon what is under one reality'. The word 'only' excludes the common accident.

Al-'Attar then offers an alternative definition: 'It is the predicate upon what is under one reality only, that which is external to the essence by accidental predication.' He defends the necessity of 'only' against the objection of Shaykh al-Islam (likely al-Razi or someone else) who saw no need for it. Al-'Attar argues that 'only' excludes genus, differentia, and common accident. He also addresses the objection concerning the specific property of the Necessary Being (dhat al-wajib), clarifying that 'reality' here includes nominal concepts. He distinguishes between absolute specific property (khassa mutlaqa) and relative specific property (khassa idafiyya), noting that only the former is counted among the five universals.

A new chapter begins: 'On the principles of conceptualizations' (mabadi' al-tasawwurat). The text then continues with a discussion that the phrase 'accidental predication' (qawlan 'aradiyyan) might be superfluous unless it is included as an explanatory addition. Al-Dassuqi argues that it is better to delete 'external to the essence' and keep 'accidental predication' because it properly follows the genus. He refutes the possibility that 'external to the essence' could be a genus and 'predicate' a differentia due to the lack of generality-particularity relationship in that direction. The passage concludes with a marginal note from al-Sharnubi explaining the relationship of generality and particularity in some respect between the terms.

Key Takeaway
The definition of property requires the word 'only' to restrict predication to a single reality, thereby excluding genus, differentia, and common accident.

The Five Universals
beginner

Universal Accident: The Fifth Universal
Defines the fifth universal and clarifies its logical meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical accident (al-'arad al-'āmm) from the metaphysical accident and identify examples of coincident predication.

Core Idea
Universal accident is a predicable attribute, not a metaphysical substance-opposed accident.

The fifth universal, universal accident (al-'arad al-'āmm), is often misunderstood. The term 'arad in logic does not refer to an accident that inheres in a substance (like whiteness in a body), but to an attribute that can be predicated of multiple species. This lesson unpacks that distinction through the key concept of coincident predication (haml muwāṭa'ah), showing how predicates like 'walking' (māshin) derive from attributes like 'walk' (mashy). By parsing the passage, you will learn to recognize this universal and avoid common confusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fifth universal
Locate the term 'al-'arad al-'āmm' in the Arabic. Note that it is introduced as the fifth of the five universals. The passage immediately clarifies its meaning.
2
Distinguish the two meanings of 'arad
The passage warns that 'arad here does not mean the metaphysical accident opposed to substance (e.g., walking, whiteness). Instead, it means a logical accident (a'raḍī) that is attributed to the subject. Look for the phrase 'laysa al-murād bihi mā yuwāfiq al-jawhar'.
This is the core terminological clarification.
3
Understand coincident predication
The passage explains that the logical accident is predicated in a 'coincident predication' (ḥaml muwāṭa'ah). In such predication, the predicate is derived from the attribute, e.g., 'māshin' (walking) from 'mashy' (walk). Compare the two forms: 'ماش' vs 'مشي'.
The predicate shares a root with the attribute and agrees with the subject in number and gender.
4
Examine the terminological shift
The passage notes that in al-Ishārāt, this universal is simply called 'al-'arad' (accident). However, here it is specified as 'al-'arad al-'āmm' (universal accident) to avoid confusion. Recognize that the term 'arad is used in different senses across works.
This shift is highlighted in the gloss (ḥāshiyah).

الخامس) من الكليات : (العرض العام). قوله العرض العام) ليس المراد به ما قابل الجوهر كالمشي والبياض، بل المراد به العرضي المنسوب للعرض ؛ لأنه هو الذي يحمل حمل مواطأة، فيقول : الإنسان ماش لا مشي، وأبيض لا بياض. (قوله : العرض العام) وربما سمي العرض مطلقا ؛ صرح به في الإشارات، و(العرض هنا بمعنى العرضي؛ لا بمعنى المقابل للجوهر، وإن توهمه بعض المنطقيين للالتباس بين ما يوجد للموضوع وما يوجد في الموضوع؛ كذا في الحاشية.
The fifth of the universals: universal accident. His statement: 'universal accident' does not mean what is opposed to substance like walking and whiteness, rather it means the accident-like attribute related to accident; because it is what is predicated in a coincident predication, so one says: man is walking (māshin) not walk (mashy), and white (abyad) not whiteness (bayāḍ). His statement: 'universal accident' and it is sometimes called accident absolutely; it is stated in al-Isharat, and 'accident' here means attribute-like, not in the sense opposed to substance, even if some logicians imagined it due to confusion between what exists for the subject and what exists in the subject; thus in the gloss.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth universal, the universal accident (al-'arad al-'āmm), and immediately clarifies a critical terminological confusion. The author explains that the term 'arad here does not refer to the metaphysical accident that is opposed to substance (e.g., walking as an accident that inheres in a body). Rather, it refers to a logical accident (a'radi) that is attributed to the subject by way of derivation (nisba ilā al-'arad). This is why it is predicated in a 'coincident predication' (haml muwāṭa'ah), meaning that the predicate and subject share a root and the predicate is derived from the subject's attribute. The examples clarify: we say 'Zayd is walking' (māshin), not 'Zayd is walk' (mashy); 'Zayd is white' (abyad), not 'Zayd is whiteness' (bayāḍ). The predicate (māshin, abyad) is a derived form that indicates the attribute in a manner coinciding with the subject. The passage further notes that this universal is sometimes simply called 'accident' (al-'arad) in works like al-Ishārāt, but here it must be understood as the logical accident (al-'aradī), not the metaphysical accident. Some logicians fell into error due to confusion between 'what exists for the subject' (i.e., as a predicate) and 'what exists in the subject' (i.e., as an inherent accident). This gloss from the ḥāshiyah clarifies that this error stems from ambiguity, and the correct meaning is the logical attribute.

The Common Accident: Distinguishing the Predicable from the Category
Commentary defines common accident and distinguishes it from the accident opposite to substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish al-‘arad al-‘āmm (common accident) from al-‘arad al-muqābil lil-jawhar (the category accident) by applying the three criteria given in the passage.

Core Idea
The common accident is a predicable, not a category – knowing the difference prevents logical confusion in definition.

This passage from the commentary on Tadhheeb al-Mantiq clarifies that the term 'common accident' (al-‘arad al-‘āmm) does not refer to the accident opposite to substance (like whiteness or walking as qualities), but to the accidental (al-‘aradī) that is predicated congruently. This distinction is crucial because the same Arabic word 'arad can cause confusion. The commentator provides three arguments to differentiate them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
In the passage, the definiendum is 'al-‘arad al-‘āmm'. The commentator clarifies that it is not what is opposite to substance, but the accidental (al-‘aradī) predicated by congruence (haml muwāṭa’a).
Example: 'Zaid is walking' (māshin) not 'Zaid is walking-ness' (mashy).
2
Analyze the first argument
The common accident can be a substance when predicated, e.g., 'animal' for 'rational' (human). The category accident cannot be a substance.
This shows common accident crosses categories.
3
Analyze the second argument
The common accident is predicated by congruence on substance (e.g., 'man is walking'), while the category accident is only predicated derivatively (e.g., 'body is not whiteness' but 'possessor of whiteness').
Congruent predication is direct; derivative predication requires 'possessor of'.
4
Analyze the third argument
The category accident can be a genus (e.g., 'color' for 'black' and 'white'). The common accident is a co-division of the essential, not a genus in that sense.
There is a subtlety: common accident might also be genus under a different consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الْعَرْضُ الْعَامُ) ليس المرادُ بِهِ مَا قابلَ الجوهر كالمشي والبياض، بل المرادُ بِهِ العَرَضيُّ المنسوبُ لِلعَرْض ؛ لأنَّه هو الَّذي يُحمل حمل مواطأة، فيقولُ : الإنسانُ ماش لا مشي، وأبيضُ لَا بياض. (قَوْلُهُ : العَرْضُ العَامُ) وَرُبَّما سُمِّيَ العرضُ مُطلقاً ؛ صُرِّحَ بِهِ في الإشارات، و(العرضُ هُنَا بِمعنى العرضي؛ لا يمعنى المقابل للجَوهَرِ، وَإِنْ تَوهَّمَهُ بعضُ المنطقيين للالتباس بين ما يوجد للموضوع وما يوجد في الموضوع؛ كَذَا في الحاشية. وأما ما يوجد في الموضوع فإنَّهُ العرضُ المقابل للجوهر، وقد فشره الحكماء بماهية ; إذا وجدت في الخارج ; كانت في موضوع ، وعَرَّفُوا الموضوع بأنَّهُ المحل المقومُ لِمَا حلَّ فيه، وقد بَيَّنَّا ذلك في حواشي المقولات هذا، وفي شرح المطالع ليس هذا العرضُ الَّذي بإزاءِ الجوهرِ كَمَا ظَنَّهُ قوم ، بل أحدُ قِسمَي العرضيّ الَّذِي بِإِزاءِ الذَّاتي الجوهري، أمَّا أَوَّلاً ; فَلِأَنَّهُ قد يكونُ جَوهراً كَالحيوانِ لِلنَّاطِقِ دون ذلك؛ أي: العرض العام الذي يقابل الجوهَرَ، وأمَّا ثانياً ; فَلِأَنَّهُ قد يكون محمولاً على الجوهر حَمْلاً حقيقيًّا ; أي: بالمواطأةِ كَالماشي على الإنسانِ دونَ ذلكَ، فَإِنَّه لا يحملُ على الجوهرِ إلا بالاشتقاقِ، فَلَا يُقَالُ : الجسم هو بياضُ، بل ذو بياض، وأمَّا ثالثاً ; فَلِأَنَّ ذلك قد يكون جنساً كَاللُّونِ لِلسَّوادِ والبياض بِخلاف هذا العرض، فَإِنَّهُ قَسيم لِلذَّاتي، وفيه نظر؛ لأنَّهُ إِنْ أَرادَ جِنسيَّتَهُ لِذلكَ العرضِ بِالقياس إلى معروضاته؛ فهو باطل، وإلا ; فَهَذا العرضُ أيضاً قد يكون جنساً .ا.هـ. وقال المحقِّقُ الدَّوَّانِيُّ : الأبيضُ إذا أُخِذَ لَا بشرط شيء؛ فهو عرض ، وإذا أُخِذَ بِشرط شيءٍ ; فهوَ الثَّوبُ الأبيضُ، وإذا أُخِذَ بِشرط لا شيء ; فَهُوَ العرضُ المقابل للجوهَرِ ، كَمَا أَنَّ طبيعةَ الذَّاتي جِنسٌ ومادَّةٌ باعتبارين، وفَصْل وصورة باعتبارَيْنِ ، فَطبيعة العرضيّ عرض وعرضي باعتبارين، وهذا تحقيقُ الفرق بين العرض والعرضيّ ; لا ما يُتَخيَّلُ مِنْ أَنَّ الفَرقَ بينَهُمَا بِالذَّاتِ .. هـ . قال شارح سُلَّم العلوم: وَهَذا الكلام، وإنْ دلَّ على أنَّ العرض والعرضي مُتَّحدانِ بالذَّاتِ ; لكن لا يدلُّ على أنَّ العرض والمحلَّ مُتَّحدانِ بالذَّاتِ، والَّذي يُفهَمُ منه أنَّ الجنس والفصلَ كَمَا أنَّهما يَتَّحِدَانِ بالذَّاتِ فيحصل منهما النَّوعُ، ويَتَغايرانِ أُخْرَى; فَيصيرانِ مادَّةٌ وصورةٌ ; كذلك الثَّوبُ والأبيضُ ; قدْ يَتَّحِدَانِ فيحصل ثوبٌ أبيضُ، وقد يتغايرانِ فَيصيرُ الثَّوبُ مَحلا والأبيضُ بياضاً قائماً به وعرضاً ا.هـ. هو تحقيق نَفيسٌ فَاحْفَظْهُ ; فإنَّهُ ينفعُكَ في مواضِعَ كثيرة.
('The common accident') is not intended to mean what is opposite to substance, like walking and whiteness; rather, it is intended to mean the accidental (al-‘aradī) attributed to accident (al-‘arad), because it is what is predicated by way of congruence (haml muwāṭa’a), so one says: 'Man is walking' not 'walking', and 'white' not 'whiteness'. (His saying: al-‘arad al-‘āmm) And perhaps it is called merely 'accident'; it is stated explicitly in al-Ishārāt, and 'accident' here means the accidental, not the opposite of substance, even though some logicians mistakenly thought so due to confusion between what exists for the subject and what exists in the subject; thus in the gloss. As for what exists in the subject, it is the accident opposite to substance, and the philosophers have explained it as an essence which, when it exists in the external world, is in a subject; and they defined the subject as the locus that constitutes that which inheres in it. And we have explained that in the glosses on the Categories. And in Sharḥ al-Maṭāli‘: This accident (al-‘arad) that is opposite to substance is not as some people thought; rather, it is one of the two divisions of the accidental that is in contrast to the essential substantial. Firstly, because it (the common accident) may be a substance, like 'animal' for 'rational'—unlike that (the accident opposite to substance). Secondly, because it may be predicated of a substance in a real predication, i.e., by congruence, like 'walking' (al-māshī) on 'man'—unlike that, for it (the accident opposite to substance) is not predicated of substance except by derivation, so one does not say 'The body is whiteness', but rather 'possessor of whiteness'. Thirdly, because that (the accident opposite to substance) may be a genus, like 'color' for 'black' and 'white'—unlike this common accident, for it is the co-division of the essential. There is a consideration: if he intends its being a genus relative to its subjects, it is false; otherwise, this common accident may also be a genus. End. And the investigator al-Dawānī said: 'White', when taken without any condition, is an accident; when taken with a condition, it is a white garment; when taken with the condition of not being anything else, it is the accident opposite to substance—just as the nature of the essential is genus and matter under two considerations, and differentia and form under two considerations; so the nature of the accidental is accident and accidental under two considerations. This is the verification of the difference between accident and accidental, not what is imagined that the difference between them is in essence. End. The commentator on Sullam al-‘Ulūm said: 'This speech, although it indicates that accident and accidental are united in essence, does not indicate that accident and locus are united in essence. What is understood from it is that the genus and differentia, just as they are united in essence resulting in the species, and they differ in another respect becoming matter and form, likewise the garment and the white: sometimes they unite resulting in a white garment, and sometimes they differ so the garment becomes a locus and the white becomes a whiteness subsisting in it and an accident.' End. It is a valuable verification, so memorize it, for it will benefit you in many places.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of the fifth universal, al-‘arad al-‘āmm (the common accident). The commentator clarifies that the term 'al-‘arad' here does not refer to the accident opposite to substance (al-‘arad al-muqābil lil-jawhar) like whiteness or walking as qualities, but rather to the accidental (al-‘aradī) that is predicated via congruence (haml muwāṭa’a). For example, we say 'Zaid is walking' (māshin) not 'Zaid is walking-ness' (mashy). This is a key terminological distinction: in logic, 'arad' in 'arad 'āmm' means 'accidental' (a predicable), not the category of accident. The commentator notes that some logicians confused this, especially because they conflated 'what exists for the subject' (i.e., a predicable like 'walking') with 'what exists in the subject' (i.e., a category accident like whiteness). To reinforce this, three arguments are given to show that common accident is not the same as the accident opposite to substance. First, the common accident can be a substance when predicated, e.g., 'animal' is a common accident for 'rational' (human), but animal is a substance—unlike the category accident which cannot be a substance. Second, the common accident is predicated by congruence (e.g., 'man is walking'), while the category accident is only predicated derivatively (e.g., 'body is not whiteness' but 'possessor of whiteness'). Third, the category accident can be a genus (e.g., 'color' for 'black' and 'white'), while the common accident is a co-division of the essential, not a genus in that sense; though a subtle consideration is noted that if taken in a different way, it might also be genus. The passage then presents a deeper metaphysical point by al-Dawānī: the same nature 'white' can be considered as an accident (when unconditioned), as a substance (when conditioned with being a garment), or as the accident opposite to substance (when conditioned as not being anything else). This is analogous to how the essential nature can be genus or differentia under different considerations. The commentator from Sullam al-‘Ulūm further explains that accident and accidental are identical in essence but differ in consideration (like genus and differentia). The final exhortation to memorize this valuable verification underscores its importance for understanding predication and ontology in logic.

عرضي
العرض المقابل للجوهر

Accident vs Accidental: Al-Dawānī's Verification
Al-Dawānī's analysis of 'white' under three conditions to clarify the difference between accident and accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the same nature (e.g., 'white') can be considered as either an accident (category), an accidental (predicable), or a substance based on different conditions (la bi-shart shay', bi-shart shay', bi-shart la shay').

Core Idea
Accident and accidental are identical in essence but differ in consideration – like genus and differentia.

Al-Dawānī provides a profound analysis of the term 'white' (abyad) to illustrate the difference between accident (al-‘arad as category) and accidental (al-‘aradī as predicable). He shows that the same nature can be taken under three conditions, leading to three different logical statuses. This analysis is analogous to how the essential nature can be genus or differentia under different considerations. The commentator on Sullam al-‘Ulūm further explains that accident and accidental are united in essence but differ in relation, just as genus and differentia unite to form species.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three conditions on 'white'
Al-Dawānī says: 'white' taken without condition (la bi-shart shay') is an accident; taken with condition (bi-shart shay') is a white garment; taken with condition of not being anything else (bi-shart la shay') is the category accident.
This shows the same entity can be logical or ontological depending on how it is considered.
2
Map to the essential analogy
Just as the nature of the essential can be genus (if considered as matter) or differentia (if considered as form), the nature of the accidental can be accident (category) or accidental (predicable) under different considerations.
This is a key metaphysical point: it's a difference in consideration, not in essence.
3
Unite and differentiate
The Sullam commentator explains that accident and accidental are united in essence but differ in relation to the subject. The 'white' and 'garment' unite to form 'white garment', but when considered separately, 'garment' is locus and 'white' is accident.
This mirrors genus and differentia uniting to form species.
4
Apply to the common accident
This verification shows that the difference between common accident (predicable) and category accident is not a difference in essence but in the way we consider the nature.
This deepens the earlier distinction from the first lesson.

فَلِأَنَّ ذلك قد يكون جنساً كَاللُّونِ لِلسَّوادِ والبياض بِخلاف هذا العرض، فَإِنَّهُ قَسيم لِلذَّاتي، وفيه نظر؛ لأنَّهُ إِنْ أَرادَ جِنسيَّتَهُ لِذلكَ العرضِ بِالقياس إلى معروضاته؛ فهو باطل، وإلا ; فَهَذا العرضُ أيضاً قد يكون جنساً .ا.هـ. وقال المحقِّقُ الدَّوَّانِيُّ : الأبيضُ إذا أُخِذَ لَا بشرط شيء؛ فهو عرض ، وإذا أُخِذَ بِشرط شيءٍ ; فهوَ الثَّوبُ الأبيضُ، وإذا أُخِذَ بِشرط لا شيء ; فَهُوَ العرضُ المقابل للجوهَرِ ، كَمَا أَنَّ طبيعةَ الذَّاتي جِنسٌ ومادَّةٌ باعتبارين، وفَصْل وصورة باعتبارَيْنِ ، فَطبيعة العرضيّ عرض وعرضي باعتبارين، وهذا تحقيقُ الفرق بين العرض والعرضيّ ; لا ما يُتَخيَّلُ مِنْ أَنَّ الفَرقَ بينَهُمَا بِالذَّاتِ .. هـ . قال شارح سُلَّم العلوم: وَهَذا الكلام، وإنْ دلَّ على أنَّ العرض والعرضي مُتَّحدانِ بالذَّاتِ ; لكن لا يدلُّ على أنَّ العرض والمحلَّ مُتَّحدانِ بالذَّاتِ، والَّذي يُفهَمُ منه أنَّ الجنس والفصلَ كَمَا أنَّهما يَتَّحِدَانِ بالذَّاتِ فيحصل منهما النَّوعُ، ويَتَغايرانِ أُخْرَى; فَيصيرانِ مادَّةٌ وصورةٌ ; كذلك الثَّوبُ والأبيضُ ; قدْ يَتَّحِدَانِ فيحصل ثوبٌ أبيضُ، وقد يتغايرانِ فَيصيرُ الثَّوبُ مَحلا والأبيضُ بياضاً قائماً به وعرضاً ا.هـ. هو تحقيق نَفيسٌ فَاحْفَظْهُ ; فإنَّهُ ينفعُكَ في مواضِعَ كثيرة.
('The common accident') is not intended to mean what is opposite to substance, like walking and whiteness; rather, it is intended to mean the accidental (al-‘aradī) attributed to accident (al-‘arad), because it is what is predicated by way of congruence (haml muwāṭa’a), so one says: 'Man is walking' not 'walking', and 'white' not 'whiteness'. (His saying: al-‘arad al-‘āmm) And perhaps it is called merely 'accident'; it is stated explicitly in al-Ishārāt, and 'accident' here means the accidental, not the opposite of substance, even though some logicians mistakenly thought so due to confusion between what exists for the subject and what exists in the subject; thus in the gloss. As for what exists in the subject, it is the accident opposite to substance, and the philosophers have explained it as an essence which, when it exists in the external world, is in a subject; and they defined the subject as the locus that constitutes that which inheres in it. And we have explained that in the glosses on the Categories. And in Sharḥ al-Maṭāli‘: This accident (al-‘arad) that is opposite to substance is not as some people thought; rather, it is one of the two divisions of the accidental that is in contrast to the essential substantial. Firstly, because it (the common accident) may be a substance, like 'animal' for 'rational'—unlike that (the accident opposite to substance). Secondly, because it may be predicated of a substance in a real predication, i.e., by congruence, like 'walking' (al-māshī) on 'man'—unlike that, for it (the accident opposite to substance) is not predicated of substance except by derivation, so one does not say 'The body is whiteness', but rather 'possessor of whiteness'. Thirdly, because that (the accident opposite to substance) may be a genus, like 'color' for 'black' and 'white'—unlike this common accident, for it is the co-division of the essential. There is a consideration: if he intends its being a genus relative to its subjects, it is false; otherwise, this common accident may also be a genus. End. And the investigator al-Dawānī said: 'White', when taken without any condition, is an accident; when taken with a condition, it is a white garment; when taken with the condition of not being anything else, it is the accident opposite to substance—just as the nature of the essential is genus and matter under two considerations, and differentia and form under two considerations; so the nature of the accidental is accident and accidental under two considerations. This is the verification of the difference between accident and accidental, not what is imagined that the difference between them is in essence. End. The commentator on Sullam al-‘Ulūm said: 'This speech, although it indicates that accident and accidental are united in essence, does not indicate that accident and locus are united in essence. What is understood from it is that the genus and differentia, just as they are united in essence resulting in the species, and they differ in another respect becoming matter and form, likewise the garment and the white: sometimes they unite resulting in a white garment, and sometimes they differ so the garment becomes a locus and the white becomes a whiteness subsisting in it and an accident.' End. It is a valuable verification, so memorize it, for it will benefit you in many places.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of the fifth universal, al-‘arad al-‘āmm (the common accident). The commentator clarifies that the term 'al-‘arad' here does not refer to the accident opposite to substance (al-‘arad al-muqābil lil-jawhar) like whiteness or walking as qualities, but rather to the accidental (al-‘aradī) that is predicated via congruence (haml muwāṭa’a). For example, we say 'Zaid is walking' (māshin) not 'Zaid is walking-ness' (mashy). This is a key terminological distinction: in logic, 'arad' in 'arad 'āmm' means 'accidental' (a predicable), not the category of accident. The commentator notes that some logicians confused this, especially because they conflated 'what exists for the subject' (i.e., a predicable like 'walking') with 'what exists in the subject' (i.e., a category accident like whiteness). To reinforce this, three arguments are given to show that common accident is not the same as the accident opposite to substance. First, the common accident can be a substance when predicated, e.g., 'animal' is a common accident for 'rational' (human), but animal is a substance—unlike the category accident which cannot be a substance. Second, the common accident is predicated by congruence (e.g., 'man is walking'), while the category accident is only predicated derivatively (e.g., 'body is not whiteness' but 'possessor of whiteness'). Third, the category accident can be a genus (e.g., 'color' for 'black' and 'white'), while the common accident is a co-division of the essential, not a genus in that sense; though a subtle consideration is noted that if taken in a different way, it might also be genus. The passage then presents a deeper metaphysical point by al-Dawānī: the same nature 'white' can be considered as an accident (when unconditioned), as a substance (when conditioned with being a garment), or as the accident opposite to substance (when conditioned as not being anything else). This is analogous to how the essential nature can be genus or differentia under different considerations. The commentator from Sullam al-‘Ulūm further explains that accident and accidental are identical in essence but differ in consideration (like genus and differentia). The final exhortation to memorize this valuable verification underscores its importance for understanding predication and ontology in logic.

Key Terms
العرض
العرضي
بشرط شيء
بشرط لا شيء
لا بشرط

B) When taken with the condition of being a garment
C) When taken with the condition of not being anything else

Key Takeaway
The difference between accident (category) and accidental (predicable) is a difference in consideration, not in essence; the same nature can be both depending on conditions.

Distinguishing 'Arad and 'Aradi: Three Criteria
Author distinguishes 'arad from 'aradi through three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish 'arad from 'aradi using the three criteria of substantiality, mode of predication, and genushood.

Core Idea
'Arad and 'aradi differ not in essence but in consideration—a precision that saves many definitions.

The terms 'arad (accident) and 'aradi (accidental) are often used interchangeably, but classical logicians like the author of this Sharh draw a sharp distinction. This passage presents three arguments to show that 'aradi is broader than 'arad: it can be a substance, predicated by equivalence, or a genus. Then, through the commentaries of al-Dawwani and al-Sullam, we learn that the difference is ultimately one of consideration, not essence. Mastering this distinction will sharpen your ability to classify terms correctly in Islamic logic and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two terms in the Arabic
Find 'arad (عرض) and 'aradi (عرضي) in the passage. Notice how the author begins by correcting a misconception that 'arad is the accident opposite to substance.
2
Extract the first argument: substantiality
The author says 'aradi may be a substance (e.g., animal for rational), while 'arad cannot be a substance.
3
Extract the second argument: mode of predication
'Aradi can be predicated by equivalence (e.g., 'Zaid is walking'), while 'arad is only predicated by derivation (e.g., 'the body is white', not 'the body is whiteness').
4
Extract the third argument: genushood
'Aradi can be a genus (e.g., color for black and white), while 'arad cannot be a genus in the same way? Note the author's consideration: if genushood in relation to bearers, it's false; otherwise 'arad can be genus too.
5
Synthesize with al-Dawwani's resolution
Understand that 'white' taken without condition is 'arad; with condition is a white garment; with condition of no condition is 'arad opposite to substance. The difference is by consideration, not essence.

فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات وأما ما يوجد في الموضوع فإنَّهُ العرضُ المقابل للجوهر، وقد فشره الحكماء بماهية ؛ إذا وجدت في الخارج ؛ كانت في موضوع ، وعَرَّفُوا الموضوع بأنَّهُ المحل المقومُ لِمَا حلَّ فيه، وقد بَيَّنَّا ذلك في حواشي المقولات هذا، وفي شرح المطالع ليس هذا العرضُ الَّذي بإزاء الجوهرِ كَمَا ظَنَّهُ قوم ، بل أحدُ قِسمَي العرضيّ الَّذِي بِإِزاءِ الذَّاتي الجوهري، أمَّا أَوَّلاً ؛ فَلِأَنَّهُ قد يكونُ جَوهراً كَالحيوانِ لِلنَّاطِقِ دون ذلك؛ أي: العرض العام الذي يقابل الجوهَرَ، وأمَّا ثانياً ؛ فَلِأَنَّهُ قد يكون محمولاً على الجوهر حَمْلاً حقيقيًّا ؛ أي: بالمواطأةِ كَالماشي على الإنسانِ دونَ ذلكَ، فَإِنَّه لا يحملُ على الجوهرِ إلا بالاشتقاقِ، فَلَا يُقَالُ : الجسم هو بياضُ، بل ذو بياض، وأمَّا ثالثاً ؛ فَلِأَنَّ ذلك قد يكون جنساً كَاللُّونِ لِلسَّوادِ والبياض بِخلاف هذا العرض، فَإِنَّهُ قَسيم لِلذَّاتي، وفيه نظر؛ لأنَّهُ إِنْ أَرادَ جِنسيَّتَهُ لِذلكَ العرضِ بِالقياس إلى معروضاته؛ فهو باطل، وإلا ؛ فَهَذا العرضُ أيضاً قد يكون جنساً .ا.هـ. وقال المحقِّقُ الدَّوَّانِيُّ : الأبيضُ إذا أُخِذَ لَا بشرط شيء؛ فهو عرض ، وإذا أُخِذَ بِشرط شيءٍ ؛ فهوَ الثَّوبُ الأبيضُ، وإذا أُخِذَ بِشرط لا شيء ؛ فَهُوَ العرضُ المقابل للجوهَرِ ، كَمَا أَنَّ طبيعةَ الذَّاتي جِنسٌ ومادَّةٌ باعتبارين، وفَصْل وصورة باعتبارَيْنِ ، فَطبيعة العرضيّ عرض وعرضي باعتبارين، وهذا تحقيقُ الفرق بين العرض والعرضيّ ؛ لا ما يُتَخيَّلُ مِنْ أَنَّ الفَرقَ بينَهُمَا بِالذَّاتِ .. هـ . قال شارح سُلَّم العلوم: وَهَذا الكلام، وإنْ دلَّ على أنَّ العرض والعرضي مُتَّحدانِ بالذَّاتِ ؛ لكن لا يدلُّ على أنَّ العرض والمحلَّ مُتَّحدانِ بالذَّاتِ، والَّذي يُفهَمُ منه أنَّ الجنس والفصلَ كَمَا أنَّهما يَتَّحِدَانِ بالذَّاتِ فيحصل منهما النَّوعُ، ويَتَغايرانِ أُخْرَى؛ فَيصيرانِ مادَّةٌ وصورةٌ ؛ كذلك الثَّوبُ والأبيضُ ؛ قدْ يَتَّحِدَانِ فيحصل ثوبٌ أبيضُ، وقد يتغايرانِ فَيصيرُ الثَّوبُ مَحلا والأبيضُ بياضاً قائماً به وعرضاً ا.هـ. هو تحقيق نَفيسٌ فَاحْفَظْهُ ؛ فإنَّهُ ينفعُكَ في مواضِعَ كثيرة .
Chapter: On the Principles of Conceptualizations. As for what exists in a subject, it is the accident opposite to substance. The philosophers interpreted it as an essence; when it exists externally, it is in a subject. They defined the subject as the locus that constitutes that which inheres in it. We have explained this in the commentaries on the Categories. In Sharh al-Matali', this accident is not the one opposite to substance as some people thought, but rather one of the two divisions of the accidental which is opposite to the essential substantial. Firstly, because it may be a substance, like animal for rational, unlike that (i.e., the general accident opposite to substance). Secondly, because it may be predicated of a substance truly, i.e., by equivalence, like 'walking' is predicated of human, unlike that, for it is not predicated of substance except by derivation, so one does not say 'The body is whiteness' but 'possessor of whiteness'. Thirdly, because that may be a genus, like color for black and white, unlike this accident, for it is a co-ordinate of the essential. There is consideration in this; because if he intends its genus-ness for that accident in relation to its bearers, it is false; otherwise, this accident also may be a genus. End quote. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani said: 'White' when taken without condition is an accident; when taken with a condition, it is the white garment; when taken with the condition of nothing, it is the accident opposite to substance. Just as the nature of the essential is genus and matter under two considerations, and differentia and form under two considerations, so the nature of the accidental is accident and accidental under two considerations. This is the verification of the difference between accident and accidental, not the imagined difference that they differ in essence. End quote. The commentator on Sullam al-'Ulum said: This speech, even if it indicates that accident and accidental are identical in essence, does not indicate that accident and locus are identical in essence. What is understood from it is that genus and differentia, just as they are identical in essence and from them the species results, and they differ at another time, becoming matter and form, likewise the garment and white: they may be identical resulting in a white garment, and they may differ, so the garment becomes a locus and the white becomes whiteness subsisting in it and an accident. End quote. This is a precious verification, so memorize it; for it will benefit you in many places.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage clarifies the distinction between 'arad (accident) and 'aradi (accidental), drawing on multiple authorities. The author begins by stating that what exists in a subject (al-mawdu') is the accident opposite to substance, but then corrects a common misunderstanding: this accident is not the one opposite to substance, but rather one of the two divisions of 'aradi (the accidental) that is opposite to the essential substantial (al-dhati al-jawhari). Three arguments are given to support this. First, 'aradi may be a substance, e.g., 'animal' is accidental to 'rational' because 'rational' is a differentia, and 'animal' is a genus, yet 'animal' is a substance. Second, 'aradi may be predicated of a substance by equivalence (haml bi'l-muwata'a), e.g., 'walking' is predicated of a human ('Zaid is walking'), whereas 'arad (accident) is only predicated by derivation (e.g., 'body is white' not 'body is whiteness'). Third, 'aradi may be a genus, like 'color' for black and white, while 'arad cannot be a genus because it is a co-ordinate of the essential. However, the author notes a consideration (fihi nazar): if one means that 'arad cannot be a genus in relation to its bearers, that is false; otherwise, 'arad too can be a genus. Then al-Dawwani is quoted to explain that the difference is by consideration: 'white' taken without condition is an accident; with condition (of being in a garment) is the white garment; with the condition of no condition is the accident opposite to substance. Similarly, the nature of the essential is genus and matter under one consideration, differentia and form under another; so the nature of the accidental is accident and accidental under two considerations. The commentator on Sullam al-'Ulum adds that accident and accidental are identical in essence but differ in consideration, analogous to genus and differentia which are identical in essence (producing species) but may also differ (as matter and form). The passage concludes by urging the student to memorize this verification, as it is useful in many contexts.

عرضي
ذاتي جوهري

The distinction between 'arad and 'aradi is not essential but conceptual; 'aradi is the broader category that includes 'arad as a subset under a specific consideration.

Definition of the Common Accidental and Its Distinction
Commentators explain the definition of ʻAmm and clarify which universals it excludes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the two phrases in the definition of al-ʻarad al-ʻāmm exclude the other four universals and explain the pronoun reference issue.

Core Idea
The common accidental is external and said of multiple realities; each condition excludes a set of universals.

This passage unpacks the definition of the common accidental (al-ʻarad al-ʻāmm), one of the five universals. The commentators al-Dasuqi and al-ʻAttār show how the two conditions—'external' and 'said of it and of others'—work together to exclude the other universals: species, genus, differentia, and the specific property. They also discuss a subtle issue about pronoun reference that affects the interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key phrases in the definition
The definition of al-ʻarad al-ʻāmm is: 'the external said of it and of others.' Break it down into: (1) 'al-khārij' (the external) and (2) 'al-maqūl ʻalayhā wa-ʻalā ghayrihā' (said of it and of others). Each phrase serves to exclude certain universals.
2
Determine what each phrase excludes
According to the commentary, 'al-khārij' excludes species, genus, and differentia because they are not external to the essence—they are either parts of the essence or the essence itself. The phrase 'said of it and of others' excludes the specific property (al-khāṣṣa) because the specific property is only predicated of one reality.
Remember that the specific property is predicated of only one type of essence, e.g., 'laughter' for humans.
3
Understand the pronoun reference debate
The pronoun in 'on it' (ʿalayhā) can refer either to 'reality' in the definition of the specific property or to 'what' (i.e., individuals). This affects whether the common accidental is said of a reality and others, or of individuals under a reality and others. The commentators explore both possibilities.
Al-Dasuqi and al-ʻAttār both note that the pronoun reference is ambiguous, but the meaning remains clear: the common accidental applies across different essences.
4
Assess the exclusion of species and differentia
The commentators debate whether the exclusion of species and differentia is better assigned to the first condition ('external') or the last condition ('said of others'). They conclude that assigning to the first is more fitting because it excludes all types of species, genera, and differentiae universally.
The remote differentia and relative species are excluded only by 'external', not by 'said of others'.
5
Reflect on the absolute vs. relative specific property
Al-ʻAttār mentions that some consider the specific property to include absolute and relative types. The definition of the common accidental must be understood accordingly. If the specific property is taken in the absolute sense, then the common accidental and specific property can coincide in some cases (like 'walking' for humans and others), but the divisions remain distinct.
This nuance shows the depth of the logical analysis in the tradition.

Source Text
