وَهُوَ الخارج المقُوْلُ عَلَيْهَا وَعَلَى غَيْرِها، فقوله: «الخارج» يُخرجُ غير الخاصة . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: وَهُوَ الْخَارج . . . إلخ) فيهِ مَا مرَّ، وقوله : المقول عليها؛ أي: على حقيقة بدون قوله : واحدة، ويُحتمل رجوعُ الصَّميرِ إلى ما مِن قوله : ما تحتَ حقيقةٍ واحدة، وأُنتَ باعتبار معناها ؛ إذ هي واقعة على أفراد كما سبق، وكذا يُقال في قوله : وعلى غيرها . والحاصلُ : أنَّ الضَّمير فى عليها يحتمل رجوعه لحقيقة الواقعة في تعريف الخاصَّة، ويحتمل رجوعه لما الواقع في تعريفها، فالمعنى على الأوَّل: المقول على حقيقة وغيرها كما في الإنسان، والفرس ماش، والمعنى على الثَّاني : المقولُ على الأفرادِ الَّتي تحتَ حقيقة والأفراد التي غيرها كـ: «زيد وعمرو»، وهذا الفرسُ والحمارُ ماش. (قَوْلُهُ: الْمَقُوْلُ عَلَيْهَا هذا لا يُنافي قولهم : إِنَّ العرض العام لا يُقالُ في الجوابِ أصلاً؛ لأنَّ المنفي قوله في الجواب، وأما قوله : على أفراده؛ أي: حمله عليها حمل مواطأة؛ سواء كانَتْ مجموعةً أو مُفردة، فثابت كـ: «زيد ماشي». العطار (قَوْلُهُ : المَقُوْلُ عَلَيْهَا وَعَلَى غَيْرِهَا الضَّميرانِ راجعانِ إلى ما في قوله في تعريف الخاصَّةِ مَا تحتَ حقيقة، وقال العصامُ : ضمير (عليها ) راجع إلى حقيقةٍ لَا إلى حقيقة واحدةٍ، إذ لا يحسنُ عطفُ ) وعلى غيرها) على حقيقةٍ واحدةٍ، كَمَا لَا يُحسَنُ رجلٌ واحدٌ وَغيرُهُ؛ تَأَمَّلْ، واعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ ذهبَ بعضُهُم إلى أنَّ الخاصَّةَ الَّتِي هِيَ إِحدَى الكُلِّيَّاتِ الخمس أعمُّ مِنَ المطلقة والإضافية، وحُمِلَ قَولُهُ: فقط؛ على الحصر الإضافي دونَ الحقيقي؛ أي: إنَّ حصر الماشي في الإنسانِ بِالنِّسبة إلى النباتات، وأما بالنسبة إلى الفرس والبغل والحمار وغيرِهَا ؛ فَلَا حصر بناءً على اعتبار قيد الحيثية في التعريفاتِ، وعلى هذا ؛ لا تكونُ التّسمية حقيقيَّةً بل اعتباريَّةً بناءً على اعتبار الأقسام بقيود الحيثيَّةِ لاجتماع الخاصةِ والعرض العام في الماشي ، أَمَّا على تخصيص التَّعريفِ بِالخاصَّةِ المطلقة على ما بَيَّنَّا سابقاً ؛ فَلَا تَتَصادَقُ الأقسام . فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات وقوله : وعلى غيرها يُخرجُ الخاصة؛ لأنَّها مقولة على أفراد حقيقة واحدة فقط . ويحتمل أن يسند إخراجُ النَّوْعِ والفصل إلى القيدِ الأخير، لكنَّ إسناد إخراجهما إلى الأَوَّلِ أَوْفَقُ؛ لخروج الأنواع والأجناس والفصول به مطلقاً. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : يَخْرُجُ غَيْرُ الْخَاصَّةِ أي : وهو الجنس، والفصل، والنَّوع؛ لأنَّ الأوَّلَيْن: ليسًا خارجين عن الماهية؛ إذ هما جزآن منها، والثالث: تمامها، فلا يوصفُ بكونه خارجاً عن الماهية ؛ لأنَّ الشَّيء لا يخرج عن نَفْسِهِ، وَلَا بكونه داخلاً فيها ؛ لأنَّ الشَّيءَ لا يدخلُ في نَفْسِهِ . (قَوْلُهُ: إِلَى الْقَيْدِ الْأَخِيرِ هو قوله : وعلى غيرها، ولكن لا يخرجُ بِهِ إِلَّا النَّوْعُ الحقيقي، والفصل القريب. (قَوْلُهُ: لَكِنَّ إِسْنَادَ إِخْرَاجِهِمَا إِلَى الْأَوَّلِ أي: قوله : (الخارج)، وقوله: (مطلقاً) راجع للثلاثة، فمعناه في الأنواع : سواءٌ كانَتْ حقيقية أم إضافية، وفي الأجناس : سواءٌ كانَتْ عاليةً أم سافلة، وفي الفصول سواء كانت قريبة أو بعيدة. ولعلَّ هذا هو وجه كون هذا الاحتمال أوفق من الاحتمال الثاني، فإنَّ الفصل البعيد لا يخرج بالقيد الأخير، وكذا النَّوعُ الإضافي، ويحتمل رجوع قوله: (مُطلقاً) للفصول فقط، وهو الأظهر؛ لأنَّ الأنواع الإضافيَّةَ أجناس . العطار (قَوْلُهُ : إِخْرَاجُ النَّوْع) أي: الحقيقي، وقوله: (والفصل)؛ أي: فصله، وقوله : (مُطلقاً)؛ يحتمل رجوعَهُ لِلثَّلاثةِ ؛ أي: لِلأنواع؛ حقيقيَّةٌ كانَتْ أو إضافيَّةٌ، وَلِلأجناس ؛ قريبةً كانَتْ أو بعيدةً، وفصول النَّوعِ وفصول الجنس، ويحتمل رجوعه للفصول فقط وَهُوَ أظهر .
And it is the external [property] said of it and of others. So his saying: 'the external' excludes other than the specific [property]. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'and it is the external... etc.) In it is what passed, and his saying: 'said of it' i.e., on a reality without his saying: 'one', and it is possible that the pronoun refers to 'what' from his saying: 'what is under one reality', and you consider its meaning; since it applies to individuals as previously. Similarly, it is said in his saying: 'and on others'. The conclusion: the pronoun in 'on it' can refer to the reality occurring in the definition of the specific [property], and can refer to 'what' occurring in its definition. The meaning according to the first: said of a reality and other than it, like 'human' and 'horse is walking'. The meaning according to the second: said of the individuals under a reality and individuals other than it, like 'Zayd and Amr', and 'this horse and donkey are walking'. (His saying: 'said of it' this does not contradict their statement: 'the common accidental is not said in the answer at all'; because what is negated is its being said in the answer. As for his saying: 'on its individuals', i.e., predicating it of them by mutual agreement, whether collectively or individually, it is established, like 'Zayd is walking'. Al-Attar: (His saying: 'said of it and of others' the two pronouns refer to 'what' in his saying in the definition of the specific property: 'what is under a reality'. Al-Isam said: the pronoun in 'on it' refers to 'a reality' not to 'one reality', since it is not proper to conjoin 'and on others' to 'one reality', just as it is not proper to say 'one man and other than him'; ponder. And know that some held that the specific property which is one of the five universals is more general than the absolute and the relative. And his saying: 'only' is interpreted as restrictive in a relative sense, not a real one; i.e., the restriction of 'walking' to 'human' is in relation to plants, but in relation to horse, mule, donkey, etc., there is no restriction based on consideration of the condition of aspect in definitions. On this basis, the naming is not real but customary, based on considering divisions by conditions of aspect due to the meeting of the specific property and common accidental in 'walking'. As for specifying the definition with the absolute specific property as we previously explained, the divisions do not coincide. Section: On the principles of conceptions. And his saying: 'and on others' excludes the specific property; because it is predicated of individuals of one reality only. And it is possible to assign the exclusion of species and differentia to the last condition, but assigning their exclusion to the first is more fitting; because the species, genera, and differentia are excluded by it absolutely. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'excludes other than the specific property') i.e., it is genus, differentia, and species; because the first two are not external to the essence; they are parts of it, and the third is its completion, so it is not described as being external to the essence because a thing does not come out of itself, nor as being internal to it because a thing does not enter into itself. (His saying: 'to the last condition') it is his saying: 'and on others', but it excludes only the real species and the proximate differentia. (His saying: 'but assigning their exclusion to the first') i.e., his saying: 'the external', and his saying: 'absolutely' refers to the three; so its meaning regarding species: whether real or relative, regarding genera: whether high or low, and regarding differentiae: whether proximate or remote. Perhaps this is the aspect of this probability being more fitting than the second probability, for the remote differentia is not excluded by the last condition, nor is the relative species. And it is possible that his saying: 'absolutely' refers only to differentiae, and that is more apparent because relative species are genera. Al-Attar: (His saying: 'exclusion of species') i.e., the real one, and his saying: 'and differentia' i.e., its differentia, and his saying: 'absolutely' can refer to the three; i.e., species: whether real or relative, genera: whether near or remote, and differentiae of species and differentiae of genus, and it can refer only to differentiae, and that is more apparent.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of the common accidental (al-ʻarad al-ʻāmm), one of the five universals. The definition given is: 'It is the external [property] said of it and of others.' The commentators, al-Dasuqi and al-ʻAttār, expand on this definition, explaining how each phrase functions to exclude other universals. The term 'external' (al-khārij) serves to exclude the specific property (al-khāṣṣa) because the latter is not external; rather, it is a part of the essence or the essence itself. The phrase 'said of it and of others' ensures that the accidental is predicated not only of one reality but of multiple different realities, thereby excluding the specific property which is predicated of only one reality. The discussion involves possible references of pronouns in the definition, with al-Dasuqi noting that the pronoun in 'on it' could refer to 'reality' in the definition of the specific property or to 'what' (i.e., the individuals). The aim is to clarify that the common accidental applies across different essences, unlike the specific property. The section heading 'On the principles of conceptions' introduces a new but related topic, and the commentary continues to argue that the exclusion of species and differentia is better assigned to the first condition ('external') than to the last, because it covers all types of species, genera, and differentiae universally.

Key Takeaway
The common accidental is defined by two conditions: it is external to the essence and predicated of multiple realities; these conditions together distinguish it from all other universals.

Distinguishing Khassa from 'Arad al-'Amm
Al-Dasuqi clarifies the usage of 'arad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish al-khassa from al-'arad al-'amm based on specificity and explain why the unqualified term 'arad refers to general accident.

Core Idea
Khassa is species-specific; 'arad al-'amm spans multiple species.

In this passage, al-Dasuqi clarifies a key terminological point: when logicians say 'arad' without qualification, they mean general accident, not special property. This may seem confusing since khassa is also an accident, but the distinction lies in specificity. Understanding this prevents misclassification in definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two accidental predicates
Locate the terms khassa and 'arad al-'amm in the Arabic. Note that both are accidents but differ in scope.
Khassa is specific to a single species; 'arad al-'amm applies across species.
2
Observe the default meaning of 'arad
Al-Dasuqi states that when 'arad is used absolutely, it refers to 'arad al-'amm. Why? Because khassa is restricted, so the unqualified form defaults to the broader one.
3
Explain the exclusion of khassa
Khassa is not included because it is limited to individuals of a type, unlike 'arad al-'amm which is common. This is a convention of the discipline.
Even writing (khassa for man) is an accident, but not the default 'arad.
4
Apply the distinction to an example
Take 'writing' as a property of man. Is it khassa or 'arad al-'amm? It is khassa because only humans write. Compare to 'blackness' which applies to many species.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الخاصَّةِ والعَرَضِ العام (وَكُلّ مِنْهُمَا ) ؛ أي من الخاصَّةِ والعرضِ العام، ينقسم إلى : العَرَضِ اللازم، والعرض المفارق، وكلُّ واحدٍ من اللازم والعرض المفارقِ ينقسم إلى أقسام فنقول في التقسيم : الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ : مِنَ الْخَاصَّةِ وَالْعَرْضِ الْعَامّ اعلَمُ أنَّ العرضَ مَتَى أُطلِق؛ انصرف للعرض العام، وَلَا ينصرفُ للخاصَّة وإن كانت عرضاً أيضاً؛ إلا أنَّها خاصَّةٌ بأفرادِ نوع، بخلافِ العرض العام؛ فإنَّهُ لا يختص بأفرادِ نوع. (1) (قَوْلُهُ : يَنْقَسِمُ ... إلخ) فاللازم ) : إما لازم للماهية، أو لازم للوجود، وكلُّ منهما : إمَّا بَيِّنُ أو غيرُ بَيِّن، والأوَّلُ : إِمَّا بَيِّن بالمعنى الأعم، وإِمَّا بَيِّن بالمعنى الأخص ، والمفارقُ : إمّا دائم أو سريعُ الزَّوالِ، أو بطيؤُهُ . العطار (قَوْلُهُ : يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى العَرَضِ الْلَّازِمِ لَا يَحْفَى أَنَّهُ لا يَصحُ أنْ يكونَ المقسَّمُ مَجموع الخاصَّةِ والعرضِ العام ؛ فإِنَّ هَذَا المجموع مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ مَجموعٌ لَا مَعنِّى لَهُ، وأيضاً وحدةُ المقسَّم واجبةٌ وَلَا كُلُّ واحدٍ مِنَ الأمرَيْنِ ؛ لِمُنافاة قولِهِ : (ينقسم إلى العرضِ اللازم ... إلخ)؛ فإنَّهُ لو كانَ الأمرُ كَذَلِكَ ؛ لَقَالَ : والخاصة كذلك، فيكون في الكلام تَقسيمانِ لِمُقسَّمَيْنِ هُمَا : الخاصَّةُ والعرضُ العام، وَحينَئِذٍ فكانَ اللائقُ أَنْ يقول : وَكُلٌّ مِنهُمَا ينقسم إلى لازم ومُفارق ... إلخ ، ويكون اللازم صادِقاً بالخاصَّةِ والعرضِ العام، لا يُقالُ : ذِكْرُ العرضِ العام مُغْنِ عَنِ الخاصَّةِ ؛ لأنَّ الخاصَّةَ عَرْضُ عام أيضاً، لأنَّا نقول : هذا إنَّما يظهرُ في خاصَّةِ الجنسِ ، فإنَّها عرضُ عامٌ لِنَوعِهِ المندرج تحتَهُ؛ لَا في خاصَّةِ النَّوعِ السَّافِلِ كَالكتابةِ لِلإنسانِ ؛ فَإِنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ عرضاً عاما لشيء. من (۱) (قَوْلُهُ : فاللازم ... إلخ) حاصله أن الأقسام تسعة : ستَّة للازم، وثلاثة للمفارق وهي لكلّ الخاصة والعرض العام فهي إذن ثمانية عشر، وسيبين أن الشيء الواحد يكون خاصة لشيء كالزوجية للعدد المنقسم، وعرضاً عاماً لأفراده كالأربعة، وكالفقر فإنه خاصة غير لازمة للحيوان وعرض عام لكلِّ نوع من أنواعه كالإنسان. ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
Divisions of al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm. (And each of them) i.e., of al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm, divides into: necessary accident and separable accident. And each of necessary and separable accident divides into divisions. So we say in the division: al-Dasuqi: (His saying: i.e., from al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm) Know that when 'arad is used absolutely, it refers to al-'arad al-'amm, and does not refer to al-khassa even though it is also an accident; except that it is specific to individuals of a species, unlike al-'arad al-'amm which is not specific to individuals of a species. (1) (His saying: it divides ... etc.) al-lazim: either necessary for essence, or necessary for existence. And each of them: either self-evident or not self-evident. The first: either self-evident in the broader sense, or self-evident in the narrower sense. And al-mufariq: either permanent or quick in vanishing, or slow in vanishing. al-'Attar: (His saying: it divides into necessary accident ... etc.) It is not hidden that it is not correct for the dividend to be the combination of al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm; for this combination in itself has no meaning. Also, the unity of the dividend is obligatory, and it is not each of the two things; because it contradicts his saying: (it divides into necessary accident ... etc.); for if the matter were so, he would have said: and al-khassa likewise. Then there would be in the speech two divisions for two dividends, namely: al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm. And then it would be appropriate for him to say: and each of them divides into necessary and separable ... etc., and al-lazim would be true of both al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm. It cannot be said: the mention of al-'arad al-'amm suffices over al-khassa; because al-khassa is also a general accident. For we say: this only appears in the khassa of the genus, for it is a general accident for its species that falls under it; not in the khassa of the lowest species like writing for man; for it is not a general accident for anything. From (1) (His saying: al-lazim ... etc.) The summary is that the divisions are nine: six for al-lazim, and three for al-mufariq. And they are for each of al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm. Thus they are eighteen. And it will be shown that one thing can be a khassa for something like evenness for the divisible number, and a general accident for its individuals like four, and like poverty: it is a non-necessary khassa for animal and a general accident for every species of its types like man. End. al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the division of two types of accidental predicates: al-khassa (special property) and al-'arad al-'amm (general accident). The author begins by stating that each of these divides into two main categories: al-'arad al-lazim (necessary accident) and al-'arad al-mufariq (separable accident). This is a standard classification in logic, as accidents are either inseparable from the subject or separable. The commentator al-Dasuqi then clarifies a terminological point: when the term 'arad (accident) is used without qualification, it refers specifically to general accident, not to special property, even though special property is also an accident. The reason is that special property is restricted to individuals of a single species, while general accident applies across multiple species. Next, al-Dasuqi expands on the subdivisions: al-lazim (necessary accident) is further divided into what is necessary for the essence (lazim li al-mahiyya) and what is necessary for existence (lazim li al-wujud). Each of these two can be either self-evident (bayyin) or non-self-evident (ghayr bayyin). The self-evident itself is of two types: self-evident in the broader sense and self-evident in the narrower sense. The separable accident (al-mufariq) is divided into three types: permanent (da'im), quickly vanishing (sari' al-zawal), and slowly vanishing (bat't' al-zawal). The commentator al-'Attar then raises an objection: he points out that it is not correct to take the combined category of 'khassa and 'arad al-'amm' as the dividend for this division, because that combination has no meaning and the dividend must be a single unified concept. Instead, the proper procedure would be to say 'each of them divides into necessary and separable,' implying two parallel divisions. Al-'Attar also addresses a potential counterargument: one might say that mentioning al-'arad al-'amm suffices for both since al-khassa is also a general accident. He refutes this by noting that while the special property of a genus is a general accident for its species, the special property of the lowest species (like writing for man) is not a general accident for anything. Finally, al-Sharnubi summarizes the total number of divisions: six for the necessary and three for the separable, and since these apply to both khassa and 'arad al-'amm, the total is eighteen. He gives examples: evenness is a khassa for the divisible number but a general accident for its individuals like four; poverty is a non-necessary khassa for animal but a general accident for human.

Subdivisions of Necessary Accident (al-Lazim)
Al-Dasuqi expands on lazim subdivisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify necessary accidents (lazim) into essential vs. existential, and into self-evident (bayyin) vs. non-self-evident, with subcategories of bayyin.

Core Idea
Necessary accidents split by essence/existence and by evidentness.

After establishing that each accidental predicate divides into necessary and separable, the text now delves into the subdivisions of the necessary. These are crucial for evaluating whether an accident truly inheres and how easily its necessity is recognized.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main branches of lazim
Lazim is divided into 'necessary for the essence' (lazim li al-mahiyya) and 'necessary for existence' (lazim li al-wujud). The first is inseparable from the definition; the second from actual existence.
E.g., the possibility of writing is not essential but existentially necessary for a writer.
2
Further divide each branch by evidentness
Each of these can be self-evident (bayyin) or non-self-evident (ghayr bayyin). Bayyin means the necessity is clear without proof.
3
Subdivide self-evident into broader and narrower
Bayyin is further split: broader meaning (al-ma'na al-a'amm) and narrower meaning (al-ma'na al-akhass). The broader includes what is evident to some minds, the narrower is absolutely evident.
This distinction refines how we judge necessity.
4
Apply to an example
Take 'heat' for fire. It is lazim li al-mahiyya (essentially necessary) and bayyin (self-evident) because fire is defined by heat. But 'capacity to burn' might be lazim li al-wujud and less evident.

أقسام الخاصَّةِ والعَرَضِ العام (وَكُلّ مِنْهُمَا ) ؛ أي من الخاصَّةِ والعرضِ العام، ينقسم إلى : العَرَضِ اللازم، والعرض المفارق، وكلُّ واحدٍ من اللازم والعرض المفارقِ ينقسم إلى أقسام فنقول في التقسيم : الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ : مِنَ الْخَاصَّةِ وَالْعَرْضِ الْعَامّ اعلَمُ أنَّ العرضَ مَتَى أُطلِق؛ انصرف للعرض العام، وَلَا ينصرفُ للخاصَّة وإن كانت عرضاً أيضاً؛ إلا أنَّها خاصَّةٌ بأفرادِ نوع، بخلافِ العرض العام؛ فإنَّهُ لا يختص بأفرادِ نوع. (1) (قَوْلُهُ : يَنْقَسِمُ ... إلخ) فاللازم ) : إما لازم للماهية، أو لازم للوجود، وكلُّ منهما : إمَّا بَيِّنُ أو غيرُ بَيِّن، والأوَّلُ : إِمَّا بَيِّن بالمعنى الأعم، وإِمَّا بَيِّن بالمعنى الأخص ، والمفارقُ : إمّا دائم أو سريعُ الزَّوالِ، أو بطيؤُهُ . العطار (قَوْلُهُ : يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى العَرَضِ الْلَّازِمِ لَا يَحْفَى أَنَّهُ لا يَصحُ أنْ يكونَ المقسَّمُ مَجموع الخاصَّةِ والعرضِ العام ؛ فإِنَّ هَذَا المجموع مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ مَجموعٌ لَا مَعنِّى لَهُ، وأيضاً وحدةُ المقسَّم واجبةٌ وَلَا كُلُّ واحدٍ مِنَ الأمرَيْنِ ؛ لِمُنافاة قولِهِ : (ينقسم إلى العرضِ اللازم ... إلخ)؛ فإنَّهُ لو كانَ الأمرُ كَذَلِكَ ؛ لَقَالَ : والخاصة كذلك، فيكون في الكلام تَقسيمانِ لِمُقسَّمَيْنِ هُمَا : الخاصَّةُ والعرضُ العام، وَحينَئِذٍ فكانَ اللائقُ أَنْ يقول : وَكُلٌّ مِنهُمَا ينقسم إلى لازم ومُفارق ... إلخ ، ويكون اللازم صادِقاً بالخاصَّةِ والعرضِ العام، لا يُقالُ : ذِكْرُ العرضِ العام مُغْنِ عَنِ الخاصَّةِ ؛ لأنَّ الخاصَّةَ عَرْضُ عام أيضاً، لأنَّا نقول : هذا إنَّما يظهرُ في خاصَّةِ الجنسِ ، فإنَّها عرضُ عامٌ لِنَوعِهِ المندرج تحتَهُ؛ لَا في خاصَّةِ النَّوعِ السَّافِلِ كَالكتابةِ لِلإنسانِ ؛ فَإِنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ عرضاً عاما لشيء. من (۱) (قَوْلُهُ : فاللازم ... إلخ) حاصله أن الأقسام تسعة : ستَّة للازم، وثلاثة للمفارق وهي لكلّ الخاصة والعرض العام فهي إذن ثمانية عشر، وسيبين أن الشيء الواحد يكون خاصة لشيء كالزوجية للعدد المنقسم، وعرضاً عاماً لأفراده كالأربعة، وكالفقر فإنه خاصة غير لازمة للحيوان وعرض عام لكلِّ نوع من أنواعه كالإنسان. ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
Divisions of al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm. (And each of them) i.e., of al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm, divides into: necessary accident and separable accident. And each of necessary and separable accident divides into divisions. So we say in the division: al-Dasuqi: (His saying: i.e., from al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm) Know that when 'arad is used absolutely, it refers to al-'arad al-'amm, and does not refer to al-khassa even though it is also an accident; except that it is specific to individuals of a species, unlike al-'arad al-'amm which is not specific to individuals of a species. (1) (His saying: it divides ... etc.) al-lazim: either necessary for essence, or necessary for existence. And each of them: either self-evident or not self-evident. The first: either self-evident in the broader sense, or self-evident in the narrower sense. And al-mufariq: either permanent or quick in vanishing, or slow in vanishing. al-'Attar: (His saying: it divides into necessary accident ... etc.) It is not hidden that it is not correct for the dividend to be the combination of al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm; for this combination in itself has no meaning. Also, the unity of the dividend is obligatory, and it is not each of the two things; because it contradicts his saying: (it divides into necessary accident ... etc.); for if the matter were so, he would have said: and al-khassa likewise. Then there would be in the speech two divisions for two dividends, namely: al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm. And then it would be appropriate for him to say: and each of them divides into necessary and separable ... etc., and al-lazim would be true of both al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm. It cannot be said: the mention of al-'arad al-'amm suffices over al-khassa; because al-khassa is also a general accident. For we say: this only appears in the khassa of the genus, for it is a general accident for its species that falls under it; not in the khassa of the lowest species like writing for man; for it is not a general accident for anything. From (1) (His saying: al-lazim ... etc.) The summary is that the divisions are nine: six for al-lazim, and three for al-mufariq. And they are for each of al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm. Thus they are eighteen. And it will be shown that one thing can be a khassa for something like evenness for the divisible number, and a general accident for its individuals like four, and like poverty: it is a non-necessary khassa for animal and a general accident for every species of its types like man. End. al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the division of two types of accidental predicates: al-khassa (special property) and al-'arad al-'amm (general accident). The author begins by stating that each of these divides into two main categories: al-'arad al-lazim (necessary accident) and al-'arad al-mufariq (separable accident). This is a standard classification in logic, as accidents are either inseparable from the subject or separable. The commentator al-Dasuqi then clarifies a terminological point: when the term 'arad (accident) is used without qualification, it refers specifically to general accident, not to special property, even though special property is also an accident. The reason is that special property is restricted to individuals of a single species, while general accident applies across multiple species. Next, al-Dasuqi expands on the subdivisions: al-lazim (necessary accident) is further divided into what is necessary for the essence (lazim li al-mahiyya) and what is necessary for existence (lazim li al-wujud). Each of these two can be either self-evident (bayyin) or non-self-evident (ghayr bayyin). The self-evident itself is of two types: self-evident in the broader sense and self-evident in the narrower sense. The separable accident (al-mufariq) is divided into three types: permanent (da'im), quickly vanishing (sari' al-zawal), and slowly vanishing (bat't' al-zawal). The commentator al-'Attar then raises an objection: he points out that it is not correct to take the combined category of 'khassa and 'arad al-'amm' as the dividend for this division, because that combination has no meaning and the dividend must be a single unified concept. Instead, the proper procedure would be to say 'each of them divides into necessary and separable,' implying two parallel divisions. Al-'Attar also addresses a potential counterargument: one might say that mentioning al-'arad al-'amm suffices for both since al-khassa is also a general accident. He refutes this by noting that while the special property of a genus is a general accident for its species, the special property of the lowest species (like writing for man) is not a general accident for anything. Finally, al-Sharnubi summarizes the total number of divisions: six for the necessary and three for the separable, and since these apply to both khassa and 'arad al-'amm, the total is eighteen. He gives examples: evenness is a khassa for the divisible number but a general accident for its individuals like four; poverty is a non-necessary khassa for animal but a general accident for human.

Key Terms
لازم
لازم للماهية
لازم للوجود
بين
غير بين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the division, a necessary accident that is essential and self-evident in the broader sense is:
A) Lazim li al-wujud, bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass
B) Lazim li al-mahiyya, bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm
C) Lazim li al-mahiyya, ghayr bayyin
D) Lazim li al-wujud, bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm

Key Takeaway
Necessary accidents have a two-fold division: by essence/existence and by self-evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the property 'being made of water' for a liquid. Is it lazim li al-mahiyya or li al-wujud? Is it bayyin? Provide reasoning from the text's categories.

Subdivisions of Separable Accident (al-Mufariq) and Total Count
Al-Sharnubi summarizes the divisions and gives examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify separable accidents (mufariq) into permanent, quickly vanishing, and slowly vanishing, and calculate the total types for both khassa and 'arad al-'amm.

Core Idea
Separable accidents are divided into three temporal categories; total divisions are eighteen.

Having covered necessary accidents, the passage now turns to separable accidents. It also provides a summary of all divisions, showing that the classification yields eighteen types when applied to both special property and general accident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of mufariq
Al-mufariq is divided into: da'im (permanent), sari' al-zawal (quickly vanishing), and bat't' al-zawal (slowly vanishing). These are based on duration of separation.
Permanent separability means it can separate but rarely does; quick means it leaves soon; slow means it lingers.
2
Understand the total count of divisions
Al-Sharnubi summarizes: there are 6 types for lazim (2 essence/existence × 2 bayyin/non-bayyin × 2 broader/narrower? Actually the text says six: likely from subdivisions) and 3 for mufariq, total 9. These apply to both khassa and 'arad al-'amm, so 9×2=18.
The six for lazim come from: lazim li al-mahiyya (bayyin a'amm, bayyin akhass, ghayr bayyin) and lazim li al-wujud (same three), making six.
3
Apply to an example
Al-Sharnubi gives evenness as khassa for divisible numbers and 'arad al-'amm for its individuals like four. Evenness is lazim li al-mahiyya? Actually it's a khassa that is necessary? It can be classified.
4
Reflect on the commentator's objection
Al-'Attar noted that the dividend should be 'each of khassa and 'arad al-'amm' not their combination. This ensures clarity.

Divisions of al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm. (And each of them) i.e., of al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm, divides into: necessary accident and separable accident. And each of necessary and separable accident divides into divisions. So we say in the division: al-Dasuqi: (His saying: i.e., from al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm) Know that when 'arad is used absolutely, it refers to al-'arad al-'amm, and does not refer to al-khassa even though it is also an accident; except that it is specific to individuals of a species, unlike al-'arad al-'amm which is not specific to individuals of a species. (1) (His saying: it divides ... etc.) al-lazim: either necessary for essence, or necessary for existence. And each of them: either self-evident or not self-evident. The first: either self-evident in the broader sense, or self-evident in the narrower sense. And al-mufariq: either permanent or quick in vanishing, or slow in vanishing. al-'Attar: (His saying: it divides into necessary accident ... etc.) It is not hidden that it is not correct for the dividend to be the combination of al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm; for this combination in itself has no meaning. Also, the unity of the dividend is obligatory, and it is not each of the two things; because it contradicts his saying: (it divides into necessary accident ... etc.); for if the matter were so, he would have said: and al-khassa likewise. Then there would be in the speech two divisions for two dividends, namely: al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm. And then it would be appropriate for him to say: and each of them divides into necessary and separable ... etc., and al-lazim would be true of both al-khassa and al-'arad al-'amm. It cannot be said: the mention of al-'arad al-'amm suffices over al-khassa; because al-khassa is also a general accident. Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the division of two types of accidental predicates: al-khassa (special property) and al-'arad al-'amm (general accident). The author begins by stating that each of these divides into two main categories: al-'arad al-lazim (necessary accident) and al-'arad al-mufariq (separable accident). This is a standard classification in logic, as accidents are either inseparable from the subject or separable. The commentator al-Dasuqi then clarifies a terminological point: when the term 'arad (accident) is used without qualification, it refers specifically to general accident, not to special property, even though special property is also an accident. The reason is that special property is restricted to individuals of a single species, while general accident applies across multiple species. Next, al-Dasuqi expands on the subdivisions: al-lazim (necessary accident) is further divided into what is necessary for the essence (lazim li al-mahiyya) and what is necessary for existence (lazim li al-wujud). Each of these two can be either self-evident (bayyin) or non-self-evident (ghayr bayyin). The self-evident itself is of two types: self-evident in the broader sense and self-evident in the narrower sense. The separable accident (al-mufariq) is divided into three types: permanent (da'im), quickly vanishing (sari' al-zawal), and slowly vanishing (bat't' al-zawal). The commentator al-'Attar then raises an objection: he points out that it is not correct to take the combined category of 'khassa and 'arad al-'amm' as the dividend for this division, because that combination has no meaning and the dividend must be a single unified concept. Instead, the proper procedure would be to say 'each of them divides into necessary and separable,' implying two parallel divisions. Al-'Attar also addresses a potential counterargument: one might say that mentioning al-'arad al-'amm suffices for both since al-khassa is also a general accident. He refutes this by noting that while the special property of a genus is a general accident for its species, the special property of the lowest species (like writing for man) is not a general accident for anything. Finally, al-Sharnubi summarizes the total number of divisions: six for the necessary and three for the separable, and since these apply to both khassa and 'arad al-'amm, the total is eighteen. He gives examples: evenness is a khassa for the divisible number but a general accident for its individuals like four; poverty is a non-necessary khassa for animal but a general accident for human.

Defining and Dividing the Necessary Concomitant (al-Lāzim)
Definition and division of the necessary concomitant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given property as a necessary concomitant of essence or existence based on the definition and division provided.

Core Idea
A necessary concomitant is inseparable from a thing; its type depends on whether the inseparability is absolute or limited to one mode of existence.

In this passage, the author establishes the precise definition of the necessary concomitant and then divides it into those that accompany the essence in both mind and reality, and those that accompany it only in one realm. This distinction is fundamental for analyzing the relationships between universals and particulars in Islamic logic and for building sound definitions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition for being a necessary concomitant
The passage states that if the separation (infikāk) of a property from a thing is impossible, then it is a necessary concomitant. This impossibility can arise from the essence of the subject, the essence of the concomitant, or an external cause.
The impossibility is what defines it, not the actual presence in other things.
2
Recognize that the concomitant can be shared
The author explicitly says that even if the property is found in other things, it does not disqualify it from being a necessary concomitant, as long as it cannot be separated from this thing.
3
Note the shift from 'essence' to 'thing'
The author explains that the definition uses 'the thing' rather than 'the essence' to allow the inclusion of necessary concomitants of existence, which are not inseparable from the bare essence.
This is a subtle but crucial point for the division to be exhaustive.
4
Distinguish the two divisions
With respect to essence (al-māhiyya): the property is inseparable in both mental and external existence (e.g., evenness for four). With respect to existence (al-wujūd): the property is inseparable only in one mode (e.g., spatiality for a body only externally, universality for man only mentally).
5
Apply the division to examples
Identify from the given examples which are lāzim al-māhiyya and which are lāzim al-wujūd. Practice by classifying new examples from Islamic sciences.
Use the condition of impossibility of separation in both or one mode.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التَّصورات
(إِنِ امْتَنَعَ انْفِكَاكُهُ)؛ أي: انفكاكُ كلّ واحدٍ من الخاصَّةِ والعَرَضِ العام (عَنِ الشَّيْء؛ فَلَازِمٌ).
(قَوْلُهُ : إِنِ امْتَنَعَ انْفِكَاكُهُ... إلخ) أي: لا يجوز أن يفارقه، وإِنْ وُجِدَ في غيره؛ فَلَا يردُ اللازم الأعم، وذلك الامتناعُ إمَّا لِذاتِ الملزومِ أو لِذاتِ اللَّازِمِ أو لِأَمْرِ مُنفصل كَالسَّوادِ لِلحبشي؛ قاله عبد الحكيم.
(قَوْلُهُ : عَنِ الشَّيْءِ) عدل عن تعريف اللازمِ بِمَا يمتنعُ انفكاكه عن الماهية لِعَدَم ما يقتضيه في كلامِهِ، وَهُوَ تَقسيمُ الكُلّيّ بالنَّظَرِ لِلماهية، ووجودُ مَا يُنافيه؛ وهو خروج لازم الوجودِ، ولزومُ تَقسيم الشَّيء إلى نفْسِهِ ومُباينِهِ في قولِهِ؛ بالنَّظر إلى الماهية أو الوجودِ، لكنَّهُ لزمَهُ أَنَّ التَّقسيم غيرُ حاصرٍ؛ إذْ لا يَنحصرُ مَا يمتنعُ عَنِ الشَّيء في لازم الماهية ولازم الوجودِ؛ لأنَّ كليهما لازم بالقياس إلى الماهية، فيخرج لازمُ الشَّخصِ مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ لازمُ الشَّخصِ عَنِ التَّقسيمِ؛ قالَهُ المحشّي.
ثم إِمَّا (بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى المَاهِيَّةِ) و إِمَّا (بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى الوجُودِ) فَلَازِمُ الماهية: هو الذي لا ينفك عن الشَّيء في الذهنِ وَلَا في الخارج، ولازم الوجود: وهو الذي لا ينفك عن الشَّيء في الخارج فقط.
(قَوْلُهُ : إِمَّا بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى الْمَاهِيَّةِ) أي: يمتنع انفكاكُهُ عَنِ المَاهِيَّةِ مُطلقاً؛ أي: بحسب كلا وُجودَيْها، بمعنى أنَّها حيثُ وُجِدَتْ؛ كانَتْ مُتَّصفةٌ بِهِ، وهو لازم الماهية كالزَّوجيَّةِ لِلأربعة، فإنَّ الأربعة زوج؛ سواء كانت في الذهن أو في الخارج، أو لا يمتنع انفكاكه عنها إلا في وجودٍ خاصٌ كَالتَّحيز للجسم؛ فَإِنَّهُ إِنَّما يلزمه في الوجودِ الخارجيّ، وَكَالكُلِّيَّةِ لِلإنسان؛ فإنَّها إِنَّما تلزمه في الوجودِ العقلي، كذا في الدَّوَّاني.
Chapter: On the Principles of Concepts.
(If its separation is impossible) i.e. the separation of each of the property and the general accident from the thing (so it is a necessary concomitant).
(His statement: 'If its separation is impossible...' etc.) i.e. it is not permissible for it to separate from it, even if it is found in something else; so the more general necessary concomitant is not excluded. And that impossibility is either due to the essence of the subject, or the essence of the concomitant, or an external matter, like blackness for an Ethiopian; as stated by Abd al-Hakim.
(His statement: 'from the thing') He deviated from defining the necessary concomitant as that whose separation from the essence is impossible, due to the lack of what his words require—namely the division of the universal with respect to the essence—and the existence of what contradicts it: that would exclude the necessary concomitant of existence, and necessitate dividing a thing into itself and its opposite in his statement: 'with respect to the essence or existence.' But it follows that the division is not exhaustive, because what is impossible to separate from the thing is not limited to the necessary concomitant of essence and necessary concomitant of existence, since both are necessary relative to the essence. Thus the necessary concomitant of the individual, insofar as it is a necessary concomitant of the individual, is excluded from the division; as said by the commentator.
Then either (with respect to the essence) or (with respect to existence). So the necessary concomitant of essence is that which does not separate from the thing in the mind nor in the external world. And the necessary concomitant of existence is that which does not separate from the thing only in the external world.
(His statement: 'either with respect to the essence') i.e. its separation from the essence is absolutely impossible, i.e. with respect to both its modes of existence, meaning that wherever the essence exists, it is qualified by it. This is the necessary concomitant of essence, like evenness for four: four is even whether in the mind or in the external world. Or its separation from it is impossible only in a particular existence, like spatiality for a body: it only accompanies it in external existence, and like universality for man: it only accompanies it in mental existence; as stated in al-Dawwani.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage defines the logical concept of 'the necessary concomitant' (al-lāzim) and then divides it into two types based on the perspective of essence or existence. The author begins by stating the condition for something to be a necessary concomitant: it must be impossible to separate it from the thing. This impossibility is not affected by the concomitant being found in other things; it only matters that it cannot be absent from this particular thing. The cause of this impossibility may be intrinsic to the subject, intrinsic to the concomitant, or due to an external factor, as illustrated by blackness being inseparable from an Ethiopian due to a specific cause. The author then addresses a subtle point: why the definition uses 'the thing' rather than 'the essence'? This is to allow the division to include the necessary concomitant of existence, which is not impossible to separate from the bare essence but only from the essence as it exists externally. A potential objection is raised: if the division is made with respect to essence or existence, it may not be exhaustive because the necessary concomitant of an individual (e.g., blackness for a specific Ethiopian) falls outside the categories of necessary concomitant of essence and necessary concomitant of existence. The commentator defends the definition by showing that it does cover such cases. Finally, the passage clearly distinguishes the two types: (1) the necessary concomitant of essence (lāzim al-māhiyya), which cannot be separated from the thing in both mental and external existence, like evenness for four; and (2) the necessary concomitant of existence (lāzim al-wujūd), which cannot be separated only in one mode of existence, like spatiality for a body (external only) or universality for man (mental only). Examples from logic and physics illustrate the point.

لازم الماهية
لازم الوجود
انفكاك

D) Universality for the concept of man

Key Takeaway
A necessary concomitant is defined by the impossibility of separation; it can be of two types: necessary of essence (inseparable in both mind and external) and necessary of existence (inseparable only in one mode).

The Accidental Separable Quality: Definition and Division
Author defines 'arad mufariq and divides it into permanent and temporary with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an accidental separable quality ('arad mufariq) and classify it as permanent or temporary based on the definition and examples given in the passage.

Core Idea
An accidental separable quality is defined not by permanence, but by the possibility of separation from its subject.

In the previous lesson, we learned about inseparable qualities (lazim). Now we turn to qualities that can separate from their subjects without destroying them. The author defines 'arad mufariq and then divides it into two main types: permanent and temporary, each with examples that will sharpen your ability to apply this logical tool in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for 'arad mufariq
Find the phrase 'وَإِلَّا' (and otherwise) which refers back to the condition of inseparability. The passage states: if separation from the thing is not impossible, but possible, then it is an accidental separable quality. So the key condition is the possibility of separation.
2
Distinguish 'arad mufariq from 'arad lazim
Recall that 'arad lazim is inseparable (e.g., the blackness of a crow). Here, the opposite: if separation is possible, it is 'arad mufariq. Notice that the definition does not require actual separation, only possibility.
3
Analyze the two main divisions
The author divides 'arad mufariq into what persists (yadumu) for the subject and what vanishes (yazulu). For the vanishing type, further subdivision by speed: quickly and slowly. Note that 'persisting' here means it remains for a long time, not that it is inseparable.
4
Examine the examples
The examples: permanent poverty (persisting), redness of shyness and paleness of fear (quickly vanishing), youth and old age (slowly vanishing). These examples are from common human experience, illustrating how the same quality can be either permanent or temporary depending on the subject.
5
Test your understanding
Given a quality, ask: Is it possible for it to separate from its subject? If yes, it is 'arad mufariq. Then check: does it persist or vanish? If it vanishes, is it quick or slow? This classification helps in defining substances and accidents in kalam and fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات [العَرَضُ المفارِقُ]: (وَإِلَّا) عطفٌ على قوله : إن امتنع انفكاكه؛ أي: وإن لم انفكاكه عن الشَّيء بأن كانَ جائز الانفكاكِ عنه ؛ فَعَرَضٌ مُفَارِقٌ. والعرض المفارِقُ : إما أن (يَدُومَ) للمعروض، كالفَقرِ الدَّائِمِ، أَوْ يَزُوْلَ عنه بِسُرْعَةٍ كحُمُرةِ الخجل، وصُفْرَةِ الوَجَلِ، أَوْ بُطْءٍ كالشَّبابِ والشَّيبِ.
Chapter: On the Principles of Concepts [The Accidental Separable Quality]: (And otherwise) is connected to his saying: if its separation is impossible; i.e., if its separation from the thing is not impossible, but it is possible to be separated from it, then it is an accidental separable quality. And the accidental separable quality: either it (persists) for the subject, like permanent poverty, or it vanishes from it quickly, like the redness of shyness and the paleness of fear, or slowly, like youth and old age.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'arad mufariq (accidental separable quality) and divides it into two main types. The author begins by stating the condition for a quality to be separable: if it is not impossible for the quality to be separated from the subject (i.e., it is not essential or necessary), then the quality is called 'arad mufariq. The word 'otherwise' (wa illa) refers back to the previous condition of inseparable (lazim) separating. Then the author divides 'arad mufariq into what persists (yadumu) for the subject, such as permanent poverty, and what vanishes (yazulu) from it. The vanishing type is further subdivided by speed: quickly, like the redness that appears from shyness and the paleness from fear; and slowly, like youth and old age. This classification helps distinguish between qualities that are inherent to a thing by nature (like poverty that may be long-lasting) and those that are transient. The examples are drawn from common human experiences, illustrating how accidents can attach and detach from subjects.

العرض المفارق

Key Takeaway

The Five Universals

Parsing the Conclusion on the Universal
Commentator defines 'mabāḥith al-kullī' and enumerates the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five universals enumerated in this passage and parse the key Arabic terms 'khātima' and 'mabāḥith al-kullī' as used in logical commentary.

Core Idea
The five universals are the lens through which every definition is evaluated.

This passage from the commentary on the Tadhheeb opens the conclusion section of the chapter on universals. The commentator first clarifies the grammatical status of the word 'khātima' (conclusion) and then defines 'mabāḥith al-kullī' (discussions of the universal) by listing the five predicables that constitute the subject of these discussions. Understanding this enumeration is essential because the five universals are the building blocks of all definitions in Islamic logic. Let us parse the terminology and extract the list.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'khātima' and its grammatical status
Find the word 'khātima' in the passage. The commentator explains that it is an inflected noun (muʿraba) acting as the predicate of an omitted subject, not a proper noun or indeclinable. This parsing ensures precise reading.
The text says 'this is a conclusion' – 'hādhihi khātima'.
2
Define 'mabāḥith al-kullī' as a compound term
Locate the phrase 'mabāḥith al-kullī'. The commentator breaks it down: 'mabāḥith' is plural of 'mabḥath' meaning 'place of research'; 'al-kullī' means 'the universal'. So it means 'the places of research of the universal' – the topics or propositions where the universal is studied.
The commentator offers two interpretations: the propositions themselves or the investigations.
3
Extract the list of five universals
Look for the enumeration after 'min ḥaythu kawnihī jinsan...' The passage lists: genus (jins), differentia (fasl), species (nawʿ), property (khāṣṣa), and common accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm). These are the five predicables under discussion.
This list is standard in Islamic logic; commit it to memory.
4
Distinguish the two interpretations of 'mabāḥith'
The passage says 'wa hiya al-qaḍāyā...' (they are the propositions) and then 'aw al-abḥāth...' (or the investigations). The commentator allows both meanings: the propositions studied or the inquiries themselves. Understand that the term covers both the content and the activity.
This ambiguity is common in logic texts; the context clarifies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[خاتمة] أي : هذه خاتمة لمباحثِ الكُلّيّ. (قَوْلُهُ : هَذِهِ خَاتِمَةٌ) هذا بناءً على أنَّ التَّراحِمَ مُعربة، وأنَّها خبرُ مُبتدأ محذوف ; لا على أنَّها موقوفةٌ لَا معرفة وَلَا مبنيَّة ; لِعَدَم تركْبِهَا مَعَ العامل كَمَا قِيْلَ بذلك (قَوْلُهُ : لِمَبَاحِثِ الْكُلِّي جمعُ : مبحث ; بمعنى محلّ البحث، وهي القضايا الَّتي يُبحث فيها عن الكُلِّي مِن حيث كونه جنساً أو فصلاً أو نوعاً أو خاصةً أو عرضاً عاما، أو الأبحاث التي تتعلق بالكُلِّي مِن الحيثية المذكورة.
[Conclusion] That is, this is a conclusion for the discussions of the universal. (His saying: 'This is a conclusion') This is based on the assumption that 'al-tarāḥīm' is inflected, and that it is a predicate of an omitted subject; not that it is a proper noun or indeclinable, because it is not combined with a governing word as was said. (His saying: 'for the discussions of the universal' — plural of 'mabḥath' meaning 'place of research') And they are the propositions in which the universal is investigated in terms of being a genus, a differentia, a species, a property, or a common accident, or the investigations that pertain to the universal from the mentioned perspective.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of the conclusion section (khātima) of the chapter on universals. The commentator explains the grammatical status of the word 'khātima' (conclusion) and clarifies that 'mabāḥith al-kullī' (discussions of the universal) refers either to the propositions where the universal is examined as one of the five predicables (genus, species, differentia, property, common accident) or to the investigations themselves. The enumeration of the five universals is a standard list in Islamic logic, and this passage confirms that the conclusion summarizes the discussions of these five items. The grammatical note about 'khātima' being an inflected noun (muʿraba) rather than a proper noun ensures precise parsing of the text.

The Three Aspects of the Universal: Logical, Natural, and Composite
Author defines the logical universal and analyzes it into three types: logical, natural, and composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical universal, natural universal, and composite universal, and correctly classify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The same word 'universal' can mean three different things: an accident, a substrate, or their combination.

One of the most important distinctions in logic is understanding what 'universal' really means. The passage shows that when we say 'animal is universal', there are three different considerations: the concept itself (natural universal), the property of being shared (logical universal), and the combination of both. This distinction is essential for precise reasoning in every Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of the logical universal
Find the phrase 'ما لا يمنع نفس تصوره عن وقوع الشركة فيه' and note that it defines the logical universal as a concept whose very nature does not prevent multiple things from sharing it. For example, 'human' does not prevent Zayd, 'Amr, and others from being humans.
This definition focuses on the attribute of being universal.
2
Understand the natural universal as substrate
The passage explains that the natural universal is the quiddity itself (e.g., animal) considered as the thing that receives the logical universal. It is the concept taken in itself, not as a property. The text mentions two views: either it is the concept as substrate (al-‘Attar’s first view) or the concept in itself (the preferred view).
The natural universal is the 'what' that is universal, not the 'how' of being universal.
3
Recognize the composite universal
The composite universal is the combination of the natural and logical universals — the concept together with the attribute of universality. The passage uses the example of saying 'the universal is universal', where three things arise: the concept in itself (natural), the predicated universality (logical), and their composite.
The composite is the entire complex: the substrate plus the accident.
4
Practise distinguishing the three
Take an example like 'the genus is a universal'. Identify which aspect is being considered: when you say 'genus' in itself, it is the natural universal; when you say 'genus has universality', that universality is the logical universal; and saying 'the universal genus' as a single entity is the composite.
This distinction is critical for avoiding confusion in definitions and syllogisms.

Source Text

أحدها : المفهُومُ الكُلّي)، ويُسمَّى كُلِّيَّا مَنْطِقِيًّا)، وهو ما لا يمنعُ نفْسُ تصوره عن وقوع الشركة فيه . الدسوقي . (قَوْلُهُ : الْمَفْهُوْمُ الْكُلِّيُّ أي : مفهوم هذا اللفظ ؛ أي: ما يُفهَمُ مِن لفظ كُلِّي مِن غير مُلاحظة شيءٍ مخصوص، وقوله : (وهو ما لا يمنع ... إلخ)؛ تفسير لِمَا يُفهم من لفظ الكُلّيّ؛ أي: وهو شيءٌ لا يمنعُ نَفْس تصوره... إلخ، هذا هو الكُلّي المنطقي، وأفراد هذا الكُلّيّ : إنسان وحيوان، وناطق، وضاحك، وماشي. ، العطار أنَّ الكُلّيّ الطَّبيعيّ هُوَ مَفهوم الحيوانِ مَثَلاً مِنْ حيثُ هو معروضٌ لِلكُلّيّ المنطقيّ وَهُوَ أحدُ قَوْلَيْن ثانيهما : أنَّهُ الحيوانُ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ فعلى كُلِّ قول يرد عليهِ مُقَابِلُهُ، والعجب أنَّهُ ارتضَى فيما بعد أنَّ الكُلّي الطَّبيعي هُوَ الماهيَّةُ مِنْ حيثُ هِيَ هِيَ ؛ مُعَنْوناً عنه بالأوفق، وعليه فالوارد هو الماهيَّةُ مِن حيثُ العروض، وتحرير المقامِ أنَّ في قولِهِمْ : الحيوانُ كُلّيّ ؛ ثلاثَ اعتباراتٍ ؛ أي : بِالنِّسبةِ لِمَا يتعلَّقُ بِهِ الغرضُ ، وإلا ؛ فَبَقِيَ أمورٌ أُخَرُ هِيَ الحيوان المقيَّد، على أنَّ الكُلِّيَّ الطَّبيعي هي الماهيَّةُ مِن حيثُ هِيَ ، أو الحيوانُ المطلقُ على أنَّهُ الماهيَّةُ مِنْ حيثُ العروض والعارضُ المقيَّدُ والحُكْمُ والنِّسبةُ ، لكن لم يقعْ مِنَ الشَّارح هذا القولُ، فليس الباقي إلا أحد الأمرين المذكورين والعارض المقيَّدُ، مير زاهد : وفي عبارة المثن إشارة إلى أنَّ إطلاق الكُلِّيِّ على المفهوماتِ الثَّلاثَةِ بالاشتراكِ اللفظي كَمَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ شارحُ المطالع في رسالة تحقيقِ الكُلِّيَّاتِ . قالَ (قَوْلُهُ: المَفْهُوْمُ الْكُلّي) أي: مفهومُ الكُلّيّ الصَّادق على الحيوانِ صدق العارض على المعروض، وهذا المفهومُ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ هُوَ، أَو مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّه تعرضُ له الكُلَّيَّةُ ؛ أي: مِنْ حيث اشتراكُهُ بينَ الكُلّيّ العارض للإنسانِ والكُلّيّ العارض للفرس إلى غير ذلكَ ؛ كُلّي طبيعي ، والكُلّي العارضُ لَهُ كُلِّيَّ مَنطقيٌّ، فَفِي قولِنَا : الكُلّى كُلّى أيضاً ؛ أمور ثلاثة : مفهومُ الكُلّي مِن حيثُ هُوَ ، والكُلِّي المحمول عليه، والمجموع المركَّبُ مِنْهُمَا ، وكَذَا في قولِنَا : الكُلِّي جنس، والجنس القريب نوع، إلى غير ذلكَ، فَتَدَبَّرُ ؛ فإنَّهُ قد أشكل الفرقُ بين هذهِ المفهوماتِ الثَّلَاثَةِ على مَنْ يدَّعِي التَّفَرُّدَ بِحَلِّ المشكلاتِ ؛ قالَهُ عبد الحكيم .
One of them: the universal concept, and it is called a logical universal, and it is that whose very conception does not prevent the occurrence of sharing in it. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'the universal concept' means the concept of this word; i.e., what is understood from the word 'universal' without observing anything specific. And his statement: 'and it is that which does not prevent... etc.' is an interpretation of what is understood from the word 'universal'; i.e., it is a thing whose very conception does not prevent... etc. This is the logical universal. The individuals of this universal are: human, animal, rational, laughing, walking. Al-'Attar: The natural universal is the concept of animal, for example, inasmuch as it is the substrate of the logical universal. This is one of two statements; the second is that it is animal in itself. According to each statement, its opposite is objected to. And it is remarkable that he later approved that the natural universal is the quiddity in itself, entitling it as 'the more appropriate.' According to this, what occurs is the quiddity in terms of being a substrate. The clarification of the matter is that in their statement 'animal is universal' there are three considerations: i.e., in relation to what the purpose is attached to, otherwise there remain other things which are the restricted animal. Such that the natural universal is the quiddity in itself, or the absolute animal on the basis that it is the quiddity in terms of being a substrate, and the restricted accident, and the judgment, and the relation. But this statement did not occur from the commentator, so nothing remains except one of the two mentioned matters and the restricted accident. Mir Zayed: And in the expression of the text there is an indication that the application of 'universal' to the three concepts is by verbal equivocation, as the commentator on al-Matali' stated in the treatise on the investigation of universals. He said: (His statement: the universal concept) means: the concept of the universal that is true of animal with the truth of an accident on its substrate. This concept in itself, or in terms that universality attaches to it, i.e., in terms of its commonality between the universal that is an accident for human and the universal that is an accident for horse, etc., is a natural universal. And the universal that is an accident for it is a logical universal. So in our statement 'the universal is also a universal' there are three things: the concept of the universal in itself, the universal predicated upon it, and the composite of the two. Similarly in our statement 'the universal is a genus, and the proximate genus is a species', etc. So reflect; for the difference between these three concepts has become difficult for one who claims uniqueness in solving problems. Said by 'Abd al-Hakim.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory commentary on the first principle of tasawwurat: the universal concept. It begins by defining the logical universal (al-kulli al-mantiqi) as that whose conception does not prevent multiple things from sharing in it – for example, 'human' does not prevent sharing by Zayd, 'Amr, etc. The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that this definition explains what is understood from the word 'universal' without additional restrictions. Then al-'Attar introduces the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi'i) with two views: it is either the concept as substrate of the logical universal (e.g., animal as that which receives the attribute 'universal') or the concept in itself. The author notes that the preferred view is that the natural universal is the quiddity itself. The passage then discusses the three considerations when saying 'animal is universal': (1) the concept in itself, (2) the concept as substrate of universality, (3) the composite of both. Mir Zayed adds that calling all three 'universal' is a case of homonymy. A second commentary by 'Abd al-Hakim restates this: the natural universal is the concept as it is, the logical universal is the attribute of universality attaching to it, and the composite is the combination. The author warns that many who claim to unravel complexities still confuse these three concepts. The entire passage aims to clarify the precise distinctions necessary for defining universals.

Key Terms
المفهوم الكلي
الكلي المنطقي
الكلي الطبيعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an individual (fard) of the logical universal?
A) The concept 'animal' in itself
B) The concept 'human' (insan)
C) The quiddity of animal as substrate
D) The composite of logical and natural universals

Key Takeaway
The universal is not a single concept but three: the natural universal (the essence itself), the logical universal (the property of being universal), and the composite (both together).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify which of the three universals (logical, natural, or composite) applies to: (a) the concept 'intoxicant' in itself, (b) the property of being an intoxicant that can apply to multiple substances, (c) the combination of the concept and its universality.
?
In the definition of 'prayer' as 'specific words and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim', determine which aspect of the universal is being defined: the natural (essence of prayer), the logical (its applicability to all valid prayers), or the composite?

Distinguishing Natural Universal from Natural Genus
Definition of ma'rud and distinction of natural universal from natural genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the natural universal from the natural genus by identifying their respective substrates (ma'rud) as defined by the logicians.

Core Idea
They differ not in essence but in relation: one is substrate for universality, the other for genericity.

This lesson tackles a subtle but critical distinction in Islamic logic: the difference between the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi'i) and the natural genus (al-jins al-tabi'i). Both involve the same quiddity (e.g., 'animal'), but they differ based on what that quiddity serves as a substrate for. The passage, dense with commentary, clarifies this by examining the concept of the subject of the universal (ma'rud al-kulli). You will learn to identify each term correctly and avoid a common confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'ma'rud al-mafhum al-kulli'
Find the definition of 'ma'rud' in the text: it means 'the thing of which the concept of the universal is true, considered as receiving universality'. Understand that it is not the quiddity in itself, but the quiddity in a certain relation.
Examples given: 'human, animal, rational, laughing, walking'.
2
Extract the core distinction from al-Sayyid's statement
Al-Sayyid says: if animal in itself were natural universal, then no difference would exist with natural genus. But the correct view is: animal as substrate for universality = natural universal; animal as substrate for genericity = natural genus. Note the two different relations.
The relation determines the logical property, not the essence itself.
3
Analyze the objection and response
An objection claims that if animal is a common individual for both, then they would be identical. The response clarifies that al-Sayyid meant if animal in itself were both, then concepts would coincide; but since they are distinguished by relation, there is a difference.
This reinforces that the distinction is relational, not essential.
4
Connect to the external existence of the natural universal
The passage explains that the natural universal exists externally only as the nature (quiddity), not with the attribute of universality. This matches al-Tusi's view that natures taken in themselves are called natural universals.
Universality is a mental accident; the nature itself is extramental.

وحيوان وناطق وضاحك وماش»، فالحيوان كُلّي طبيعي من حيثُ كونه معروضاً لِلكُلِّيّ المنطقيّ؛ لَا مِن حيثُ ذاتُهُ . العطار (قَوْلُهُ : وَمَعْرُوْضُهُ) أي : مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ مَعروض ، فإِنَّهُ هُوَ الكُلِّيُّ مَعروضاً لَهُ ؛ جنس طبيعي .ا.هـ. وكتب عبد الحكيم على قوله : فَالصَّوابُ . . . إلخ : هذا ما ذكرَهُ الشَّارحُ في شرح المطالع، وقالَ : إِنَّهُ مَنصوص في «الشَّفاء»، وقال المحقِّقُ التَّفتازاني : وهذا مُصرَّحٌ بِهِ في كلامِ المتقدمينَ والمتأخّرينَ ، إلا أنَّ بعضَهُم صرَّحُوا بالقيد، وبعضَهُم تركُوهُ ، وقال : معنى قولهم: الحيوانُ من حيثُ هو كُلّي طبيعي ؛ أَنَّهُ معَ قطع النَّظَرِ عَنْ عوارض سوى الكُلِّيَّةِ، وكذا الحال في الجنسِ الطّبيعي وغيرهما ، ومعنى قولهم : الكُلَّيُّ الطبيعي موجود الخارج ؛ أنَّ الطَّبيعة التي يعرضُ لها الاشتراك في العقلِ مَوجودةٌ في الخارج؛ لا أَنَّها مَعَ اتّصافِهَا بِالكُلِّيَّةِ موجودة فيه، لكنَّ كلام المحقِّقِ الطُّوسي في شرح الإشاراتِ صريح فيما هو المشهورُ حيثُ قالَ: المعاني التي لا تمنعُ مَفهوماتُها عَنَ وقوع الشّركةِ؛ قد تؤخذُ مِن حيثُ هِيَ هِيَ؛ لَا مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّها واحدة أو كثيرةٌ أو كُلِّيَّةٌ أو جزئيَّةٌ أو معدومةٌ، إلى قولِهِ : فإنَّها مِن حيثُ هِيَ كَذلِكَ تُسمَّى طبائع ؛ أي : طبائع أعيان الموجوداتِ وحقائقها وهي التي تُسمَّى بِالكُلِّيِّ الطَّبيعي .ا.هـ. وأورد على قولِ السَّيِّدِ : فَلَا فرقَ إذنْ أنَّ كونَ الحيوانِ فَرداً لَهما؛ لا يوجبُ اتِّحَادَهُمَا، بَلْ بينَهُمَا فرقٌ بِالعموم والخصوص، وأجابَ عبد الحكيم : بأَنَّ مَعنى كلام السَّيِّدِ أَنَّهُ إذا كان الحيوانُ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ كُلِّيًّا طبيعيًّا وجنساً طبيعيًّا أيضاً ؛ كانَ مَفهومُهُمَا الطَّبيعة
Chapter: On the Principles of Tasawwurat. And the second of them: its subject (ma'ruduhu), i.e., that to which universality attaches. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'its subject' means the subject of the concept of the universal; i.e., that of which the concept of the universal is true, such as 'human, animal, rational, laughing, walking.' So animal is a natural universal inasmuch as it is a substrate for the logical universal, not in terms of its essence. Al-'Attar: (His statement: 'and its subject') means: inasmuch as it is a substrate, for it is the natural universal according to what is the verification, not the essence of the substrate in itself. Al-Sayyid said: If the concept of animal in itself were a natural universal, then there would be no difference between the concept of the natural universal and the concept of the natural genus. So the correct view is that the concept of animal inasmuch as it is a substrate for the concept of the universal or is fit to be a substrate for it is a natural universal; and inasmuch as it is a substrate for the concept of genus or is fit to be a substrate for it is a natural genus. End quote. And 'Abd al-Hakim wrote on his statement 'so the correct view... etc.': This is what the commentator mentioned in Sharh al-Matali', and he said: It is explicitly stated in al-Shifa'. And al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani said: This is explicitly stated in the words of the early and later scholars, except that some of them stated the condition explicitly and some omitted it. He said: The meaning of their statement 'animal inasmuch as it is a natural universal' is that it is with cutting off consideration from accidents other than universality. And similarly for natural genus and others. And the meaning of their statement 'the natural universal exists externally' is that the nature that the commonality in the intellect attaches to exists externally; not that it exists externally with its description of universality. But the statement of al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi in Sharh al-Isharat is explicit in what is well-known, where he said: The meanings whose concepts do not prevent the occurrence of sharing; they may be taken in themselves, not in terms of being one or many or universal or particular or nonexistent, up to his statement: For they in themselves are called natures; i.e., the natures of the entities of existents and their realities, which are called the natural universal. End quote. And an objection was raised against al-Sayyid's statement: So there is no difference then – that the fact that animal is an individual for both does not necessitate their unity; rather there is a difference between them by generality and particularity. And 'Abd al-Hakim answered: That the meaning of al-Sayyid's statement is that if animal in itself were a natural universal and also a natural genus, then their concept would be the nature.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage, part of a chapter on the principles of concepts, defines the second principle: the subject of the universal (ma'rud al-kulli), i.e., that which receives the attribute of universality. Al-Dasuqi explains it as that of which the concept of the universal is true, listing examples like 'human' and 'animal'. He clarifies that 'animal' is a natural universal only insofar as it is a substrate for the logical universal, not in its pure essence. Al-'Attar reinforces this, stating that the natural universal is precisely the substrate, not the essence per se. Al-Sayyid then highlights a crucial distinction: if the concept of animal in itself were the natural universal, there would be no difference between natural universal and natural genus. To avoid confusion, he distinguishes them: animal considered as a substrate for universality is the natural universal, while considered as a substrate for genericity is the natural genus. This view is supported by references to al-Shifa' and al-Taftazani. The passage also discusses the external existence of the natural universal: it is the nature that exists externally, not the nature with the property of universality. However, al-Tusi's view in Sharh al-Isharat identifies the natural universal with natures taken in themselves. Finally, an objection is raised and answered: animal being a common individual to both natural universal and natural genus does not make them identical; they differ in generality and particularity. 'Abd al-Hakim clarifies that al-Sayyid meant that if animal in itself were both, then the concepts would coincide – but since the correct view distinguishes them by the relation (to universality vs. to genericity), the objection is resolved.

معروض المفهوم الكلي
الجنس الطبيعي

Checkpoint
According to al-Sayyid's view in the passage, what distinguishes the natural universal from the natural genus?
A) The natural universal is the quiddity in itself, while the natural genus is the quiddity as substrate.
B) The natural universal is the quiddity as substrate for universality, the natural genus as substrate for genericity.
C) The natural universal exists externally, while the natural genus exists only in the mind.
D) There is no distinction; they are the same concept.

Key Takeaway
The natural universal and natural genus are distinguished not by essence but by the relation of a quiddity serving as a substrate for universality or for genericity.

The Natural Universal as Subject for Logical Universality
Author defines and refines the natural universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the natural universal as the subject of logical universality and differentiate it from the essence of individuals.

Core Idea
The natural universal is the subject, not the essence, of logical universality.

In this passage, the author clarifies a common misunderstanding about the natural universal. While earlier logicians like al-Shamsiyya might have considered it as the essence of individuals, the author refines this: the natural universal is the individuals themselves considered as subjects that receive universality. This distinction is crucial for understanding how universals function in logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of natural universal
Find where the author says 'al-kulli al-tabi'i ma sadaqat al-mantiqi...' and note the correction about 'not in terms of their essence but in terms of being a subject'.
2
Understand the correction of al-Shamsiyya
The author explicitly states 'la min haythu dhatiha kama huwa zahir kalam al-Shamsiyya'—the natural universal is not the essence itself but the individuals as subjects.
This is a critical refinement of the earlier position.
3
Analyze the naming
Why is it called 'natural' and 'universal'? The author explains: 'universal' because it is a subject for the concept of universality; 'natural' because it is attributed to nature as an individual to its concept.
This etymology grounds the terminology.
4
Apply the definition
Use the definition to identify a natural universal: e.g., 'human' considered as the collection of individuals that are predicated by 'human'.
Contrast with the logical universal which is the property of being universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرق بين المفهوم والمعروض ظاهر. والحاصل: أن الكلي الطبيعي ما صدقات المنطقي؛ أي: الأفراد التي يصدق عليها الكلي المنطقي كالحيوان وما معه، لكن لا من حيث ذاتها كما هو ظاهر كلام الشمسية، بل من حيث كونها معروضة للكلية المنطقية؛ أي: متصفة بمنعها للاشتراك. (قوله: كلِّيًّا طبيعيًّا) سُمِّي الكلي الطبيعي كلِّيًّا لأنه معروض لمفهوم الكلي من حيث هو معروض له، وطبيعيًّا لأنه منسوب إلى الطبيعة نسبة الفرد إلى المفهوم. 
فصل: في مبادئ التصورات
والتغاير بين هذين المفهوم والمعروض ظاهر. فإن المفهوم: هو ما لا يمنع نفس تصوره عن وقوع الشركة فيه. والمعروض: هو ما تعرض له الكلية، كالحيوان والإنسان مثلاً. ومن المعلوم أن مفهوم الكلي ليس هو بعينه مفهوم الحيوان، ولا جزءاً له.
The difference between the concept and the subject is clear. The summary is: the natural universal is that to which the logical universal applies—i.e., the individuals of which the logical universal is predicated, like 'animal' and what is with it—but not in terms of their essence as appears in the statement of al-Shamsiyya, rather in terms of their being a subject for logical universality, i.e., being characterized by it preventing sharing. (Quote: 'natural universal') It is called universal because it is a subject for the concept of the universal inasmuch as it is a subject for it, and natural because it is attributed to nature as an individual is attributed to the concept.
Chapter: On the Principles of Concepts
The distinction between these two—the concept and the subject—is clear. For the concept is that whose mere conception does not prevent the occurrence of sharing in it. And the subject is that to which universality occurs, like animal and human for example. And it is known that the concept of the universal is not identical to the concept of animal, nor a part of it.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes the natural universal from the logical universal, focusing on the difference between concept (mafhum) and subject (ma'rud). The author begins by stating that the difference is clear, then clarifies that the natural universal is not the essence of the individuals (as the Shamsiyya implies) but rather the individuals considered as subjects of logical universality—meaning they are characterized by the property of preventing sharing (i.e., being universal). The phrase 'natural universal' is then analyzed: it is 'universal' because it serves as a subject for the concept of universality, and 'natural' because it relates to nature as an individual relates to its concept. The section titled 'مبادئ التصورات' (Principles of Concepts) formally defines 'concept' as that whose mere conception does not prevent multiple individuals from participating in it, and 'subject' as that which receives universality, giving the examples of 'animal' and 'human'. It then emphasizes that the concept of the universal is neither identical to nor a part of the concept of 'animal'—they are distinct mental entities. This critical refinement guards against confusing the logical universal (the abstract property of being universal) with the natural universal (the concrete reality like 'animal' that is characterized by universality). Throughout, the commentary by al-Dasuqi and al-‘Attar refines the earlier position of al-Shamsiyya.

المعروض
الكلية المنطقية

B) The individuals considered as subjects for logical universality.
C) The logical concept that prevents sharing.
D) The genus in a definition.

Key Takeaway
The natural universal is the individuals considered as subjects of logical universality, not the essence.

Concept vs Subject: Distinguishing the Two Sides of Universality
Author defines concept and subject and distinguishes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the logical concept (mafhum) of universality and the subject (ma'rud) that receives it, and explain why they are not identical.

Core Idea
The concept of universality is not the same as the subject that exhibits it.

In the second part of the passage, the author formally defines 'concept' and 'subject' as two distinct entities. The concept is that which, in its mere conception, does not prevent sharing—this is the logical universal. The subject is that to which universality occurs, like 'animal' or 'human'. The author emphasizes that the concept of the universal is neither identical to nor part of the concept of 'animal'. This is a critical refinement that prevents confusion between what universality is and what it characterizes.

Guided Path
1
Find the definitions of mafhum and ma'rud
Locate in the passage: 'al-mafhum huwa ma la yamna' nafsu tasawwuruhu 'an wuqu' al-sharika fihi' and 'al-ma'rud huwa ma ta'rud lahu al-kulliyya'. Memorize or note these definitions.
These are technical definitions central to Islamic logic.
2
Apply the definitions to an example
Take 'animal' as subject: it receives universality because many individuals share in being animal. The concept of 'universal' is separate: it is the property of being predicable of many.
The concept of 'universal' is not identical to the concept 'animal'.
3
Recognize the distinctness
The author explicitly states: 'walaysa mafhum al-kulli huwa bi'aynihi mafhum al-hayawan, wala juz'an lahu'. This is a strong assertion of ontological distinctness.
This avoids the mistake of thinking universality is a part of the nature of things.
4
Relate to the natural universal
The natural universal is the subject (like 'animal'), while the logical universal is the concept (like 'being universal'). The passage's first part already distinguished them; this section formalizes the distinction.
Together, they form a complete picture of how universals function in logic.
