The Three Kinds of Universals: Natural, Logical, and Rational
Author defines and distinguishes the three types of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of universals (natural, logical, rational) and identify each in a given definition from Islamic theology or jurisprudence.

Core Idea
A universal can be considered as essence, as attribute, or as both combined in the mind.

In logic, a universal (kulli) can be viewed from three distinct perspectives: as a concept that can be predicated of many, as the essence that bears that predication, and as the mental composite of the two. This lesson unpacks the passage from al-Tadhheeb that clarifies these distinctions, guarding against common confusions. Mastering this tripartite classification is essential for precise definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two basic components
Read the Arabic: 'المفهوم والمعروض ظاهر'. The author refers to 'the concept' (al-mafhum) and 'the subject' (al-ma'rud). These are the two fundamental elements that any universal involves. The concept is the logical universal; the subject is the natural universal.
2
Define the logical universal (concept)
The text defines the concept as 'ما لا يمنعُ نَفْسُ تصورِه عن وقوع الشَّرِكَةِ فيه' — that whose very conception does not prevent sharing in it. This means the logical universal is the attribute of universality itself, considered as a mental notion that can be applied to many individuals.
Think of the attribute 'whiteness' that can be shared by many white objects.
3
Define the natural universal (subject)
The subject is 'ما تَعرُضُ له الكُلِّيَّةُ' — that to which universality attaches. Examples given are 'الحيوان' (animal) and 'الإنسان' (human). These are essences considered in themselves, before adding the attribute of being universal.
The natural universal is the reality, e.g., 'horse' as a species, not yet considered as predicated of many.
4
Recognise their relationship
The text stresses that the concept of the universal is not identical to the subject (like animal) nor a part of it, but is an external attribute fit to be predicated. This guards against the error of thinking the logical universal is the same as the essence or a component of it.
Just as whiteness is neither the same as a white garment nor a part of it, universality is not the same as animal nor a part of animal.
5
Understand the rational universal
The third type is 'المجموع' (the composite) of the concept and the subject, called the rational universal (كلي عقلي). This is the universal as it exists in the mind, combining the essence (natural) with the attribute of universality (logical).
When we think of 'animal' as predicable of many, we are using the rational universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات والتَّغاير بين هذين المفهوم والمعروض ظاهر. فإِنَّ المفهوم : هو ما لا يمنعُ نَفْسُ تصورِه عن وقوع الشَّرِكَةِ فيه . والمعروضُ : هو ما تَعرُضُ له الكُلِّيَّةُ، كالحيوان والإنسان مثلاً ومن المعلومِ أنَّ مفهوم الكُلِّيَّ ليس هو بعينه مفهوم الحيوان، ولا جزءاً له، بل خارج عنه صالح لأن يُحمل على الحيوان وعلى غيره، كالإنسان، والنَّاطِقِ ممَّا تعرضُ له الكلية في العقل (وَ) ثالثها : (المجْمُوعُ) المركَّبُ من المفهوم والمعروض، ويسمى كليا (عَقْلِيًّا ).
Chapter: On the principles of concepts. And the distinction between these two – the concept and the subject – is apparent. For the concept is that whose very conception does not prevent the occurrence of sharing in it. And the subject is that to which universality attaches, like animal and human, for example. And it is known that the concept of the universal is not identical to the concept of animal, nor is it a part of it, but rather it is external to it, fit to be predicated of animal and of other things, such as human and rational, among things to which universality attaches in the mind. And third: the composite, composed of the concept and the subject, and it is called 'rational' universal.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes the three types of universals: the logical universal (kulli mantiqi), the natural universal (kulli tabi'i), and the rational universal (kulli 'aqli). It begins by stating that the difference between the 'concept' (mafhum) and the 'subject' (ma'rud) is clear. The 'concept' here refers to the meaning of the word 'universal' (kulli), defined as that which does not prevent sharing in its conception — this is the logical universal, an attribute. The 'subject' is the reality to which universality attaches, such as 'animal' or 'human' — this is the natural universal, the essence considered in itself. The author then clarifies that the concept of the universal is not identical to the subject (e.g., 'animal'), nor is it a part of it; rather, it is an external attribute that can be predicated of the subject and of others, like 'rational'. This is analogous to whiteness attaching to a garment. The third type is the composite of the concept and the subject, called the rational universal, which is the universal considered as a mental construct combining the essence and the attribute of universality. The commentary by al-Dasuqi and al-Attar refines these definitions, correcting misconceptions such as those of the author of al-Shamsiyya, and notes that the logical universal is not a part of the natural universal but a separate attribute. The passage guards against conflating the logical universal with the natural universal or thinking it is a component of it.

المعروض
المجموع

D) The attribute of being predicated of many, considered in the mind.

Key Takeaway
Every universal can be considered in three ways: as a concept (logical), as an essence (natural), and as a mental composite (rational); failing to distinguish them leads to confusion in definitions and arguments.

6 min
Distinguishing the Three Universals: Logical, Natural, Rational
Defines the three types of universal and their naming rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-kulli al-mantiqi, al-kulli al-tabi'i, and al-kulli al-'aqli and explain the rationale for each name.

Core Idea
The same universal can be viewed in three ways: as a concept, as a reality, or as a mental composite.

Logic deals with concepts, not just things in the world. The same universal, like 'human', can be examined as a logical tool (genus/species), as a real nature existing in individuals, or as a composite of the two in the mind. This lesson unpacks these three aspects, which are foundational for precise definition in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Locate in the Arabic the terms for logical, natural, and rational universal. Note that the author defines each by its relation to the logician, to external reality, and to the mind.
The logical universal is the concept itself; the natural universal is its external referent; the rational universal is their composite.
2
Understand the naming rationale
Why is each called what it is? The logical universal is investigated by the logician; the natural universal is a nature; the rational universal only exists in the mind. The commentators discuss why the logical universal isn't also called rational.
The reason for naming is not arbitrary—it reflects the aspect under which the universal is considered.
3
Analyze the composite nature of the rational universal
The rational universal combines the logical concept and its subject in a mental composition. Al-'Attar explains that this involves abstraction, a common operation in logic.
This composite is purely mental; it does not exist externally even if its individual instances do.
4
Apply the distinction to a definition
Take an example from fiqh: the term 'riba'. The logical universal is the concept 'riba' as a genus with differentia; the natural universal is the actual prohibited transactions; the rational universal is the mental composite of the concept and its instances.
This distinction prevents confusion between the definition and the defined instances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات
فإذا تقرر هذا فنقولُ : مفهومُ الكُلّيّ يسمَّى كُلِّيًّا منطقيا ؛ لأنَّ المنطقي إِنَّما يبحث عنه، ومعروضه يسمَّى كُلِّيًّا طبيعيا ؛ لأنَّه طبيعة من الطبائع، والمجموع المركَّبُ منهما يُسمَّى كُلِّيًّا عقليا ؛ لعدم تحققه إلا في العقل .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : إِنَّمَا يَبْحثُ عَنْهُ أي : من حيثُ كونُهُ جنساً أو نوعاً أو فصلاً أو خاصَّةً أو عرضاً عاماً .
(قَوْلُهُ : وَمَعْرُوْضُهُ) أي : من حيثُ إِنَّهُ مَعروضُهُ؛ لَا مِن حيثُ ذاتُهُ كَمَا مرَّ .
(قَوْلُهُ : طَبِيعَةٌ مِنَ الطَّبَائِعِ) أي: حقيقة من الحقائق.
(قَوْلُهُ: إِلَّا فِي الْعَقْل) أي : والمنطقي أيضاً لا تحقَّقُ لهُ إِلَّا في العقلِ، وَلَا يُقالُ: يلزمُ مِن ذلكَ أنْ يُسَمَّى المنطقي عقليا ؛ لأنَّ علَّةَ التسمية لا تقتضي التسمية أنَّ الكُلِّيَّ المنطقي وجدَ له حكمة تقتضي تسميته باسم آخر، وهذا لا حكمة.
العطار
والمنطقي عقلي، قاله المحشّي ، والسُّؤال ظاهر، ومُحصِّلُ الجَوابِ : أَنَّا نَعتبرُ في المركَبِ ذاتَ الطَّبيعي بقطع النَّظَرِ عَن كَونِهِ مَعروضاً لِلمنطقيّ، فيرجعُ لِلتّجريد، وهو شائع كثيراً في كلامهم، وقول البعض ؛ أي: المركَّبُ مِن مفهوم العارض والمعروض من حيثُ هُوَ مَعروض ، وردُّه على المحنِّي بأنَّ مَا قالَهُ وَسوَسَةٌ، فَتَأَمَّلْ مُنْصِفَاً . (قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّ الْمَنْطِقِيَ إِنَّمَا يَبْحَثُ عَنْهُ) عِلَّةٌ لِلتَّسمية، ومعنى بحث المنطقي عنه؛ أنَّهُ يأخذُ مفهوم الكُلّيّ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ بِلا إسنادٍ لِمَادَّةٍ مَخصوصةٍ، ويورد عليه أحكاماً لتكون تلك الأحكام شاملةً لجميع ما صدق عليهِ مَفهومُ الكُلِّيَّ، لَا أَنَّهُ يبحثُ عن الكُلِّي نَفْسِهِ حَتَّى تكونَ القضيَّةُ طبيعيَّةً، كَذَا في السَّيْدِ وعبدِ الحكيم .
(قَوْلُهُ: لِأَنَّهُ طَبِيعَةٌ مِنَ الطَّبَائِعِ) أي: حقيقةٌ من الحقائقِ . (قَوْلُهُ: لِعَدَمِ تَحَقُّقِهِ أي : هذا المفهوم إلا في العقلِ ؛ لأنَّ التَّركيبَ مِنَ المعروض والعارض عقلي صرف ؛ سواءٌ قُلْنَا بوجودِ مَا يصدق عليه في الخارج.
(١) (قَوْلُهُ : باسم (آخر) أي: غير العقلي وهو المنطقي، والحكمة هي بحث المنطقي عنه، لأنه هو الذي يوصل إلى المجهول، بخلاف الجزئي. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي
Chapter: On the Principles of Concepts. So when this is established, we say: The concept of the universal is called 'logical universal' because the logician only investigates it; its subject is called 'natural universal' because it is a nature among natures; and the composite of the two is called 'rational universal' because it does not actualize except in the mind. Al-Dasuqi: His saying 'he only investigates it' means in terms of its being a genus, species, differentia, property, or accident. His saying 'and its subject' means in terms of it being his subject, not in terms of its essence as previously. His saying 'a nature among natures' means a reality among realities. His saying 'except in the mind' means the logical also has no actuality except in the mind, and it is not said that the logical should be called rational because the reason for naming does not necessitate naming the logical universal with another name, and there is no wisdom in that. Al-'Attar: And the logical is rational, said by the commentator, and the question is apparent, and the summary of the answer: we consider in the composite the essence of the natural abstracted from its being a subject for the logical, turning to abstraction, which is common in their speech. The saying of some, i.e., the composite of the concept of the accident and the subject in terms of it being a subject, and his refutation of the commentator that his saying is nonsense, so consider fairly. His saying 'because the logician only investigates it' is the cause for naming, and the meaning of the logician's investigation of it is that he takes the concept of the universal as such without attribution to a particular matter, and applies rulings to it so that those rulings encompass all that the concept of the universal applies to, not that he investigates the universal itself so that the proposition would be natural, as stated by al-Sayyid and 'Abd al-Hakim. His saying 'because it is a nature among natures' means a reality among realities. His saying 'because it does not actualize' means this concept only actualizes in the mind; because the composition of the subject and the accident is purely mental, whether we affirm the existence of what it applies to externally. (1) His saying: 'another name' i.e., other than 'rational' which is 'logical', and the wisdom is the logician's investigation of it because it leads to the unknown, unlike the particular.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the three types of universal: logical, natural, and rational. It is from a commentary (sharh) on a matn, and it expands on the matn's claim. The author begins by stating that the concept of the universal (mafhum al-kulli) is called the logical universal because the logician investigates it. This means the logician examines the universal in terms of its logical functions: as a genus, species, differentia, property, or accident. The subject (ma'rud) of that concept—the external individual or nature that bears the universal—is called the natural universal, because it is a nature among natures (i.e., a reality). The composite of the two—the concept and its subject—is called the rational universal, because it only exists in the mind. Al-Dasuqi comments that the logical universal also exists only in the mind, and one might ask why it is not called 'rational' as well. The answer is that the reason for naming the logical universal is its being investigated by the logician, and that wisdom does not require renaming it. Al-'Attar adds more detail: the logical universal is indeed rational (mental) as the commentator said, but there is a subtlety: when we consider the composite 'rational universal', we abstract the natural universal from its being a subject of the logical universal, resulting in a purely mental composite. This is a common abstraction in their discourse. He also clarifies that the logician investigates the concept of the universal as such, without tying it to any particular matter, in order to apply general rulings to all instances. This distinguishes his investigation from that of the natural scientist who studies the universal itself as a natural proposition. The passage thus provides a precise taxonomy of the universal, crucial for understanding definitions and scientific reasoning in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
الكلي المنطقي
الكلي الطبيعي
الكلي العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Al-kulli al-tabi'i
C) Al-kulli al-'aqli
D) Al-kulli al-juz'i

Key Takeaway
The three universals—logical, natural, rational—are aspects of the same universal, distinguished by whether we consider the concept, its external reality, or their mental composite.

The Threefold Division Applied to the Five Universals
Author extends the threefold division to genus, species, difference, property, and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical, natural, and mental aspects of any universal among the five.

Core Idea
Every universal has three facets: logical concept, natural subject, and mental composite.

The previous lesson taught you that a universal like 'genus' can be viewed in three ways: as a concept in the mind, as a subject in reality, and as their combination. Now the author applies this same threefold division to all five universals: genus, species, difference, property, and accident. Even the particular (juz'i) gets the same treatment, though logicians rarely discuss it. This systematic extension shows the coherence of the logical framework.

Guided Path
1
Review the threefold division
Recall: For any universal, there is a logical aspect (the concept as predicated), a natural aspect (the external subject that carries that property), and a mental aspect (the composite of both existing only in the mind).
The example of 'animal' as genus: logical genus = the concept 'animal' predicated; natural genus = actual animals; mental genus = the combination.
2
Apply to one universal in the passage
The author takes genus as the example. He identifies the logical genus (mafhum al-jins), natural genus (ma'rud al-jins, e.g., 'animal' or 'growing body'), and mental genus (al-majmu' al-murakkab).
Read the Arabic: 'fama'hum al-jins... yusamma jinsan mantiqiyyan... ma'rud al-jins... jinsan tabi'iyyan... al-majmu' al-murakkab... jinsan 'aqliyyan.'
3
Extend to the other four universals
The passage states 'wa kadha al-naw' wa sa'ir al-kulliyyat al-khams.' So species, difference, property, and accident each have three aspects. Try to name them for 'species' using 'insan' (human) as natural species.
Logical species: concept 'human'; natural species: individual humans; mental species: composite.
4
Note the extension to the particular
Al-Attar adds that even the particular (juz'i), like Zayd, has three aspects: natural particular (Zayd's essence preventing sharing), logical particular (the concept 'that which prevents sharing'), and mental particular (composite). But logicians ignore it because their aim is universals.
This shows completeness: the threefold division is universal, but only relevant for the five universals in logical study.

وَكَذَا الأَنْوَاعُ الخَمْسَةُ من الجنسِ والنَّوع، والفصل، والخاصة، والعَرَضِ العام، يعتبر فيها الأمورُ الثَّلاثةُ المذكورة. فَمفهوم الجنس - وهو المقولُ على الكثرة المختلفة الحقيقة في جواب ما هو ؟ - يُسمَّى جنساً منطقياً. ومعروضُ الجنس - أي ما تعرضُ له الجنسية كالحيوان والجسم النَّامي مثلاً - يُسمَّى : جنساً طبيعيًّا، والمجموع المركَّبُ منهما يُسمَّى : جنساً عقليا، وكذا النّوعُ وسائر الكليَّاتِ الخَمسِ. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : فَمَنْهُوْمُ الْجِنْسِ) أي : الواقع محمولاً في قولك مثلاً: «الحيوان جنس». (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ : مَا تَعْرُضُ لَهُ الْجِنْسِيَّةُ) أي: والحقائق التي تعرضُ لها الجنسيَّةُ المنطقيَّةُ ؛ أي: من حيثُ إِنَّها مَعروضةٌ لها وموصوفةٌ بها . (قَوْلُهُ : وَسَائِرُ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ الْخَمْسِ) أي : باقيها، وليس المراد جميعها، وإلا لدخلَ مَا تقدَّمَ مِن الجنسِ والنَّوع . العطار لِكَوْنِ العارض والمعروض موجودَيْنِ في الخارج كالأبيضِ، أو قُلْنَا بِعَدَمِهِ؛ لِعَدَمِ كَوْنِ العارضِ موجوداً ، قاله عبد الحكيم، ومِثْلُهُ في عدم الوجودِ إلا في العقل الكُلّيّ المنطقيّ، ولكنَّهُ لا يُسمَّى عَقليّا ؛ لأنَّ عِلَّةَ النَّسيمِةِ لا يُحِبُّ إِطْرادُهَا . (قَوْلُهُ: وَكَذَا الْأَنْوَاعُ الْخَمْسَةُ بَلْ والجزئي تعرضُ له الاعتباراتُ الثَّلاثُ، فإذا قُلْنَا : زيد جزئيّ ; فذاتُ زيدٍ مِن حيثُ تمنعُ الشِّركةَ جُزئي طبيعي، ومفهوم الجزئي؛ أعني : مَا يمنعُ الشِّركةَ؛ جزئي منطقي، والمجموع المركَّبُ منهما جزئي عقلي، ولم يتعرَّضُوا له؛ لِعَدَم تَعلَّقِ الغرضِ بِهِ. (قَوْلُهُ : وَمَعْرُوضُ الْجِنْسِ ) أي : مِنْ حيثُ ذاتُهُ أوْ بِقيدِ كونه معروضاً على اختلافِ الرَّأْيَيْنِ، وكلامُ الشَّارحِ ظَاهِرُ في التَّقييدِ .
And similarly, the five types: genus, species, difference, property, and general accident, the three mentioned matters are considered in them. So the concept of genus - which is predicated of many differing in essence in answer to 'what is it?' - is called logical genus. And the subject of genus - i.e., that which genus-ness attaches to, such as animal and growing body, for example - is called natural genus, and the composite of the two is called mental genus. Likewise for species and the rest of the five universals. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'the concept of genus' means that which occurs as a predicate in your saying, for example, 'Animal is a genus.' His statement: 'that which genus-ness attaches to' means the realities to which logical genus-ness attaches, i.e., insofar as they are subjects and described by it. His statement: 'and the rest of the five universals' means the remaining ones, and not all of them, otherwise what was mentioned before of genus and species would be included. Al-Attar: Because the accident and subject exist externally like white, or we say with their non-existence due to the accident not existing, said Abd al-Hakim, and similarly in non-existence except in the mind the logical universal, but it is not called mental because the cause of naming does not require its uniformity. His statement: 'and similarly the five types' rather, the particular also has the three considerations. So if we say: Zayd is particular; then the essence of Zayd insofar as it prevents sharing is natural particular, and the concept of particular, i.e., that which prevents sharing, is logical particular, and the composite of the two is mental particular, but they did not address it because the purpose is not attached to it. His statement: 'and the subject of genus' means from the perspective of its essence or with the condition of it being a subject, according to the difference of the two views, and the words of the commentator are apparent in the restriction.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage applies the threefold division (logical, natural, mental) to each of the five universals: genus, species, difference, property, and accident. The author takes genus as an example: its concept (mafhum) when predicated in answer to 'what is it?' is logical genus; its subject (ma'rud), like 'animal' or 'growing body', is natural genus; and the combination of both is mental genus. The same applies to the other four universals. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that 'concept of genus' means the universal as predicate, and 'subject' refers to the realities that possess genus-ness. Al-Attar adds an important extension: the particular (juz'i) also has these three considerations, using Zayd as an example: Zayd's essence preventing sharing is natural particular, the concept 'that which prevents sharing' is logical particular, and their composite is mental particular. However, logicians usually do not discuss the particular's threefold division because their focus (gharad) is on universals. This highlights the systematic nature of Islamic logic in applying distinctions consistently.

جنس طبيعي
جنس عقلي

Three Aspects of the Five Universals: Logical, Natural, Rational
Defines threefold consideration of universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the logical, natural, and rational aspects of any universal (e.g., genus, species) and identify which aspect is being used in a given definition or argument.

Core Idea
Every universal has three faces: concept, instance, and the mental composite.

You already know the five universals: genus, species, differentia, property, and common accident. Now we add a crucial nuance: each universal can be considered in three ways. This threefold distinction prevents confusion between a mere concept and its real-world manifestation. Mastering it is essential for precise definitions in logic and its applied sciences like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three considerations
The passage states that for each of the five universals there are three considerations: logical, natural, and rational. Read carefully to see how these are defined for genus.
The three terms are: منطقي (mantiqi, logical), طبيعي (tabi'i, natural), عقلي (aqli, rational).
2
Understand the logical genus
Logical genus is the very concept of genus: a universal predicated of many differing in reality in answer to 'what is it?'. This is the meaning in your mind, independent of any specific example.
Think of it as the definition or ma'na.
3
Understand the natural genus
Natural genus is the external entity that actually possesses the property of genus. For example, 'animal' or 'growing body' are natural genera – they are real things that can be pointed to.
This is the subject (ma'rud) that genus-ness attaches to.
4
Understand the rational genus
Rational genus is the composite of both the logical concept and its natural subject. It exists only in the mind as a combination of the two.
It is 'aqli because it is a mental synthesis.
5
Apply to the other universals
The passage says the same threefold division applies to species, differentia, property, and common accident. Try to extend the analysis: what would be logical species, natural species, rational species?
For species, logical species is the concept 'species', natural species is e.g. 'human', rational species is the composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكَذَا الأَنْوَاعُ الخَمْسَةُ من الجنسِ والنَّوع، والفصل، والخاصة، والعَرَضِ العام، يعتبر فيها الأمورُ الثَّلاثةُ المذكورة. فَمفهوم الجنس - وهو المقولُ على الكثرة المختلفة الحقيقة في جواب ما هو ؟ - يُسمَّى جنساً منطقياً. ومَعروضُ الجنس - أي ما تعرضُ له الجنسية كالحيوان والجسم النَّامي مثلاً - يُسمَّى : جنساً طبيعيًّا، والمجموع المركَّبُ منهما يُسمَّى : جنساً عقليا، وكذا النّوعُ وسائر الكليَّاتِ الخَمسِ
And likewise the five species — genus, species, differentia, property, and common accident — the three mentioned considerations are considered in them. The concept of genus — which is predicated of many differing in reality in answer to 'what is it?' — is called logical genus. The subject of genus — i.e., that which genus-ness attaches to, like animal and growing body, for example — is called natural genus. And the composite of the two is called rational genus. Similarly for species and the rest of the five universals.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage defines the three aspects of each of the five universals. Taking genus as an example: the logical genus is the abstract concept of genus as a predicate answering 'what is it?'. The natural genus is the external entity that actually possesses the property of being a genus, such as 'animal' or 'growing body'. The rational genus is the combination of both the concept and its subject. This threefold division applies analogously to species, differentia, property, and common accident.

Key Takeaway
The threefold distinction—logical (concept), natural (external subject), rational (composite)—applies to each of the five universals, and understanding it prevents category errors in defining terms and reasoning.

Grammatical Substitution in 'Al-Anwa' and the Genus-as-Type Objection
Explains grammatical substitution and resolves an objection about genus as a type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the grammatical substitution in 'al-anwa'' and explain why the objection that genus becomes a type does not undermine the five universals classification.

Core Idea
The definite article in 'al-anwa'' substitutes for a possessive, making the five universals types under the universal-as-genus.

This passage tackles a subtle grammatical issue in the phrase 'al-anwa' al-khamsa' and answers a logical objection. Understanding this helps clarify the taxonomic relationship between the universal and its five types, preventing a common misunderstanding about genus and species.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Grammatical Role of 'al'
Note that the definite article 'al' in 'al-anwa'' is not the usual definite article. The commentator says it stands in place of a possessive pronoun (niyaba 'an al-mudaf ilayhi). Determine what that possessive pronoun refers to: 'its' (the universal's) types.
2
Parse the Objection
The objector says: if the five universals are types (anwa'), then genus (jins) among them would be a type, which seems contradictory because genus is above type. Understand the logical tension.
3
Understand al-Dasuqi's Commentary
Al-Dasuqi notes that the objection should apply not only to genus but to any of the five universals except species itself, since species is already a type. This shows the objection is not specific to genus.
4
Resolve the Seeming Contradiction
The resolution is that the universal (al-kulli) is being treated as a genus that encompasses all five universals as its types. So each of the five, including genus itself, is a type relative to that overarching universal. No contradiction exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات
واعلَمْ أَنَّ الأَلِفَ واللَّامَ في الأنواع» عِوَض عن المضاف إليه، وهو الضميرُ العائد إلى الكُلّي - أي : وكذا أنواعه الخمسة ـ فالكُلِّي جنس تحته أنواع، وهي الكليات الخمس .
فإن قيل : إذا كانتِ الكُلِّيَّاتُ أنواعاً ؛ يلزم أن يكون الجنس نوعاً .
قال الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : يَلْزَمُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْجِنْسُ نَوْعَاً لا وجه لتخصيص السُّؤالِ بالجنسِ، فإنّهُ جارٍ في أخواتِهِ مَا عدا النَّوع ، فالظَّاهِرُ أن يقول : يلزم أن يكونَ كُلُّ واحدٍ مِمَّا عدا النَّوع ، وهو الجنس والفصل والخاص والعرضُ العام نوعاً .
Chapter: On the Principles of Conceptualizations. Know that the definite article 'al' in 'al-anwa'' is a substitute for the possessive pronoun, which is the pronoun referring back to the universal — i.e., 'and likewise its five types' — so the universal is a genus under which there are types, namely the five universals. If it is said: If the universals are types, then it necessitates that genus be a type. Al-Dasuqi said: His statement 'it necessitates that genus be a type' — there is no reason to specify the objection to genus, for it applies to its counterparts except species, so it would be apparent that he should say: it necessitates that each one other than species — namely genus, differentia, property, common accident — be a type.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage clarifies a grammatical subtlety in the phrase 'al-anwa' al-khamsa'. The definite article 'al' here does not indicate definiteness by prior mention but stands for a possessive construction: 'its five types', where 'its' refers to the universal (al-kulli). Thus, the universal is a genus that has the five universals as its types. An objection is raised: if the five universals are types (anwa'), then genus itself — which is one of them — would be considered a type, contradicting its role as a higher genus. Al-Dasuqi responds by noting that the objection should apply equally to all the universals except species itself, since species is already a type by definition. The implied resolution is that these five are types relative to the universal considered as a genus, so there is no contradiction: each universal is a type under that overarching genus.

Key Terms
الألف واللام
الكلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The grammatical reading of 'al-anwa'' as 'its types' resolves the seeming contradiction that genus would be a type when the five universals are called types.

The Relativity of Genus and Species in the Five Universals
Defines the five universals and resolves an objection about genus being a species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the same universal can function as both genus and species depending on the perspective, using the example of animal.

Core Idea
Genus and species are not fixed; the same universal can be a genus in one relation and a species in another.

This passage tackles a classic objection: if the five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, common accident) are all species of the universal, then the genus itself would be a species, which seems contradictory. The commentators resolve this by showing that the terms 'genus' and 'species' are relative — a concept can be a species when considered under a higher category and a genus when considered over lower categories. Understanding this relativity is crucial for using the five universals correctly in definition and classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection states: if the five universals are species (of the universal), then the genus becomes a species. This appears problematic because genus and species are normally distinct.
The objector assumes fixity of roles.
2
Find the resolution in the text
The response (قلت) says: 'There is no harm, for it is a species under one consideration and a genus under another.' The same concept can be both, depending on the level of generality.
3
Analyze the example given
The example of 'animal' (الحيوان) is used: it is a species of the universal (because it is subsumed under the concept of universal) and a genus for species like human, horse, etc.
This illustrates the dual consideration.
4
Apply the principle to another universal
Consider 'rational' (differentia): it is a species under the universal (as a concept) but a differentia when predicated of many species. Explain its dual role.
This reinforces the relative nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات واعلَمْ أَنَّ الأَلِفَ واللَّامَ في الأنواع» عِوَض عن المضاف إليه، وهو الضميرُ العائد إلى الكُلّي - أي : وكذا أنواعه الخمسة ـ فالكُلِّي جنس تحته أنواع، وهي الكليات الخمس . فإن قيل : إذا كانتِ الكُلِّيَّاتُ أنواعاً ؛ يلزم أن يكون الجنس نوعاً . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : عِوَضٌ عَن الْمُضَافِ إِلَيْهِ هذا مذهب الكوفيين، أما عند البصريين؛ فَفِي الكلام حذف حذف؛ أي: وكذا الأنواع الخمسة الكائنة له؛ أي: لِلكُلِّي يعتبر في كلِّ واحدٍ منها الأمور الثَّلاثة المذكورة. قَوْلُهُ : فَالْكُلّيّ أي : مِن حيثُ هو جنس . قَوْلُهُ: يَلْزَمُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْجِنْسُ نَوْعَاً لا وجه لتخصيص السُّؤالِ بالجنسِ، فإنّهُ جارٍ في أخواتِهِ مَا عدا النَّوع ، فالظَّاهرُ أنْ يقول : يلزم أن يكونَ كُلُّ واحدٍ مِمَّا عدا النَّوع ، وهو الجنس والفصل والخاص والعرضُ العام نوعاً .) العطار (قَوْلُهُ : عِوَضٌ عَنِ المُضَافِ إِلَيْهِ لم يجعلْها عهديَّةً؛ لأَنَّهُ لمْ يتقدَّم التَّعرُّض لكونها أنواعاً لِلكُلِّيّ حتّى يصح العهد. قَوْلُهُ : يَلْزَمُ أَنْ يَكُوْنَ الْجِنْسُ نَوْعَاً لا وجه لتخصيص الجنس، بلْ مثلُهُ سائرُ الكُلِّيَّاتِ مَا عدا النَّوع الحقيقي، فكانَ الظَّاهِرُ أنْ يقول: يلزمُ أنْ يكونَ ما عدا النوع نوعاً، ويقول في الجوابِ : فإنَّها نوع باعتبارِ جنس وفصل وخاصَّة، وعرض عام باعتبار مثلاً : الحيوانُ؛ باعتبار اندراجهِ تحت مفهوم الكلّيّ نوع منه، وباعتبار مقولتيه على الكثرة المختلفة الحقيقة جنس، ويُقالُ مثلُه في البقيَّةِ، وقَدْ يُجَابُ بأَنَّ الشَّارحَ تركَ التَّنصيص على البقيَّةِ اتكالاً على معرفته بالمقايسة.) قُلْتُ : لا محذور في ذلك، فإنَّه نوع باعتبار، وجنس باعتبار آخر .
Section: On the principles of concepts. Know that the definite article in 'the species' is a substitute for the possessive pronoun, which is the pronoun referring back to the universal - i.e., 'and likewise its five species'. So the universal is a genus under which there are species, namely the five universals. If it is said: if the universals are species, it follows that the genus is a species. al-Dasuqi (his statement: 'substitute for the possessive' - this is the view of the Kufans; as for the Basrans, there is an omission in the speech; i.e., 'and likewise the five species belonging to it'; i.e., for the universal, the three mentioned matters are considered in each of them. His statement: 'so the universal' i.e., insofar as it is a genus. His statement: 'it follows that the genus is a species' - there is no reason to restrict the question to the genus, for it applies to its siblings except the species, so it is apparent that he should say: it follows that each of what is other than the species, namely the genus, differentia, property, and common accident, is a species.) al-Attar (his statement: 'substitute for the possessive' - he did not make it for specific reference because there was no prior mention of them being species for the universal so that specific reference would be valid. His statement: 'it follows that the genus is a species' - there is no reason to restrict it to the genus; rather, the same applies to the other universals except the true species. So it was apparent that he should say: it follows that what is other than the species is a species, and he says in the answer: they are species under one consideration and genus under another, as for example: animal; by its subsumption under the concept of universal it is a species of it, and by its being predicated of multiple different realities it is a genus, and similarly for the rest, and it may be answered that the commentator left out specifying the rest relying on knowledge by analogy.) I said: There is no harm in that, for it is a species under one consideration and a genus under another.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and dialectical explanation of the five universals. It begins by clarifying that the phrase 'the species' (الأنواع) in the text refers to the five universals as species of the universal (الكلي). The author then raises an objection: if the universals (i.e., the five: genus, species, differentia, property, common accident) are species of the universal, then the genus itself would be a species, which seems contradictory because genus and species are distinct. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that the objection is valid for all universals except the true species (النوع الحقيقي), suggesting that the objector should have said 'it follows that everything other than the species is a species'. Then al-Attar elaborates that the answer is that each universal can be considered a species in one respect (as subsumed under the concept of universal) and a genus in another (as predicated of multiples). The example is given of 'animal': it is a species of the universal and a genus for its own species. The final response 'I said' (قلت) affirms that there is no problem because the dual consideration resolves the apparent contradiction. This lesson teaches the relativity of the terms genus and species within the framework of the five universals.

Key Terms
الكلي
الجنس
النوع

Checkpoint

A) The genus is always a species when considered absolutely.
B) The genus is a species under one consideration (as subsumed under the universal) and a genus under another (as predicated of many).
C) The objection is invalid because the genus cannot be a species under any consideration.
D) The five universals are not actually species of the universal.

Key Takeaway
The same universal can be both a species and a genus depending on the level of analysis, so there is no contradiction in the five universals being species of the universal.

Existence of the Natural Universal: Qualification and Refutation
Commentator qualifies the existence of the universal natural and refutes al-Razi's argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct sense in which the natural universal exists externally and refute al-Razi's misconception using the distinction between mental and external parts.

Core Idea
The natural universal exists externally only through its individuals, not as a separate entity – and not all such universals are actual.

This passage from a commentary on al-Tadhheeb refines the doctrine of the natural universal's external existence. It first qualifies the claim as an indefinite proposition, then refutes al-Razi's argument that the universal is an external part of an individual. Finally, it presents the correct understanding: the universal exists via its instances, but the quiddity with universality is purely mental. This lesson will equip you to navigate this nuanced position and avoid common pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commentator’s thesis
The commentator affirms the existence of the natural universal in the external world but immediately adds a qualification. Locate the phrase 'wa al-haqq' and note the condition that follows.
The qualification is crucial: not every natural universal exists externally.
2
Recognize the indefinite proposition
The statement 'the universal natural exists externally' is classified as an indefinite proposition (muhamila). Understand why examples like 'partner of the Creator' and 'phoenix' show that the claim is not universal.
3
Extract al-Razi's argument
al-Razi argued that the universal natural is a part of an existing individual (e.g., this animal), and since a part of an existent is existent, the universal must exist. Identify the premises and conclusion.
This is a categorical syllogism if we add the implicit major premise.
4
Follow the refutation
The commentator refutes al-Razi by rejecting that the absolute (al-mutlaq) is an external part; it is a mental part. Also, if it were external and one meaning, it would have contradictory attributes. Trace these two refutation steps.
The refutation uses the distinction between mental and external existence.
5
Understand the correct meaning
The true sense of existence: the natural universal exists externally in that there are external individuals (like Zayd and 'Amr) that instantiate the quiddity. But the quiddity with the attribute of universality is mental only, as universality contradicts external existence.
This is the position of the Shaykh (Ibn Sina).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالحَقُّ : وُجُودُ الكُلِّيِّ الطَّبِيعِيِّ فِي الْخَارِجِ. أي : قد يكون موجوداً فيه لا أنَّ كُلَّ كُلِّي طَبيعي موجودٌ في الخارج، إذْ مِنَ الكُلِّيَّاتِ الطَّبيعيَّةِ مَا هو ممتنع الوجودِ فيه كشريك الباري، وما هو معدومٌ مُمكنٌ كَالعنقاء. فَقولنا : الكُلِّي الطَّبيعي موجود في الخارج، قضيَّةٌ مُهملةٌ. وقد استدلَّ الرَّازِيُّ في شرح الرسالة على وجودِهِ بأنَّهُ جزءٌ مِنْ هذا الحيوان الموجود في الخارج، وجزء الموجود موجود. وردَّهُ المصنِّفُ في شرحِهِ عليها بأنَّا لا نسلّمُ أنَّ المطلق جزءٌ خارجي مِنَ الشَّخْصِ بَلْ ذهني، والجزء الذهني لا يجب وجوده في الخارج. وأيضاً لو كانَ المطلَقُ جزءاً خارجيًا مِنَ الأشخاص وهو معنى واحدٌ؛ لزمَ اتِّصافُهُ بِصفاتٍ متضادَّةٍ، وَوُجودُهُ في زمانٍ واحدٍ في أمكنةٍ مختلفةٍ. والحقُّ أنَّ الكُلِّيَّ الطَّبيعي موجود في الخارج، بمعنى أنَّ في الخارج شيئاً يصدق عليه الماهيَّةُ الَّتي إذا اعتبر عروضُ الكُلِّيَّاتِ لَهَا كَانَتْ كُلِّيًّا طبيعيًّا كَزيدٍ، وعمرو. وهذا ظاهر، وإليه أشارَ الشَّيخُ بِقَولِهِ : إِنَّ الطَّبيعةَ الَّتي يعرضُ الاشتراك لمعناها في العقلِ موجودةٌ في الخارج. وأَمَّا كَوْنُ الماهيَّةِ مَعَ اتّصافِهَا بِالكُلِّيَّةِ واعتبار عروضهَا لَهَا مَوجودةٌ؛ فَلَا دليل عليه، بل بديهة حاكمةٌ بأنَّ الكُلِّيَّةَ تُنَافي الوجود الخارجي.
And the truth is: the existence of the universal natural in the external world. That is, it may exist in it, not that every universal natural exists in the external world, for among the natural universals there is what is impossible of existence in it, like the partner of the Creator, and what is non-existent but possible, like the phoenix. So our saying: the universal natural exists in the external world is an indefinite proposition. And al-Razi argued in his commentary on al-Risalah for its existence by saying it is a part of this animal existing in the external world, and a part of an existent is existent. And the author refuted it in his commentary on it by saying: we do not accept that the absolute is an external part of the individual, but rather a mental part, and the mental part does not necessarily exist in the external world. Also, if the absolute were an external part of individuals while being a single meaning, it would necessitate that it be described with contradictory attributes, and that it exists at one time in different places. And the truth is that the universal natural exists in the external world, meaning that in the external world there is something to which the quiddity applies such that if the predication of universals to it is considered, it would be a universal natural, like Zayd and 'Amr. And this is apparent, and the Shaykh indicated it by his saying: the nature whose meaning in the intellect is susceptible to participation exists in the external world. As for the quiddity being, along with its description of universality and considering its predication to it, existent, there is no evidence for it; rather, intuition judges that universality contradicts external existence.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the doctrine of the universal natural's existence in the external world. The commentator begins by affirming the truth: the universal natural exists externally, but immediately qualifies this statement. Not every universal natural exists; some are impossible (like a partner of Allah) and some are possible but not actualized (like the phoenix). Thus the proposition 'the universal natural exists externally' is an indefinite (muhamila) proposition, not a universal one. The passage then presents and refutes al-Razi's argument that the universal natural is an external part of an existing individual (e.g., this animal), and since the part of an existent is existent, the universal must exist. The refutation, drawn from the author's commentary, rejects that the absolute (al-mutlaq) is an external part; rather it is a mental part, and mental parts do not necessarily have external existence. Additionally, if the absolute were an external part of multiple individuals while being a single meaning, it would lead to absurdities such as contradictory attributes and existence in multiple places at once. The correct understanding is that the universal natural exists externally in the sense that there are external individuals (like Zayd and 'Amr) that instantiate the quiddity which, when considered as subject to predication of universals, would be called a universal natural. This is the meaning indicated by the Shaykh (Ibn Sina): the nature that admits of sharing in the intellect exists externally. However, the quiddity itself with the attribute of universality does not exist externally, because universality is a mental concept that cannot be instantiated in external reality. This lesson guards against the misconception that the universal natural is a separate Platonic entity or that universality itself exists in the external world.

Key Terms
الكلي الطبيعي
القضية المهملة
المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what kind of proposition is 'The universal natural exists in the external world'?
A) A universal affirmative proposition
B) A universal negative proposition
C) An indefinite proposition
D) A conditional proposition

Key Takeaway
The natural universal exists externally only in the sense that its instances (individuals) are external; the quiddity with universality is purely mental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the claim: 'The prohibition of riba (usury) exists as a separate entity in the external world.' Using the commentator's distinction, explain why this claim is mistaken and how the prohibition actually exists.

The Natural Universal: Conditional Argument Against Dependent Existence
Dassuqi discusses two views on the natural universal's external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional syllogism (modus tollens) used to deny the dependent existence of the natural universal and evaluate its logical validity.

Core Idea
One thing cannot be in two places at once; therefore the universal cannot be a part of any individual.

This passage from Dassuqi's commentary examines whether the natural universal exists in the external world. While everyone agrees it cannot exist independently (since only particulars are external), there is disagreement on dependent existence. The denial hinges on a crisp conditional argument: if the universal were a part of each particular, it would be multiply located, which is impossible. Let's unpack that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
First, locate the two views: (1) The natural universal exists dependently within its individuals (the view followed by the commentator). (2) It has no external existence at all, not even dependently. Notice the phrase 'ذهب آخرون' and 'واختاره بعض المحققين'.
2
Extract the conditional argument against dependent existence
The deniers say: 'If the universal were a part of the particular existing externally, then one thing would inhabit multiple places at one time.' This is a conditional (if-then) statement. Find the Arabic: 'لَو كانَ جزءاً له؛ لَلَزِمَ أنْ يحلَّ الشَّيءُ الواحد في أمكنةٍ مُتعدّدةٍ في آن واحد'.
3
Identify the denied consequent
The consequent is 'one thing would be in multiple places at once'. This is impossible by first principles (one body cannot occupy two locations simultaneously). The passage says 'يلزم' (it would follow), but the impossibility is tacitly accepted.
4
Recognize the argument as modus tollens
The structure: If P (universal is a part), then Q (one thing in multiple places). But not Q (impossible). Therefore not P. This is valid modus tollens. The conclusion: the universal is not a part, so it has no dependent existence.
The argument is sound only if the impossibility of multiple location is granted, which it is in physical terms.
5
Assess the logical force
The argument does not prove the universal has no mode of existence at all (it may exist mentally), but it refutes the claim of external dependent existence. The passage shows this is a chosen view by 'some investigators'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات لا بمعنى الاستقلال، بل بمعنى وجود أشخاصه وأفراده، فإنَّ أفراده إذا كانت موجودة في الخارج، وهو جزء من الأفراد، فيكون موجوداً في الخارج تبعاً وضمناً. والحاصل: أنَّ الكُلِّيَّ الطَّبيعي لا وجود له في الخارج استقلالاً باتفاق؛ لأنَّ الموجود في الخارج لا يكون إلا جزئيا. واختلف؛ هَل له وجود في خارج الأعيان في ضمن أفرادِهِ؛ فيكون وجوده في الخارج تبعاً؛ لأنَّهُ جزءٌ لِلأفراد الموجودة، وجزء الموجود موجود؟، وهذا قول جماعة، وتبعهُمُ الشَّارح. وذهب آخرون إلى أنَّ الكُلِّيَّ الطَّبيعي لا وجود له؛ لا استقلالاً وَلَا تبعاً، واختاره بعضُ المحققين قائلاً : لَا تُسلَّمُ أنَّ الكُلّيّ جزء للجزئي الموجود في الخارج ؛ إذْ لَو كانَ جزءاً له؛ لَلَزِمَ أنْ يحلَّ الشَّيءُ الواحد في أمكنةٍ مُتعدّدةٍ في آن واحد ؛ لأنَّ الحيوانَ الكُلِّيَّ مُتحقِّق في زيدٍ وبكر المختلفي المكان والأوصاف، فيلزمُ أَنَّهُ مَوجودٌ.
Chapter on the principles of concepts. Not in the sense of independence, but in the sense of the existence of its individuals and particulars. For if its individuals exist in the external world, and it is a part of the individuals, then it exists in the external world dependently and implicitly. The conclusion: The natural universal has no independent existence in the external world by agreement, because what exists in the external world is only particular. There is disagreement: does it have existence in the external world within its individuals, such that its existence is dependent, because it is a part of the existing individuals, and the part of an existing thing exists? That is the view of a group, and the commentator follows them. Others hold that the natural universal has no existence, neither independently nor dependently, and some investigators chose this, saying: it is not conceded that the universal is a part of the particular existing in the external world; for if it were a part of it, it would necessarily follow that one thing would inhere in multiple places at one time, because the universal animal is realized in Zayd and 'Amr who differ in place and attributes, so it would follow that it exists.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary of Dassuqi on the sharh of Tahdhib al-Mantiq. It discusses the ontological status of the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi'i). The author begins by stating that the universal is not independent in existence, but its existence is through the existence of its individuals. He then presents the agreed fact that the universal does not exist independently, because only particulars exist externally. Then he presents a disagreement: one group holds that the universal exists dependently within its individuals as a part of them; another group denies any external existence. Dassuqi then outlines the argument of the latter group using a conditional syllogism: if the universal were a part of the particular, it would have to be in multiple places at once, since the universal animal is realized in Zayd and 'Amr who are in different locations. This impossibility proves it is not a part. This argument is rooted in the principle that one thing cannot be in two places simultaneously. The passage sets up the standard debate in Islamic logic about the reality of universals.

Key Terms
الكلي الطبيعي
استقلالا
تبعا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical form does the argument used by the 'some investigators' take?
A) Modus ponens: If P then Q; P; therefore Q.
B) Modus tollens: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P.
C) Disjunctive syllogism: Either P or Q; not P; therefore Q.
D) Simple conversion: 'All A are B' implies 'All B are A'.

Key Takeaway
The natural universal cannot be a part of external particulars because that would entail the absurdity of one thing being in many places at once.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the modus tollens argument used by the deniers of the natural universal's dependent existence. Write out the premises and conclusion in your own words, using an Islamic example (e.g., the universal 'living being' and individuals Zayd and 'Amr).
?
Find a similar conditional argument in a usul al-fiqh text (e.g., 'If ritual impurity were not removed by water, then...') and identify whether it uses modus tollens or modus ponens.

Distinction Between External and Mental Parts in Definitions
Distinguishing external and mental parts and repelling the objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an external part and a mental part and determine whether each type can be predicated of the whole.

Core Idea
The part of a thing has no inherent connection to existence; it is divided into external (unpredicable) and mental (predicable).

When we define something, we often refer to its parts—like 'animal' and 'rational' for man. But not all parts are equal: some are pieces of the physical thing itself, while others are concepts that constitute the essence. This lesson unpacks that difference and shows why only mental parts are genuinely predicable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of parts introduced
Find where the passage explicitly divides the part (juz') into an external part (juz' kharji) and a mental part (juz' dhihni). Notice the examples: wood for a bed and wall for a house are external; 'animal' for man is mental.
2
Check the relation to existence and predication
The passage states that the part itself has no relation to external or mental existence; it constitutes the essence regardless of existence. But external parts have a distinct prior existence, so they cannot be predicated—you don't say 'the house is a wall' because that would imply unity of existence where there is none.
Predication requires the subject and predicate to be one in existence.
3
Distinguish the two ways mental parts are considered
For mental parts, predication depends on how the part is taken: if taken as 'conditioned by not something' (bi-shart la shay'), i.e., as a bare constituent, it is not predicated. If taken as 'unconditioned' (la bi-shart shay'), i.e., without preventing addition of other attributes, it is predicated (e.g., 'Man is an animal').
This is a key subtlety: a part can be both part of the essence and predicable, depending on the mode of consideration.
4
Apply to the objection about the natural universal
Understand how this division repels the later scholars' objection: they claimed that if the part is external, it is merely the disputed point; if mental, it need not exist externally. The passage affirms that a mental part of an external existent does not require external existence—hence the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا قولُهم في تعريف زيدٍ : إِنَّهُ حيوان ناطق ؛ فهو تعريف ماهيته الاعتبارية لا الحقيقة .
العطار
زاهد : لؤ لم تكن الأشياء حاصلةٌ بِنَفْسِهَا في الخارج؛ لم تكن حاصلةٌ بِنَفْسِهَا في الذهن، ولم تكن الذَّاتِيَّاتُ مُتحقِّقَةٌ في الوجودَينِ ا.هـ. ومَبْناهُ مَا سَلَفَ مِنْ أَنَّ التحقيق أنَّ الحاصل في الذهن هو نَفْسُ الماهية الموجودةِ خَارِجاً؛ لا شبحُها ومثالُها (فَـ) ظهرَ أنَّ جزء الموجودِ مَوجودٌ (اندفع الاعتراض) مِنَ المتأخّرينَ المنكرين لوجودِ الكُلِّيِّ الطَّبيعي في الخارج على المتقدمين القائلين بذلك (الَّذي تلقاه الفحولُ بِالقبولِ وَ) ذلك الاعتراض هو أنَّهُ إذا أُريدَ أَنَّهُ جزءٌ لَهُ في الخارج؛ فَمَمْنوع، بل هو أوَّلُ (المسألة) المتنازع فيها (وإنْ أُريدَ أَنَّهُ جزءٌ لهُ في الذَّاتِ ; فَلَا نسلّمُ أنَّ الجزء الذهني للموجودِ الخارجي يجب أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج وَ) وجه (ذلك) الاندفاعِ ظَاهِرُ (لأنَّ الجزء مَا يتقوَّمُ بِهِ الشَّيءُ وَلَا تعلَّقَ لَهُ بالخارج والذهنِ؛ بل تتقوَّمُ بِهِ الماهيَّةُ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الوجود والعدم، نَعَمْ إِنَّهُ ينقسم إلى خارجي غيرِ (مَحمولٍ كَالخشب لِلسَّرير والجدار للبيتِ، فَلَا يُقالُ: البيتُ جدارٌ ; لأنَّ الحمل يقتضي الاتحاد في الوجودِ، والجزء الخارجي للشَّيء لهُ وُجودٌ مُتقدِّم عليه في الخارج، فَلَهُ وجودٌ مُغاير لوجودِ المركَبِ لِتقدُّمِهِ عليهِ، فلو حصلَ لَهُ مَعَ المركَبِ وجودٌ أَخَرُ; كانَ لَهُ وُجودانِ حينئذ , وَهُوَ مُحالٌ (وذهني محمولٍ) في قولنا مثلاً : الإنسانُ حيوان، قالوا: وجزء الماهِيَّةِ إِنْ أخذَ بشرط لا شيء؛ أي: بشرط ا أنْ لا يكون معه زيادة مشخّصة، لا يكون محمولاً , وإِنْ أُخِذَ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ هُوَ ; أي: مِن غيرِ التِفَاتٍ إلى أنْ يكونَ مَعَهُ شيءٌ أو لا يكونُ; كانَ محمولاً، فَقَولُهُ: (بحسب اختلافِ اعتبارِهِ بِشرط لا شيء) المنافي لِلحَمْلِ وَلَا بشرط شيءٍ) المصحّحُ له ; راجع للجزء الذهني، وأمَّا الجزء الخارجي; فَلَا يحملُ عَلَى مَا حَقَّقَ في مَوضِعِهِ مِنْ كُتُبِ الحكمة والكلام المبسوطة، فإنَّ هذه المسألة شهيرة أطالوا فيها الكلام، وقد ذكرَهَا السَّيِّدُ في كثيرٍ مِنْ مُؤلَّفاتِهِ وَلَوْ كانَ بينَهُمَا ) أي : الماهية
And as for their statement in defining Zayd: 'He is a rational animal', it is a definition of his mental essence, not the real one. Al-‘Attar: Zayd: If things were not obtained by themselves in the external world, they would not be obtained by themselves in the mind, and the essentials would not be realized in both existences. (End quote) Its basis is what preceded: that the verification is that what is obtained in the mind is the very essence existing externally, not its phantom or likeness. So it appears that the part of the existent is existent. (Thus) the objection from the later scholars who deny the existence of the natural universal in the external world against the earlier scholars who affirm that—which the leading authorities accepted—is repelled. And that objection is: If it is intended that it is a part of it in the external world, it is denied; rather it is the very matter of dispute. And if it is intended that it is a part of it in the essence, we do not grant that the mental part of the external existent must exist externally. And the reason for that repulsion is apparent, because the part is that by which the thing is constituted and has no relation to the external or the mind; rather the essence is constituted by it without regard to existence or non-existence. Yes, it divides into an external part which is not predicated, like wood for the bed and the wall for the house; so one does not say 'The house is a wall', because predication requires unity in existence, and the external part of a thing has an existence prior to it externally, so it has an existence distinct from the existence of the composite due to its priority over it. If it had another existence with the composite, it would then have two existences, which is impossible. And a mental part is predicated, as in our saying 'Man is an animal'. They said: If the part of the essence is taken as conditioned by not something, i.e. conditioned that no distinctive addition be with it, it is not predicated. And if it is taken as it is in itself, i.e. without attention to whether something is with it or not, it is predicated. So his statement: 'According to the difference of its consideration: conditioned by not something, which prevents predication, and unconditioned, which makes it valid' refers to the mental part. As for the external part, it is not predicated, as has been verified in its place in the expanded books of wisdom and theology. This issue is famous; they prolonged speech about it, and al-Sayyid mentioned it in many of his works. And if between them, i.e. the essence
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage opens with a clarification about the definition of an individual like Zayd as 'rational animal'. The author states that such a definition captures the mental essence (al-mahiyya al-i‘tibariyya), not the real essence (al-haqiqa) that exists externally. Al-‘Attar then adds a note from someone named Zayd (possibly a scholar) arguing that if things were not obtained by themselves in the external world, they would not be obtained in the mind either, and the essential properties (dhatiyyat) would not be realized in both realms. The basis is that what exists in the mind is the very essence existing externally, not a mere likeness. From this, it becomes apparent that a part of an existing thing is itself existent. This serves to repel an objection made by later scholars (al-muta’akhkhirin) who deny the existence of the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi‘i) in the external world, against the earlier scholars (al-mutaqaddimin) who affirm it. The objection is: if the part is claimed to be part of the thing in the external world, it is denied because that is the very point of contention. If it is claimed to be part in the essence (i.e., a mental part), the later scholars argue that a mental part of an external existent need not exist externally. The repudiation is straightforward: a part is that by which a thing is constituted, and it has no inherent connection to external existence or mental existence; it constitutes the essence without regard to existence or non-existence. However, the author then explains that the part divides into two types: an external part (juz’ kharji) which is not predicable, such as wood for a bed or a wall for a house—one does not say 'the house is a wall' because predication requires unity in existence, and the external part has its own distinct prior existence, so it cannot be identical with the composite. A mental part (juz’ dhihni) is predicable, as in 'Man is an animal'. The discussion continues with conditions for predication: if the part is taken as conditioned by not something (bi-shart la shay’), i.e., with no additional specifications, it is not predicated; but if taken as unconditioned (la bi-shart shay’), i.e., without attention to any addition, it is predicated. This applies to the mental part. The external part, on the other hand, is never predicated, as established in works of philosophy and theology. The author notes that this issue is well-known and al-Sayyid (likely al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani) discussed it extensively.

Key Terms
الجزء
الكلي الطبيعي
بشرط لا شيء
الجزء الخارجي
الجزء الذهني

Checkpoint
Why is it incorrect to say 'The house is a wall' according to the passage?
A) Because a wall is not a part of the house.
B) Because predication requires unity in existence, but the wall has a prior distinct existence.
C) Because the term 'house' is more general than 'wall'.
D) Because the part is mental, not external.

Key Takeaway
The part as such is neutral to existence; it divides into external (unpredicable) and mental (predicable under certain conditions), and this distinction clarifies long-standing debates about the natural universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a definition from a fiqh manual, e.g., 'Sale is the exchange of property for property with mutual consent.' Identify which parts are external (the physical property) and which are mental (the concept of exchange). Explain why the mental part is predicable of specific sale transactions.
?
Consider the argument: 'Alcohol is prohibited because it is an intoxicant. Every intoxicant is prohibited.' If 'intoxicant' is taken as a mental part of alcohol (the essence of intoxication), is it correctly predicated? Validate using the conditions in this lesson.

Proof of Non-Existence of Universal Natural and Derivation of Universals
Author argues against external existence of universal natural and explains derivation of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the argument that the universal natural does not exist externally and distinguish between essential and accidental properties extracted from simple essences.

Core Idea
Universals are mental abstractions from particular existents; they are not real external entities.

In this passage, the author presents a proof that the universal natural (al-kulli al-tabi'i) has no external reality. He then explains how the intellect extracts universal concepts from simple external entities (hawiyyat) and distinguishes between essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) attributes. This distinction is foundational for understanding how definitions and descriptions work in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Locate the sentence where the author states that the universal natural has no existence. It begins with 'ومما يدل على أنه لا وجود للكلي الطبيعي'.
The claim is that the universal natural is not a concrete external entity.
2
Examine the proof
The reason given: the universal natural is among the extensions of the logical universal, which logicians have already denied external existence. Understand why this inference is valid: if the logical universal (a mental concept) does not exist externally, then its extension (the universal natural) cannot either.
The proof relies on the negative consensus of logicians.
3
Understand the extraction process
The intellect takes simple external realities (hawiyyat) and extracts universal concepts by noting commonalities (like animality) and differences (like rationality). These universals include genus, differentia, and other properties.
Hawiyyat are particular, simple, external entities.
4
Distinguish essential and accidental
According to the text, what is extracted from the essence itself is called essential (dhati) and constitutes a part; what is extracted by noting an external matter is called accidental ('aradi). Find the sentence that clarifies this.
Essential properties are part of the quiddity; accidental properties depend on something external.
5
Apply the distinction to existence
Existence is given as an example of an accidental attribute: it is extracted by the intellect when it observes that the essence entails effects requiring a cause. This leads to the Illuminationist view that existence is a mental consideration.
Existence is not a real attribute but a mental abstraction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِمَّا يدلُّ على أنَّهُ لَا وجودَ لِلكُلِّيِّ الطبيعي ؛ وجودَ لِلكُلِّيّ الطبيعي ؛ أَنَّهُ مِن ما صدقاتِ الكُلِّي المنطقي، وقد قالُوا بعدم وجودِهِ كَمَا قالَ الشَّارح، ومِمَّا ينبغي التنبه له أنَّ الماهية العطار أنَّ الأشخاص لأنَّ القولَ يتعدَّى بِنَفْسِهِ لِلجملةِ، وَلَا داعي للتّضمينِ (هويَّاتٌ) جمعُ هويَّة؛ نسبةً ل: هوَ، يستعمل في الحقيقة الجزئية الخارجة (بسيطة) أي : لا تَركيب فيها (في الخارج) وإنْ عرضَ لَهَا التَّركيب في التَّعقُلِ فَإِنَّهُ (ينتزعُ العقلُ منها بحسب) اعتبارِ (المشاركاتِ) في الجزء الأعم كالحيوانيَّة (والمباينات) بسبب الفصول كَالنَّاطقيَّةِ والصَّاهليَّةِ أموراً كُلَّيَّةً) هي : الجنس والفصل وغيرُهُمَا مِنَ الخواص، فَهذهِ الكُلِّيَّاتُ كُلُّهَا مُنتزعةٌ مِنَ الهويَّاتِ البسيطة (إلَّا أَنَّ) الفرق بينَ الذَّاتيّ والعَرَضِيّ ؛ أَنَّ (مَا يُنتزَعُ مِن ذواتِهَا يُسمَّى جزئيا ) أي : جزءاً لها (وذاتيّا) منها (وَمَا يُنتزَعُ عنها أي : عن تلك الأشخاص أو الهويَّاتِ البسيطة بملاحظةِ أمر خارج ) كانتزاع الضّحكِ بواسطة إدراك الأمور الغريبة مثلاً (يُسمَّى عرضيَّا)؛ لِعروضه للذَّاتِ واستنادِهِ لأَمْرٍ خارج كالوجودِ فَإِنَّهُ) عرض للماهية (يُنتزع عنها بِملاحظةِ تَرتُبِ الآثار المطلوبة مِنَ الشَّيء) المقتضية لوجودِهِ على مَا قالَ الإشراقيُونَ : إِنَّ الماهيَّةَ هي الأثر المترتب على تأثيرِ الفاعل ومعنى التَّأثير : الاستتباعُ، ثمَّ العقلُ ينتزعُ منها الوجود ويصفُهَا مثلاً : ماهيَّةُ زيدٍ تَستتبع الفاعل في الخارج، ثمَّ يصفُهَا العقل بالوجودِ، والوجود ليس إلا اعتباراً عقليًّا انتزاعيَّا، كَمَا أَنَّهُ يحصلُ مِنَ الشَّمسِ أثرٌ في مُقابلها مِنَ الضَّوء المخصوص، وليسَ هَهُنَا ضَوءٌ مُستقر ثابت في نفسِهِ تجعلُهُ الشَّمسُ مُتَّصِفاً بالوجودِ ، لكنَّ العقل يعتبر الوجود ويصفُهَا بِهِ فيقولُ : وُجِدَ الضَّوءُ بِسبب الشَّمسِ
And among the things that indicate that the universal natural has no existence, (the universal natural) is that it is among the extensions of the logical universal, and they have said that it does not exist as the commentator said, and among the things that should be attended to is that the essence... al-Atar: that the individuals because the verb passes by itself to the sentence, and there is no need for inclusion. (Hawiyyat) plural of huwiyya, relative to 'huwa', used for the particular external reality, (simple) i.e., no composition in (the external), and if composition occurs to it in intellection, then (the intellect extracts from it according to) the consideration of (participations) in the more common part like animality (and distinctions) due to differentia like rationality and whinnying, universal things) which are genus, differentia, and other properties. So all these universals are extracted from the simple entities (except that) the difference between essential and accidental is that (what is extracted from its essences is called partial) i.e., a part of it (and essential) from it, (and what is extracted from them, i.e., from those individuals or simple entities by noting an external matter) like the extraction of laughter by means of perceiving strange things for example (is called accidental); because it is accidental to the essence and is based on an external matter like existence, for it is) accidental to the essence (extracted from it by noting the ordering of the desired effects from the thing) which necessitate its existence according to what the illuminationists said: that the essence is the effect consequent upon the agent's influence, and the meaning of influence is entailment, then the intellect extracts from it existence and describes it. For example: Zayd's essence entails the agent externally, then the intellect describes it with existence, and existence is nothing but an intellectual intentional abstraction, just as from the sun an effect occurs in its opposite of particular light, and here there is no stable light subsisting in itself that the sun makes it qualified with existence, but the intellect considers existence and describes it with it, saying: light came into existence due to the sun.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining two main points: first, the proof that the universal natural does not exist externally, and second, how universals are derived from simple essences. The author begins by stating that one indicator that the universal natural lacks external existence is that it is among the extensions of the logical universal, which has already been denied existence by logicians. Then he shifts to a discussion of simple entities (hawiyyat), which are particular external realities with no composition in the external world. The intellect, when considering these simple entities, extracts universal concepts by noting either commonalities (like animality) or differences (like rationality). These extracted universals include genus, differentia, and other properties. The commentary then distinguishes between essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi): what is extracted from the essence itself is called essential and constitutes a part of the essence; what is extracted by noting an external matter is called accidental. Existence is given as an example of an accidental attribute; it is extracted by the intellect when it observes that the essence entails effects requiring a cause. This leads to a discussion of the Illuminationist view that essence is the effect of the agent, and existence is merely an intellectual consideration, like light coming from the sun. The lesson emphasizes that universals are not real external entities but mental abstractions.

هُويَّة
ذاتي
عَرَضِي

Key Takeaway
The universal natural is a mental abstraction, not an external reality; universals are derived by the intellect from particular entities, and essential attributes come from the essence itself, while accidental ones require external consideration.
