Refuting Objections Against the Natural Universal
Commentator refutes two objections against the existence of the natural universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical exchange on the existence of the natural universal and evaluate the strength of the refutations.

Core Idea
The universal exists externally as a common meaning, not as a separate sensible object.

In this passage, the commentator defends the existence of the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi'i) against two powerful objections. You will learn how he resolves the dilemma of one existence subsisting in two things and counters the claim that every external existent is individuated. This dialectical exercise sharpens your ability to distinguish valid from invalid objections in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Locate the two objections in the Arabic: the first is a dilemma (either with individual existence or a different existence), and the second is that every external existent is individuated. Understand why each objection is meant to disprove the universal's external existence.
2
Analyze the first refutation
Note that the commentator chooses the first horn (existence with the individual) and admits one existence subsisting in two things. He then denies the impossibility, limiting it to existent accidents only. Reflect on why existence itself can be considered a conceptual matter.
The support from Hikmat al-Ayn shows that absolute animal only exists when restricted, so its existence is the same as the restricted animal's.
3
Analyze the second refutation
See how the commentator dismisses the second objection as an 'imaginary judgment' (hukm wahmi) – a judgment by the intellect mixed with imagination. Understand that the pure intellect can grasp the common meaning beyond individuated sensible objects.
Al-Dawwani's expansion from Ibn Sina emphasizes that the common meaning (e.g., 'human') is applied with one meaning to individuals, and it is not a separate sensible thing.
4
Evaluate the dialectical structure
Map out the objection-and-answer pattern (naqd wa jawab). Check whether each refutation directly addresses the objection and whether the authorities cited provide sufficient backing.
Both refutations accept part of the objection but deny the logical consequence, using distinctions (accident vs. conceptual, pure intellect vs. mixed with imagination).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات
الدسوقي
التى تتحقَّقُ في الأفراد على القولِ الأَوَّلِ هي الماهيَّةُ لا بشرط شيء، أما الماهية بشرط لا شيء، فهو الكُلِّي مِن حيثُ كُلِّيَّته، وهذا لا يحتوي عليه الفرد والماهية بشرط شيء جنس الأفراد
العطار
العلَّةُ نَفْسُهَا (فَ) يُعَلَّلُ التَّشخُصُ (بموادّها ) أي : ذاتيّاتِها (أو أعراض تكشفُ لها) وهو الواقع، وحينئذ ؛ يلزمُ وجود الماهية خارجاً فإنَّ الاحتياج في الاتّصافِ بالتَّشخُصِ إلى العلَّةِ يقتضي أن يكون الانصافُ بِهِ خارجيا هو أي: الاتصافُ الخارجي (يقتضي وجود الموصوفِ في الخارج مَّ أَشارَ لِدفع ما أورده أيضاً على القول بوجودِ الكُلّيّ الطَّبيعيّ خَارجاً بِقَوْلِهِ : وَلَا غبار على هذا المطلب إلا ما قالُوا مِنْ أَنَّهُ لَوْ كانَ مَوجوداً ؛ فإمَّا بوجودِ الفردِ فَيلزمُ قيام وجودٍ واحدٍ بأمرَيْنِ) إذ الفرضُ أنَّ الوجودَ لِلفردِ والماهية في ضمنِهِ، فَهُمَا مَوجودانِ بوجودِ واحد وفي حاشية مير زاجان على شرح حكمة العينِ ؛ أنَّ ذلك الوجود الواحدَ إِنْ قامَ بِكُلِّ واحدٍ مِنَ الماهيَّتَيْنِ ؛ كانَ في قوَّةِ قيام العرض الواحدِ بِمَحلَّيْنِ، وإنْ قامَ بِالمجموع؛ لزمَ وجودُ الكُلِّ بِدونِ أجزائِهِ، وإنْ قامَ بأحدِهِمَا ؛ لم يكن الموجود إلا ذلك الواحد .. هـ. (أو بوجودِ مُغاير لهُ فَلَا يصح الحملُ ؛ لأنَّ الحمل يقتضي الاتحاد في الوجودِ (و) يرد أيضاً أنَّ كُلَّ مَوجودٍ في الخارج فَهُوَ مُتَشخّصُ بِالبديهة، وهذا) أيْ: مَا ذكرَ مِنَ الأمور الموردة هو الذي قادَهُم وجَرَّهُم (إلى الحُكْمِ بِامتناع وُجودِهِ) أي : الكُلِّيِّ الطَّبيعي، وقدْ أُجيب عَنِ الأوَّلِ) وَهُوَ قَولُهُ: لَوْ كَانَ مَوجوداً ؛ فإما بوجود الفرد أو بوجودِ مُغَايرٍ لهُ بِمَا لا يحتملُ المقام إيراده) وحاصلُ ما أُجيب به اختيارُ الشَّقِّ الأوَّلِ، وتسليم لزومِ قيام الوجودِ الواحد بأمرين، فإنَّ قيامَ الشَّيء الواحدِ بِأمرين؛ إنَّما ثبتَتْ مُحاليَّتُهُ في العرض الموجود؛ لا الأمور الاعتبارية الانتزاعيَّةِ والوجود منها ، والأدلة التي أوردها على الامتناع إنَّما تَمَّتْ في بطلانِ قيام الأعراض الموجودة، ويؤيِّدُ مَا قُلْنَا قول صاحبِ حكمة العين: الحيوان المطلق لا يدخل في الوجودِ إلَّا بعدَ تَقييدِهِ بقيدٍ ؛ فإنَّهُ مَا لم يصر ناطقاً أو صَهَّالاً أو غيرهما من الفصول؛ لا يمكن دخوله في الوجودِ ، ومَنْ مَنَعَ ذلك؛ فقد كابر عقله، فإذن: . الوجودُ لا يعرضُ إِلَّا للحيوان المركَّب، فالحيوانُ النَّاطِقُ وَإِنْ كَانَ مُركَباً الماهية؛ لكن وجوده بعينه هو وجود الحيوان .ا.هـ. والثاني وهو قَولُهُ: وأنَّ كُلَّ مَوجودٍ في الخارج فَهُوَ مُتشخّص بأنَّهُ حكم وهمي ؛ أي : حَكَمَ بِهِ العقلُ مَشوباً بمخالطة الوهم، فإنَّ الحاكم في الأحكام الكاذبة هو العقل المشوب بالوهم دونَ العقل المجرَّدِ، فَإِنَّهُ إذا تجرَّدَ عَن مُخالطة الوهم ؛ كانت أحكامه صادقةٌ (كيفَ لَا) يكون مُحُكْماً وهميا (والتفتيش المذكورُ) سَابِقاً بِقَولِهِ : لا شَكٍّ أَنَّ بعض الأشخاص ... إلخ (ساق إلى وجودِ الأمرِ المشتركِ وإلى ما ذكرنَا مِنَ التَّحقيقِ؟ أشارَ الشَّيخُ الرَّئيسُ في الإشاراتِ بِقَولِهِ : تَنبيه ؛ قد يغلب على أوهام النَّاسِ أَنَّ الموجود هو المحسوس وأنَّ مَا لَا ينالُهُ الحسُّ بِجوهرِهِ، فَفَرضُ وجودِهِ مُحالٌ ... إلخ) وإلى هنا انتهى كلام عبد الحكيم، وما نقلَهُ عَنِ الإشاراتِ ذكرَهُ الدَّوَّانِيُّ مُتمِّماً ؛ فقالَ بعد قولِهِ : فَفَرضُ وجودِهِ مُحالٌ : وأَنَّ مَا لَا يتخصَّصُ بمكانٍ أو وضع بذاتِهِ أو بسببٍ ما ؛ هو فيه كأحوال الجسم، فَلَا حَظَّ لَهُ مِنَ الموجوداتِ، وأنتَ يتأتَّى لكَ أنْ تتأمل نفس المحسوس ؛ فَتعلم منه بطلان قولِ هَؤلاء ؛ لأنَّكَ ومَنْ يَستَحِقُّ أَنْ يُخَاطَبَ تَعلَمَانِ أنَّ هذهِ المحسوسات قد يقعُ عليها اسم واحد لا على الاشتراكِ الصَّرفِ ؛ بل بحسب معنى واحدٍ مثل اسم الإنسان، فإنَّكُمَا لا تشكَّانِ في أَنَّ وقوعَهُ على زيدٍ و عمرو بمعنى واحد ،موجود، فذلك الموجود لا يخلو إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ بحيثُ ينالُهُ الحسُّ أو لا يكون، فإن كانَ بَعيداً مِنْ أنْ ينالَهُ الحسُّ؛ فقد أخرجَ النَّفْسَ مِنَ المحسوسات ما ليس بمحسوس، وهذا عجيب، وإنْ كانَ مَحسوساً ؛ فَلَا محالة لهُ وضع وأين ومقدار معيَّن وكيفٌ مُتَعيّن لا يتأنَّى أن يحسّ ، بل ولا أنْ يتخيَّل إلا كذلكَ، فإنَّ كُلَّ مَحسوس وكُلَّ مُتخيَّل فإِنَّهُ يتخصَّص لا محالة بشيءٍ مِن هذهِ الأحوال، وإذا كانَ كَذلِكَ ؛ لم يكن مُلائِمَا لِمَا ليسَ بِتِلْكَ الحالةِ، فَلَمْ يكن مقولاً
Section: On the Principles of Conceptions. al-Dasuqi: That which is actualized in individuals according to the first statement is the quiddity without any condition. As for the quiddity with the condition of not being anything, it is the universal insofar as it is universal, and the individual does not contain this. The quiddity with the condition of being something is the genus of individuals. al-Attar: The cause itself, so individuation is caused by its matters, i.e., its essentials, or accidents that disclose it, and that is the reality. Then, the existence of the quiddity externally follows, for the need in being qualified by individuation to a cause necessitates that the qualification be external. That is, the external qualification necessitates the existence of the described thing in the external. Then he indicated to repel what was also objected regarding the statement of the existence of the natural universal externally by his statement: There is no dust on this point except what they said: If it existed, then either it is by the existence of the individual, so it necessitates the subsistence of one existence with two things, for the hypothesis is that existence is for the individual and the quiddity is within it, so they both exist with one existence. And in the gloss of Mir Zajan on Sharh Hikmat al-Ayn: that one existence, if it subsists in each of the two quiddities, it would have the potential of the subsistence of one accident in two loci, and if it subsists in the composite, it necessitates the existence of the whole without its parts, and if it subsists in one of them, the existent would only be that one. (Or by an existence different from it, then predication is not valid, because predication necessitates unity in existence.) And it is also objected that every existent in the external is individuated by intuition, and this is what led them to the judgment of the impossibility of its existence, i.e., the natural universal. And the answer regarding the first, i.e., his statement: If it existed, then either by the existence of the individual or by an existence different from it, with something whose mention the context does not bear. The summary of what is answered is the choice of the first alternative, and the admission of the necessity of one existence subsisting in two things, for the impossibility of a single thing subsisting in two things is only established in the existent accident, not in matters of consideration and abstraction, and existence is among them. And the proofs he brought against impossibility are only complete in the invalidity of the subsistence of existent accidents. And what supports our statement is the saying of the author of Hikmat al-Ayn: The absolute animal does not enter into existence except after its restriction by a restriction; for as long as it does not become rational or neighing or other differentiae, it cannot enter into existence, and whoever denies that has violated his intellect. Therefore, existence only accrues to the composite animal, so the rational animal, even if it is composite in quiddity, its existence itself is the existence of the animal. End. And the second is his statement: That every existent in the external is individuated, that it is an imaginary judgment, i.e., the intellect judges it mixed with the imagination, for the judge in false judgments is the intellect mixed with imagination, not the pure intellect, for when it is purified from mixing with imagination, its judgments are true. How could it not be an imaginary judgment, while the aforementioned investigation, by his statement: 'There is no doubt that some individuals... etc.' led to the existence of the common thing and to what we mentioned of verification? The chief master indicated in al-Isharat by his statement: A notice: It often dominates the imaginations of people that the existent is the sensible and that what the sense does not perceive by its essence, its existence is impossible... etc. And up to here ends the speech of Abd al-Hakim. And what he transmitted from al-Isharat, al-Dawwani mentioned as a supplement; he said after his statement: 'Its existence is impossible': And that which is not specified by a place or a position by itself or by a cause, it is like the states of the body, so it has no share of existents. And you can reflect on the sensible itself and thereby learn the falsity of these people's statement, because you and whoever deserves to be addressed know that these sensibles may be called by one name not with pure equivocation, but according to one meaning, like the name 'human', for you both do not doubt that its application to Zayd and Amr with one meaning is existent. That existent is either such that sense perceives it or not. If it is far from being perceived by sense, then you have removed from the sensibles what is not sensible, and this is strange. And if it is sensible, then inevitably it has a position, a location, a definite magnitude, and a definite quality; it cannot be sensed, nor even imagined, except in that way. For every sensible and every imagined is specified inevitably by something of these states. And if that is the case, it is not suitable for what is not in that state, so it would not be predicated.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical defense of the existence of the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi'i) in external reality. The commentator, al-Attar, begins by stating the general principle that individuation requires a cause, which implies the external existence of the quiddity. He then addresses two objections raised against the claim that the universal exists externally.

The first objection is a dilemma: if the universal existed, it would have to exist either with the existence of the individual or with an existence different from it. The first horn leads to one existence subsisting in two things (the individual and the universal), which is impossible for accidents. The second horn makes predication impossible because predication requires unity of existence. The answer selects the first horn and denies the impossibility in this case: the impossibility of one thing subsisting in two is only for existent accidents, not for conceptual or abstract matters like existence itself. The author of Hikmat al-Ayn supports this by noting that absolute animal only enters existence after being restricted, so the animal's existence is the same as the restricted animal's existence.

The second objection is that every external existent is necessarily individuated, so the universal cannot exist. The answer calls this an 'imaginary judgment'—it is the intellect mixed with imagination that judges so, not the pure intellect. The investigation of commonality leads to the existence of a shared meaning. Al-Dawwani expands with a quote from Ibn Sina's Isharat, arguing that the sensible is not the only existent; the common meaning like 'human' is applied to individuals with one meaning, and it is not a separate sensible object. If it were sensible, it would have definite position and magnitude, but then it could not be predicated of multiple individuals.

The structure follows the classical pattern of objection (naqd) and answer (jawab), with support from earlier authorities.

Key Terms
الكلي الطبيعي
الوجود الواحد بأمرين
حكم وهمي

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the first objection (dilemma of existence with the individual or different existence) fail?
A) Because the universal cannot exist at all.
B) Because the impossibility of one thing subsisting in two is only for existent accidents, not for conceptual matters.
C) Because the universal exists in the mind only.
D) Because individual existence is the only kind of existence.

Key Takeaway
The universal's external existence is defended by distinguishing between conceptual and accidental existence and by recognizing that the pure intellect grasps common meanings beyond individuation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar dialectical exchange on the issue: 'Does the ruling of obligation (wujub) exist as a universal in external acts?' State one objection and then provide a refutation using the commentator's method of distinguishing between accident and conceptual existence.
?

The Five Universals
intermediate
10 min
Why the Natural Universal Is Included
Commentators justify inclusion of natural universal and exclusion of logical and mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the natural, logical, and mental universals and explain why only the natural universal is explicitly discussed in logic textbooks.

Core Idea
The natural universal clarifies the examples; the logical is self-evident; the mental is too obscure.

In the study of universals, Islamic logicians distinguish three types: the natural, the logical, and the mental. The author of Tahdhib al-Mantiq includes only the natural universal in his discussion. This lesson unpacks the reasons behind that choice, drawing on the commentaries of al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three universals
Find in the Arabic the terms for natural (al-tabi'i), logical (al-mantiqi), and mental (al-'aqli). Understand what each signifies: the natural exists externally as a common nature; the logical is a concept predicated of many; the mental is a mental concept.
These divisions are standard in Islamic logic.
2
Understand the objection
Notice the objection: even the natural universal belongs to divine philosophy (al-ilm al-ilahi), not to logic. Why would the author include it? The commentators note this objection.
The natural universal's existence is a metaphysical question, but it is brought into logic for pedagogical clarity.
3
Analyze the response
Al-Dasuqi justifies inclusion because the natural universal clarifies the examples (like 'animal', 'human') used for the logical universal. Without concrete examples, the logical universal would remain abstract.
The author prioritizes teaching effectiveness over strict disciplinary boundaries.
4
Explain the omissions
The logical universal is omitted because examples already clarify it; the mental universal is omitted due to its extreme obscurity (ghumud). Al-'Attar notes that investigating existence is common to all universals, so the choice is pragmatic.
Thus the author's selection is based on utility and clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات
فلهذا تَرَكَ البحث عن وجودهما .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : فَلِهَذَا ) أي : فَلِأجل أنَّ البحث عن وجودِهِما خارج؛ ترك المصنِّفُ البحث عن وجودِهِمَا، وتعرَّضَ لوجودِ الطَّبيعي ؛ لتعلقِ الغرض به؛ لأنَّه يوصل للمجهولِ التَّصوريِّ؛ لأنَّهُ يكونُ جنساً ونوعاً، وفصلاً .
وقد يُقالُ : إِنَّ البحث عن وجودِ الطَّبيعي أيضاً خارج عن الصناعة؛ لأنَّه من مسائل الحكمة الإلهية الباحثة عن أحوال الموجوداتِ مِن حيثُ إِنَّها موجودة، فَالأَظهَرُ أَنْ يُقالَ : إِنَّهُ بَيَّنَ وجودَ الطَّبيعي؛ لأنَّ فيه توضيحاً للأمثلةِ الَّتي مثلُوا بها لِلكُلِّي المنطقي؛ كَحيوان وإنسان وناطق وضاحك وماشي. وهذا يُسوّغ البحث عنه في كتب الفنّ، وترك البحث عن وجودِ المنطقي؛ معَ أنَّ فيه توضيحاً لمفهوم المنطقي (۱)؛ لأنَّ العادة هي التوضيح بالأمثلة، وترك البحث عن وجودِ العقليّ؛ لمزيدِ غموضه .
(قَوْلُهُ : البَحْثِ عَنْ وجُودِهِمَا ) أي : في الخارج.
العطار
فَالبحث عنهما مِن مَسائل الحكمة الإلهيَّةِ الباحثةِ عَنْ أحوال الموجودِ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ مَوجودٌ، وَهَذا مُشتَرَك بينَهُما وبينَ الكَليّ الطبيعيّ، فَلَا وجه لإيرادِهِ وإحالتهما على علم آخر ا.هـ. وهذا الإشكال نقله المحشّي وتكلَّفَ في جوابِهِ كَتَكَلُّفِ البعضِ الآخرِ.
(١) (قَوْلُهُ : (المنطقي كذا بالنسخة التي بأيدينا، والصَّواب : الطبيعي، ووجه توضيح الأوّل للثاني أنه عارض والعارض يوضح المعروض، وقوله : لأن العادة ... إلخ؛ أي: والأمثلة للطبيعي لا للمنطقي، فلذا بحثوا عن وجوده دون المنطقي . . . هـ . الشَّرنوبي
Section: On the principles of concepts.
Thus he left off the investigation of their existence.
Al-Dasuqi:
(His statement: 'Thus') i.e., because the investigation of their existence is external, the author left off the investigation of their existence, and addressed the existence of the natural [universal] due to the relevance of the purpose; for it leads to the unknown conceptual, because it is genus, species, and differentia.
And it may be said: the investigation of the existence of the natural [universal] is also external to the art, because it is among the questions of divine philosophy which investigates the states of existents insofar as they exist. The more apparent [view] is that he clarified the existence of the natural [universal] because in that there is clarification of the examples they gave for the logical universal, such as 'animal', 'human', 'rational', 'laughing', and 'walking'. This justifies investigating it in books of the art, and leaving off investigating the existence of the logical [universal], even though in that there is clarification of the concept of the logical (1); because the custom is clarification by examples, and leaving off investigating the existence of the mental [universal] due to its greater obscurity.
(His statement: 'The investigation of their existence') i.e., in external reality.
Al-'Attar:
The investigation of them is among the questions of divine philosophy which investigates the states of the existent insofar as it exists, and this is common between them and the natural universal. So there is no justification for raising it and referring them to another science. End. This objection was transmitted by the commentator and he strained in his answer, like the straining of some others.
(1) (His statement: 'logical' — as in the copy we have, but the correct [reading] is 'natural'; the clarification of the first for the second is that it is an accident, and the accident clarifies the substance. And his statement: 'because the custom...' etc. i.e., the examples are for the natural, not the logical. So they investigated its existence excluding the logical. End. [from] al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining why the author of the matn (likely Tahdhib al-Mantiq) discussed the existence of the 'natural universal' (al-kulli al-tabi'i) but omitted discussion of the 'logical universal' (al-mantiqi) and the 'mental universal' (al-'aqli). Al-Dasuqi begins by glossing the author's phrase 'Thus he left off the investigation of their existence' (i.e., of the logical and mental universals). He explains that the author only dealt with the natural universal because it serves the purpose of leading to unknown concepts, as it can function as genus, species, and differentia. Then an objection is raised: even the natural universal belongs to metaphysics (al-ilm al-ilahi), not logic. The response is that the author clarified the natural universal only because it helps to explain the examples used for the logical universal, such as 'animal', 'human', etc. This justifies its inclusion in logic. The logical universal itself is omitted because customarily one clarifies through examples, not through the abstract concept itself. The mental universal is omitted due to its extreme obscurity. Al-Dasuqi then clarifies that 'the investigation of their existence' refers to external existence. Al-'Attar adds that investigating existence is metaphysics, so there is no need to discuss it in logic. He notes that this objection and answer have been strained by some commentators. The footnote from al-Sharnubi corrects the reading: where the text says 'logical' it should be 'natural', since the accident clarifies the substance. All examples are for the natural universal, not the logical.

Key Terms
الطبيعي (al-tabi'i)
المنطقي (al-mantiqi)
العقلي (al-'aqli)
مبادئ التصورات (mabadi' al-tasawwurat)
الكلي (al-kulli)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason the author included the natural universal but excluded the logical and mental universals?
A) The natural universal alone exists externally.
B) The natural universal provides concrete examples that clarify the logical universal, while the logical is clarified by those examples and the mental is too obscure.
C) The logical and mental universals are not real universals.
D) The author was only concerned with divine philosophy.

Key Takeaway
The natural universal is included in logic textbooks because it provides concrete examples for the logical universal, while the logical and mental are omitted due to self-evidence and obscurity respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which type of universal (natural, logical, or mental) each of the following refers to: (a) 'humanity' as the common nature shared by all humans, (b) 'human' as a concept that can be predicated of Zayd, Amr, and Khalid, (c) 'human' as a mental image in your mind. Explain your reasoning based on the lesson.
?
In a fiqh text, a scholar says: 'The prohibition of wine is due to its intoxication – and intoxication is a state that renders the intellect absent.' Determine whether 'intoxication' here is being treated as a natural universal or a logical universal, and justify your answer using the criteria from the lesson.

Why Negatives Are Called Categorical
Author explains the classification and naming of negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why negative categorical propositions are still called 'hamliyya' by tracing the reasoning of al-Sayyid al-Jurjani and al-Isam.

Core Idea
A single transfer based on appropriateness in positives suffices to name negatives as categorical.

You already know the basic division: categorical propositions are either positive (affirming) or negative (denying). But a problem arises: negatives like 'Zayd is not standing' contain no actual predication (haml), yet they share the name 'categorical'. How can that be? This passage from the Tadhheeb sharh brings together several prominent logicians—al-Sayyid al-Jurjani, al-Isam, and others—to justify this inclusive naming. By following their reasoning, you will understand a key principle of terminological transfer in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division and the problem
The passage begins by stating that categorical propositions are either positive (affirming something) or negative (denying something). Al-Dasuqi points out that negatives have no predication (haml) yet are still called hamliyya. Why is this a problem?
2
Understand al-Sayyid's solution
Al-Sayyid al-Jurjani argues that the technical terms (categorical, conditional, disjunctive) were transferred from linguistic meanings to technical concepts based on an appropriateness (munasaba) found in some individuals—namely, the positives. This one transfer suffices to cover all individuals, so negatives do not require a separate transfer. He insists that uniformity of appropriateness is not necessary; it is enough that appropriateness exists in some.
3
Examine al-Isam's refinement
Al-Isam, in his gloss on al-Qutb, suggests that for negatives the appropriateness could be opposition (tadadd). Since opposition is a recognized basis for terminological transfer, it validates including negatives. He also refutes an objection about conditionals and disjunctives by noting that the whole can be named after a part.
4
Consider alternative explanations
Sharh al-Matali' offers other possibilities: negatives are called categorical metaphorically because they resemble positives in their terms (subject and predicate), or because they are opposites of positives, or because their parts have the potential to accept predication. These serve as additional justifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي : - إمَّا (مُوجِبَةٌ) إن حكم فيها بالثبوتِ المذكور . الدسوقي : «زيد قائم»، وأما السَّالبة؛ فليس فيها حَمْلٌ كَـ: «زيد ليس بقائم»، مع أنَّها تُسمى حملية أيضاً. العطار : لأنَّهُ جوابُ الشَّرطِ المذكور، والحمليّة نسبة للحمل ؛ لاشتمالها عليه في الجملة، فدخلت السوالب، قال السَّيِّدُ : والظَّاهِرُ أنَّهم نقلوا هذه الأسامي؛ يعني: حملية ومُتَصِلَةٌ ومُنفصلةً مِنَ المعاني اللّغويَّةِ إلى المفهومات الاصطلاحية بناءً على وجودِ المناسبة في بعض أفراد هذه المفهومات؛ أعني : الموجبات، فإنَّ هذا القدر من المناسبة كافٍ في صحةِ النَّقل ، فَلا حاجة إلى التزام النقل مرَّتَيْنِ .ا.هـ. يعني: أنَّ الاطراد في المناسبة غير لازم، فيكفي في الإطلاق على كل الأفراد وجود المناسبة في بعضِهَا وَلَا حاجة إلى القول بأنَّ إطلاق هذهِ الأسامي على السوالب لِشَبَههَا بالموجبات في الأطرافِ مثلاً، ويجعل هذا وجهاً للتّسميةِ، فَيلزمُ أَنَّهَا نُقِلَتْ عَن الموجبات إلى السوالب ؛ لتحقق هذهِ المناسبة، فيلزمُ النَّقلُ مرَّتَيْنِ، وأيضاً على تقدير نقلها إلى السوالب عن الموجبات ؛ يكون إطلاقها على الموجباتِ مهجوراً ؛ لأنَّ التَّقلَ مَشروطٌ بِهجران المعنى الأوَّلِ قالَ العصام في حاشية القُطْبِ : وَلَكَ أَنْ تعتبر مُناسبةَ السَّوالب بالتَّضاد ؛ إذْ هوَ مِنَ المناسبات المصحِّحَةِ للنَّقْلِ، لا يُقالُ : المتّصلة بمعنى مَا قامَ بِهِ الاتصال، وكذا المنفصلة بمعنى مَا قامَ بِهِ الانفصال، فلَمْ يتحقق في الموجبات أيضاً معنى الاتصال والانفصال على وجه يستدعيه إطلاق المتّصلة والمنفصلة؛ بل يتحقَّقُ في طُرُقِ المتّصلة والمنفصلة؛ لأنَّا نقول: لا بُعْدَ في تسميةِ الكُلِّ باسم جزئِهِ، فظهرَ أَنَّ التَّسميةَ في الكُلِّ مِن قبيل المنقول .ا.هـ. و شرح المطالع : أن تسمية السَّوالب بطريق المجاز لِمُشابهتِهَا إيَّاها في الأطراف، أو لِكُونِهَا مُقابلاتها، أو لأنَّ لأجزائها استعداد قبولِ الحمل والاتصال والانفصال. (قَوْلُهُ : وَهِيَ إِمَّا مُوْجِبَةٌ) أصلُ المثن : فحمليَّةٌ موجبة أو سالبةٌ، فَكِلاهما بدل، وتقديرُ الشَّارحِ هذا يقتضي أنَّ كُلَّا مِنهُما خبر مبتدأ محذوف، ويُجَابُ بِأَنَّهُ حَلُّ مَعْنَى.
And it is: either (positive) if judgment is made with the mentioned affirmation. al-Dasuqi: 'Zayd is standing', and as for the negative, there is no predication in it like 'Zayd is not standing', even though it is also called a categorical. al-Attar: because it is a response to the mentioned condition, and 'hamliyya' is a relation to predication due to its inclusion of it in some sense, so negatives are included. al-Sayyid said: apparent is that they transferred these names, i.e., categorical, conditional, and disjunctive, from linguistic meanings to technical concepts based on the existence of appropriateness in some individuals of these concepts, i.e., positives, for this amount of appropriateness suffices for the validity of transfer, so there is no need to assume transfer twice. Meaning: that uniformity in appropriateness is not necessary, so it suffices in applying to all individuals the existence of appropriateness in some of them, and there is no need to say that applying these names to negatives is due to resemblance to positives in the terms, for example, making this a basis for naming, then it would necessitate that they were transferred from positives to negatives due to this appropriateness, thus necessitating transfer twice. Also, on the assumption of transfer to negatives from positives, their application to positives would be abandoned because transfer is conditioned by abandoning the first meaning. al-Isam said in the gloss on al-Qutb: you may consider the appropriateness of negatives by opposition, for it is among the appropriate that validate transfer. It cannot be said: the conditional means that in which connection inheres, and similarly the disjunctive means that in which disconnection inheres, so the meaning of connection and disconnection is not realized in positives either in a way that requires applying 'conditional' and 'disjunctive'; rather, it is realized in the ways (types) of conditionals and disjunctives, because we say: there is no difficulty in naming the whole by the name of its part, so it appears that naming in all is of the type of transferred. And Sharh al-Matali': that naming negatives is metaphorical due to their resembling positives in the terms, or because they are their opposites, or because their parts have the potential to accept predication, connection, and disconnection. (His saying: and it is either positive) the original of the text: so a categorical is positive or negative, so both are substitutes, and the commentator's estimation implies that each is the predicate of a deleted subject, and it is answered that it is a paraphrase of meaning.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the classification of categorical propositions (حمليّة) into positive (موجبة) and negative (سالبة). The commentator begins by stating the basic division: a categorical proposition is either positive, affirming something of something, or negative, denying it. Al-Dasuqi notes that while a negative proposition like 'Zayd is not standing' does not contain a predication (حمل) in the usual sense, it is still called 'categorical'. This initiates a discussion on why negative propositions share the name 'categorical'. Al-Attar explains that the term 'categorical' relates to predication, but since negatives include predication in a general sense (because they involve a judgment of negation), they are included under the same term. Al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani elaborates that the technical terms were transferred from linguistic meanings to technical concepts based on an appropriateness (مناسبة) found in some individuals, namely the positives. This appropriateness suffices for the transfer to apply to all individuals, so negatives do not require a separate transfer. He argues against the view that negatives were named by a second transfer from positives, as that would entail two transfers and the abandonment of the original meaning for positives. Al-Isam in his gloss suggests that the appropriateness for negatives could be opposition (تضاد), which is a valid basis for transfer. He also refutes the objection that the meanings of connection and disconnection are not found in positive conditionals and disjunctives, by saying that the terms apply to the whole based on a part (e.g., the conditions of the conditional). Finally, the commentator paraphrases the original matn, explaining that the phrase 'it is either positive' implies a deleted subject: 'a categorical is either positive or negative', and both are substitutes (بدل). The passage ends by noting that the commentator's interpretation is a paraphrase of meaning.

Key Terms
حمليّة
موجبة
سالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do negative propositions share the name 'hamliyya' (categorical) with positive ones?
A) Because they contain a hidden predication that can be analyzed as a positive statement.
B) Because the term was transferred from positives to negatives based on a general appropriateness (مناسبة) found in positives, allowing it to cover both.
C) Because negatives are logically equivalent to positives by contraposition.
D) Because the term 'hamliyya' originally meant any proposition with a subject and predicate, regardless of truth value.

Key Takeaway
Negatives are called categorical because the term was transferred once based on appropriateness found in positives, without requiring a second transfer; opposition or resemblance may also serve as appropriateness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the negative proposition 'The world is not eternal' from a kalam argument. Explain why it is a categorical proposition (hamliyya) using al-Sayyid's principle of transfer based on appropriateness in positives.

The Five Universals

8 min
Distinguishing General and Special Possibility
Commentators define general vs special possibility by sides of necessity negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish general possibility from special possibility by identifying which sides of necessity are negated in a modal proposition.

Core Idea
General possibility negates necessity from one side; special possibility negates it from both sides.

Modal logic divides possibility into two types: general and special. The key difference lies in how necessity is negated. This lesson unpacks the commentators' explanations to give you a clear operational understanding of each type, using the example 'Man is a writer'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal term
Find the words 'الإمكان العام' (general possibility) and 'الإمكان الخاص' (special possibility) in the Arabic. These are the two types being defined.
They may appear as qualifications like 'بالامكان العام' or 'بالامكان الخاص'.
2
Extract the definition of general possibility
The passage states that general possibility 'يفيد سلب الضرورة عن الجانب المخالف فقط'. That means it negates necessity only from the side opposite to the relation expressed in the proposition.
For example, in 'Man is a writer with general possibility', necessity is negated only from the side of non-writing: it is not necessary that man is not a writer.
3
Extract the definition of special possibility
Special possibility 'يفيد سلب الضرورة عن الجانبين: الجانب الموافق والمخالف'. It negates necessity from both the agreeing side (the relation itself) and the opposing side.
So in 'Man is a writer with special possibility', it is not necessary that he is a writer, nor is it necessary that he is not a writer.
4
Compare the two definitions
General possibility leaves the relation itself possibly necessary (it does not address the agreeing side), while special possibility explicitly denies necessity on both sides, making the proposition fully contingent.
The commentators emphasize that special possibility is stricter: it removes necessity entirely.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the proposition 'The world is created'. If said with general possibility, it means 'it is not necessary that the world is not created' (i.e., creation is possible but could be necessary). With special possibility, it means 'neither creation nor non-creation is necessary' – the world is contingent.
This distinction is crucial in Kalam for proving the world's contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعْرُفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ. قَالَ الْمُحَشِّي: حَاصِلُهُ أَنَّ قَوْلَنَا بِالْإِمْكَانِ الْخَاصِّ يُفِيدُ سَلْبَ الضَّرُورَةِ عَنِ الْجَانِبَيْنِ: الْجَانِبِ الْمُخَالِفِ لِلنِّسْبَةِ الْمَذْكُورَةِ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ، وَالْجَانِبِ الْمُوَافِقِ لِلنِّسْبَةِ الْمَذْكُورَةِ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ الْمَلْفُوظَةِ؛ أَعْنِي الْجُزْءَ الْأَوَّلَ. قَالَ الْعَطَّارُ: قَدْ عَرَفْتَ أَنَّ (بِلَا ضَرُورَةِ الْجَانِبِ الْمُوَافِقِ ... إِلَخ) لَمَّا كَانَتِ الْمُمْكِنَةُ الْعَامَّةُ قَضِيَّةً حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِنِسْبَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ إِلَى الْمَوْضُوعِ مُقَيَّدَةً بِعَدَمِ ضَرُورَةِ خِلَافِهَا؛ كَانَتِ الْمُمْكِنَةُ الْخَاصَّةُ قَضِيَّةً حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِنِسْبَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ إِلَى الْمَوْضُوعِ، مُقَيَّدَةً بِعَدَمِ ضَرُورَةِ خِلَافِهَا وَعَدَمِ ضَرْوِرَةِ نَفْسِهَا مَعًا، فَقَوْلُهُ (أَيْضًا) إِشَارَةٌ إِلَى هَذَا، لَكِنْ لَوْ لَمْ يَذْكُرْهُ لَكَانَ أَظْهَرَ وَأَوْلَى. قَالَ الْمُحَشِّي: إِنَّ كَيْفِيَّةَ الْمُمْكِنَةِ الْخَاصَّةِ مُخَالِفَةٌ لِغَيْرِهَا، فَإِنَّ طَرِيقَ بَقِيَّةِ الْقَضَايَا أَنْ يُؤْتَى أَوَّلًا بِالْقَضِيَّةِ الْمُوَجَّهَةِ الْبَسِيطَةِ، ثُمَّ تُقَيَّدُ، وَهَذِهِ لَمْ يُؤْتَ فِيهَا بِجِهَةِ الْإِمْكَانِ، ثُمَّ قُيِّدَتْ لِعَدَمِ إِمْكَانِ ذَلِكَ. وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ التَّحْقِيقَ أَنَّ عَدَّ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الْعَامَّةِ مِنَ الْمُوَجَّهَاتِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ بِالْمَجَازِ كَمَا عَدُّوا السَّالِبَةَ فِي الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ وَالشَّرْطِيَّاتِ، وَأَنَّ الْمُمْكِنَةَ لَا حُكْمَ فِيهَا بِالْفِعْلِ، فَهِيَ كَالْمُطْلَقَةِ مِنْ حَيْثُ الْجِهَةُ وَإِنِ احْتَوَتِ الْفِعْلِيَةُ عَلَى الْحُكْمِ دُونَ الْمُمْكِنَةِ، قَالَ شَارِحُ الْمَطَالِعِ: الْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْفِعْلَ لَيْسَ كَيْفِيَّةً لِلنِّسْبَةِ لِأَنَّ مَعْنَاهُ لَيْسَ إِلَّا وُقُوعَ النِّسْبَةِ، وَالْكَيْفِيَّةُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ تَكُونَ أَمْرًا مُغَايِرًا لِوُقُوعِ النِّسْبَةِ الَّذِي هُوَ الْحُكْمُ، فَإِنَّ الْجِهَةَ جُزْءٌ آخَرُ لِلْقَضِيَّةِ مُغَايِرٌ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ وَالْمَحْمُولِ وَالْحُكْمِ، وَإِنَّمَا عَدُّوا الْمُطْلَقَةَ فِي الْمُوَجَّهَاتِ بِالْمَجَازِ، كَمَا عَدُّوا السَّالِبَةَ فِي الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ وَالشَّرْطِيَّاتِ، وَأَنَّهُ لَا حُكْمَ فِي الْمُمْكِنَةِ بِالْفِعْلِ؛ لِأَنَّا إِذَا قُلْنَا: الْإِنْسَانُ كَاتِبٌ بِالْإِمْكَانِ الْعَامِّ فَلَيْسَ الْحُكْمُ فِيهَا إِلَّا بِسَلْبِ الضَّرُورَةِ عَنِ الْجَانِبِ الْمُخَالِفِ، وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ فِي الْجَانِبِ الْمُوَافِقِ فَلَمْ يَتَعَرَّضْ لَهُ حَتَّى يَحْتَمِلَ أَنْ يَكُونَ بِضَرُورَةٍ أَوْ لَا. فَإِذَا قُلْنَا بِالْإِمْكَانِ الْخَاصِّ سَلَبْنَا الضَّرُورَةَ عَنِ الْجَانِبَيْنِ.
Chapter: On the possible proposition and its divisions. The commentator said: The gist is that our statement with special possibility signifies the negation of necessity from both sides: the side opposing the relation mentioned in the proposition, and the side agreeing with the relation mentioned in the uttered proposition; I mean the first part. Al-'Attar said: You have known that possibility is opposite to actuality, and that the possible proposition is not an actual judgment unlike the actual proposition; so how can one claim there is no difference between them? Reflect. Al-Dasuqi said: His statement 'without necessity of the agreeing side etc.': since the general possible proposition is a proposition in which the relation of predicate to subject is judged, qualified by the absence of necessity of its opposite; the special possible proposition is a proposition in which the relation of predicate to subject is judged, qualified by the absence of necessity of its opposite and the absence of necessity of itself together. So his statement 'also' indicates this, but if he had not mentioned it, it would have been more apparent and better. The commentator said: Indeed the modality of the special possible proposition is different from others, for the method of the rest of the propositions is that one first brings the simple modal proposition, then it is qualified, but in this one, no modality of possibility was brought, then it was qualified because that is not possible. And know that the verification is that counting the absolute universal among the modal propositions is only by metaphor, just as they counted the negative among the categoricals and conditionals, and that the possible proposition has no actual judgment in it, so it is like the absolute with respect to modality, although the actual proposition contains the judgment while the possible does not. The commentator of al-Matali' said: The truth is that actuality is not a modality of the relation because its meaning is nothing but the occurrence of the relation, and the modality must be something other than the occurrence of the relation which is the judgment, for the modality is another part of the proposition, distinct from the subject, predicate, and judgment. They only counted the absolute among the modalities metaphorically, just as they counted the negative among the categoricals and conditionals, and that there is no actual judgment in the possible proposition; because when we say 'Man is a writer with general possibility', the judgment in it is only the negation of necessity from the opposing side, and the judgment concerning the agreeing side is not addressed, so that it may possibly be with necessity or not. So when we say with special possibility, we negate necessity from both sides.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the definition and distinction between two types of possible (contingent) propositions in modal logic: العام (general) and الخاص (special). The core idea is that the special possibility negates necessity from both the side that agrees with the relation (i.e., the affirmation or negation expressed in the proposition) and the side that opposes it, whereas the general possibility only negates necessity from the opposing side. This is clarified by the commentators. Al-Dasuqi explains that the general possible proposition is a judgment with a relation of predicate to subject qualified by the absence of necessity of its opposite (i.e., the possibility of the opposite is not necessary). The special possible proposition additionally negates necessity of itself (i.e., the relation is not necessary either). Al-'Attar raises a concern: if possible propositions do not contain actual judgments (since they only state possibility), how can one conflate them with actual propositions? The commentator (al-Muhashshi) notes that the special possibility is uniquely constructed without first stating a simple modal; it is directly qualified. Then there is a verification that the absolute universal proposition (which asserts the simple actuality) is only counted among modal propositions metaphorically, because it lacks a distinct modality. The truth, from Sharh al-Matali', is that actuality is not a modality since it is the occurrence of the relation itself, not an additional quality. The modality is a separate part of the proposition. The possible proposition has no actual judgment; for example, 'Man is a writer with general possibility' only negates necessity from the opposite (i.e., it is not necessary that he is non-writer), but says nothing about the necessity of the relation itself. With special possibility, necessity is negated from both sides. This distinction is crucial in modal logic, especially in understanding the difference between contingent and necessary propositions. The commentators warn against confusing the possible with the actual and emphasize that the absolute proposition is modal only by extension.

Key Terms
الإمكان العام
الإمكان الخاص
الضرورة
الجانب الموافق
الجانب المخالف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between general possibility and special possibility in the interpretation of 'Man is a writer'?
A) General possibility negates necessity from both sides, special from one.
B) General possibility negates necessity from the opposing side only, special from both sides.
C) General possibility asserts actuality, special asserts possibility.
D) Both negate necessity from the same sides.

Key Takeaway
The core distinction between general and special possibility is the number of sides from which necessity is negated: one for general, both for special.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Four Types of Implication in Conditional Propositions
Author enumerates four types of relation between antecedent and consequent in muttasila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four types of implication (cause, effect, common cause, mutual) is present in a conditional sentence from an Islamic text.

Core Idea
Implication in conditionals is not only cause-effect; it can be effect-cause, common cause, or mutual dependence.

Conditional propositions (al-shartiyya al-muttasila) are central to logical reasoning in Islamic sciences. In this passage, the author clarifies that the relationship between the antecedent and consequent is not limited to cause and effect. He presents four distinct patterns of implication, each with concrete examples. Understanding these patterns will help you analyze arguments in kalam, fiqh, and usul al-fiqh more precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional sentence
Find the 'in' (if) construction and separate the antecedent (al-muqaddam) from the consequent (al-tālī). Write them down.
Example: 'If the sun has risen, then daytime exists.' Antecedent: 'the sun has risen'; consequent: 'daytime exists'.
2
Check if the antecedent causes the consequent
Ask: Does the occurrence of the antecedent bring about the consequent? If yes, this is the first type: antecedent is cause (علة) of consequent.
Classic example: sunrise causes daytime.
3
Check if the antecedent is an effect of the consequent
Reverse the causal direction: Does the consequent cause the antecedent? If yes, this is the second type: antecedent is effect (معلول) of consequent.
Example: 'If daytime exists, the sun has risen.' Daytime does not cause sunrise, but the reverse implication holds ontologically.
4
Check if both are effects of a common cause
If neither directly causes the other, see if they share a third cause that produces both. If so, this is the third type.
Example: 'If the sun has risen, the world is illuminated.' Both daytime and illumination are effects of sunrise.
5
Check for mutual implication (al-taḍāyuf)
If the conception of each requires the conception of the other, like father and son, then it is mutual implication. This is two-way, unlike the one-way implications above.
Example: 'If Zayd is a son of 'Amr, then 'Amr is a father of Zayd.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأنْ يكونَ المقدَّمُ عِلَّةٌ للتَّالى كَقَولِنَا : إنْ كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعةٌ ; فالنَّهارُ موجود، أو يكونَ المقدَّمُ معلولاً لهُ كَمَا في عكس المثالِ، أو يكونا معلولي عِلَّةٍ واحدةٍ كَ: إِنْ كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعةٌ فَالعالَمُ مُضيءٌ ، وَكَالتّضايف، وهو أن يكون الأمر بحيثُ يكونُ تعقُلُ كُلّ منهما بالقياس إلى تَعقُلِ الْآخَرِ كَقولِنَا : إِنْ كانَ زِيدٌ ابناً لعمرو ; فَعَمرُو أَبْ له، قال المصنِّفُ : وهذا يكون في اللُّزومِ مِنَ الطَّرِفِينِ، وأَمَّا في مجرَّدِ اللُّزومِ؛ فيكفي مُجرَّدُ الإضافةِ كَالعَمَى والبصر .ا.هـ.
Either that the antecedent is a cause for the consequent, as in our statement: 'If the sun has risen, then daytime is present'; or that the antecedent is an effect of it, as in the reverse of the example; or that both are effects of a single cause, as in: 'If the sun has risen, then the world is illuminated'; and [or] mutual implication (al-taḍāyuf), which is that the matter is such that the conception of each is relative to the conception of the other, as in our statement: 'If Zayd is a son of 'Amr, then 'Amr is a father of him.' The author said: 'This occurs in implication from both sides, but in mere implication, mere addition suffices, like blindness and sight.'
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the types of relationship between the antecedent and consequent in a conditional conjunctive proposition (al-shartiyya al-muttasila). The author enumerates four ways in which the two parts can be connected. First, the antecedent is a cause of the consequent: e.g., 'If the sun has risen, daytime exists.' Here, sunrise causes daytime. Second, the antecedent is an effect of the consequent: the reverse example, e.g., 'If daytime exists, the sun has risen.' This reverses the causal order. Third, both are effects of a single cause: e.g., 'If the sun has risen, the world is illuminated.' Both daytime and illumination are caused by the sun's rising. Fourth, mutual implication (al-taḍāyuf): the two are relative terms, like father and son; the conception of one necessarily involves the other. The author further notes that this mutual implication is a two‑way implication, whereas in mere one‑way implication it suffices to have an added relationship like blindness and sight (which are opposites but not necessarily reciprocal). The explanation uses concrete examples from natural phenomena and kinship relations to clarify the abstract logical concepts.

Key Terms
الشرطية المتصلة
المقدم
التالي
العلة
التضايف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Each is conceived only in relation to the other.
D) They are merely accidental.

Key Takeaway
Implication between antecedent and consequent can be causal, effectual, common-cause, or mutual; correctly identifying the type is crucial for evaluating logical and juristic arguments.

The Disjunctive Concept and Its Role in Particular Contradictions
Author defines the disjunctive concept and explains its adequacy for contradicting particular propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when the disjunctive concept suffices as a contradictory for a compound proposition, distinguishing between universal and particular cases.

Core Idea
The disjunctive concept works as a contradictory only when its parts are particular, not universal.

In this passage, the author defines the disjunctive concept and explains its adequacy for contradicting particular propositions. You will learn to identify cases where this concept applies and where it fails, a crucial skill for handling compound statements in Islamic logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define the disjunctive concept
The passage defines it as lifting one of the two parts without specification. Understand its truth conditions: if the original compound is true, the disjunctive concept is false; if the original is false, the disjunctive concept is true. Use an Islamic example: 'Zayd is knowledgeable and Amr is knowledgeable' – its disjunctive concept is 'Either Zayd is not knowledgeable or Amr is not knowledgeable'.
This mirrors the source's definition from Shaykh al-Islam.
2
Distinguish universal vs particular cases
When the parts are universal, the disjunctive concept is not equivalent to the categorical compound. For example, 'Every scholar is either knowledgeable about fiqh or not' is true, but 'Always either every scholar is knowledgeable about fiqh or every scholar is not' is false because some scholars are knowledgeable and some not. However, when both parts are particular, they are equivalent.
This corresponds to Al-‘Attar's point about equality.
3
Apply to a particular compound
Consider the particular compound 'Some scholars are not always reliable' (meaning sometimes reliable, sometimes not). Its contradictory is 'Every scholar is either always reliable or always unreliable' – a categorical proposition resembling a disjunctive. This is the correct form, as the disjunctive concept (a universal disjunction) would not suffice.
The passage explicitly uses the pattern 'some J is B not always'.
4
Verify the contradiction
Check that the proposed contradictory covers all cases of falsity of the original. If 'Some scholars are not always reliable' is false, then every scholar is either always reliable or always not reliable. Conversely, if the contradictory is true, the original must be false. This confirms the logical adequacy for particular propositions.
The passage explains that when the disjunctive is particular, it is equivalent and thus sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: 'The disjunctive concept' – Shaykh al-Islam said: It is the lifting of one of the two parts without specification; because if the original is true, this lifting is necessarily false, and when the original is false, this is true; because its falsity is either by the falsity of both parts together, or by the falsity of one of them determinately or indeterminately, and in all cases this lifting is realized. And his statement: 'so it is the concept... etc.' except that it should not be counted among what the discussion is about. Al-‘Attar: they are equal; because of the truth of our statement: 'Every number is either even or odd' – a disjunction that prevents both joint truth and joint falsehood – unlike when we say: 'Always either every number is even or every number is odd' – because it is possible that reality is void of both, some numbers being even and some odd. But if they are two particulars, then they are equal; for if it is true that some number is either even or odd, then it is true that either some number is even or some number is odd, and vice versa. And when this is established, we say: the compound if it is particular, like our statement: 'Some J is B not always' – its meaning is that sometimes some J is B and sometimes not B – then its contradictory is that it is not so; that is: it is not the case that some J is such that it is B sometimes and not B at other times; so every single one is either B always or not B always; because when no part is such that it is B sometimes and not B at other times, then every J is either B and never not B, or not B and never B. So the contradictory of the particular is a categorical proposition resembling a disjunctive. And since the disjunctive is not equivalent to the categorical when both are universal, the disjunctive concept between the contradictories of the two particulars – i.e., the universal disjunctive – is not sufficient for the contradictory of the particular. And where it is equivalent when it is particular, that suffices. This is what is taken from Sharh al-Matali‘, and by it and by the research we will transmit from some eminent scholars, what is in the statement of some of the glossators here, regarding the author's statement: 'but in the particular it is not sufficient... etc.', where he said: 'The disjunctive concept is a disjunction resembling the categorical' – end. (1) His statement: 'and his statement... etc.' – the final part of this expression suffices what Shaykh al-Islam said before it, and its beginning with what it contains suffices what the commentator will say: 'And applying the term contradictory to this concept' – the discussion is there – end. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a definition of the 'disjunctive concept' (al-mafhūm al-muraddad). The author begins by quoting a statement from Shaykh al-Islam, who defines it as the lifting of one of the two parts without specification. This means that if the original compound proposition (like 'Zayd is in the house or Amr is') is true, then the claim that 'either not-Zayd or not-Amr' (without specifying which) is false, because the original's truth requires both parts to be true, so denying one is false. Conversely, if the original is false, then the disjunctive concept is true because the falsity could be due to both parts being false, or one false determinately or indeterminately in all cases the disjunctive concept holds. Next, the author addresses a remark by another commentator (Al-‘Attar) about equality. He gives an example: 'Every number is either even or odd' is a true disjunction that prevents both joint truth and joint falsehood (māni‘at al-jam‘ wa al-khuluw). But if we say 'Always either every number is even or every number is odd', this is false because it could be that some numbers are even and some odd, so both universals are false. However, if both disjuncts are particular, like 'Some number is even or some number is odd', then they are equivalent to the original 'Some number is either even or odd'. He then applies this to compound propositions. Consider a particular compound like 'Some J is B not always' which means 'Sometimes some J is B and sometimes not B'. Its contradictory is 'It is not the case that some J is sometimes B and sometimes not B', which means each individual J is either always B or always not B. This is a categorical proposition (ḥamliyya) resembling a disjunctive (munfaṣila). He explains that when the parts are universal, the disjunctive is not equivalent to the categorical; hence the disjunctive concept (a universal disjunction) is not sufficient as the contradictory of a particular compound. But when the parts are particular, they are equivalent, so the disjunctive concept suffices. The author notes that this is derived from Sharh al-Matali‘, and that it reveals an error in some glossators who claim the disjunctive concept is a disjunction resembling the categorical. The lesson concludes with a footnote clarifying a textual point.

Key Terms
المفهوم المردد (al-mafhūm al-muraddad): A compound proposition that raises one of the two parts without specification, serving as a contradictory; it is a disjunction that covers all cases of falsity of the original. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable in post-Avicennan logic.]
المنفصلة (al-munfaṣila): A disjunctive proposition; here, specifically a disjunction that prevents joint truth and joint falsehood (māni‘at al-jam‘ wa al-khuluw) or similar types. [Note: In earlier logic, disjunctions were classified differently; later logicians refined types like māni‘at al-jam‘ and māni‘at al-khuluw.]
نقيض (naqīḍ): Contradictory; a proposition that is always opposite in truth value to another, covering all cases of its falsity. [Note: The concept remains consistent, but the method of construction for compound propositions is detailed here.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive concept is sufficient as a contradictory only for particular compound propositions, not universal ones, because only in the particular case is it equivalent to the required categorical form.

Simple Conversion: Definition and Proof
Author defines conversion and provides reductio proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the reductio proof that establishes the validity of simple conversion and apply it to a given proposition.

Core Idea
Simple conversion is proven by the absurdity of its denial.

The author begins the chapter on simple conversion with a concise proof. Instead of defining conversion as a rule, he demonstrates why it must hold: if the converse were false, its contradictory would be true, leading to an impossibility with the original proposition. This logical move is central to understanding immediate inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and its converse.
Take a proposition that is known to convert, e.g., 'No intoxicant is permissible' (universal negative). Its converse is 'No permissible is intoxicant'.
We need a proposition where conversion is valid.
2
Assume the converse is false.
Suppose the converse 'No permissible is intoxicant' is false, i.e., some permissible is intoxicant.
This is the starting point of the reductio.
3
Derive the contradictory of the converse.
The contradictory of the converse is 'Some permissible is intoxicant'.
This statement would be true if the converse were false.
4
Check compatibility with the original.
The original says 'No intoxicant is permissible'. Compare with 'Some permissible is intoxicant'. They are contradictory, forming an absurdity.
5
Conclude the converse must be true.
Since assuming the converse false leads to absurdity, the converse cannot be false; hence it must be true. This is the reductio proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي
وحاصلُهُ : أَنَّه لو لم يصدقِ العكسُ لَصَدَقَ نقيضُه، وهو مع الأصل الْمُحالَ ـ كما ذكرنا غيرَ مرَّةٍ - والمُحالُ ناشئ من نقيض العكس، فيلزم صِدقُ العكس.
ما لا ينعكس من الموجُهَاتِ السَّالِبَة
وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلبَوَاقِي من القضايا، وهي الوقتيتان، والوجوديتان، والممكِنَتانِ، والمطلقة العامة.
وإنَّما لم تنعكس هذه القضايا بِالنَّقْضِ؛ أي : بسببِ النَّقض الوارد على الانعكاس،
وذلك أنَّ الوقتيَّة أخصُّ تلك القضايا المذكورة، وهي لا تنعكس، فلا تنعكس القضايا المذكورة؛ لأنَّه إذا لم ينعكس الأخصُّ، لم ينعكس الأعم. أمَّا أنَّ الوقتيَّة أخصُّ القضايا المذكورة؛ فيظهرُ بأدنى تأمل.
Chapter: On simple conversion. Its summary: That if the converse were not true, its contradictory would be true, and it together with the original leads to absurdity — as we have mentioned many times — and the absurdity arises from the contradictory of the converse, so the truth of the converse follows. What does not convert among negative modal propositions: And there is no conversion for the remaining propositions, which are the two temporals, the two existentials, the two possibles, and the absolute general. These propositions do not convert because of the counterexample; that is, due to the counterexample that occurs to conversion. And that is because the temporal is the most specific of those mentioned propositions, and it does not convert, so the mentioned propositions do not convert; because if the more specific does not convert, the more general does not convert. As for the temporal being the most specific of the mentioned propositions, it becomes clear with minimal reflection.
Source pages p. 725

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion ('aks mustawi) and identifies which modal propositions do not convert. The author first presents the standard proof of conversion by reductio ad absurdum: if the converse were false, its contradictory would be true, which together with the original proposition leads to an impossibility, thereby proving the converse true. Then attention turns to negative modal propositions, listing four pairs that do not convert: temporal, existential, possible, and absolute general (i.e., absolute affirmative or negative that are not further qualified). The reason they do not convert is by counterexample (naqd): there exist instances where the original proposition is true but its converse is false. The explanation that follows focuses on the temporal proposition (waqtiyyah): it is the most specific among these, and since it does not convert, the more general propositions (which include it) also do not convert. The argument is that if the most specific case fails conversion, the broader categories that contain it must also fail. The passage then invites minimal reflection to see why the temporal is most specific—because it involves a determinate time, unlike others which either have no time or an indeterminate time.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي (al-'aks al-mustawi): Simple conversion: swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth value and quantity, i.e., from S is P to P is S. [Note: No significant shift; stable across logical texts as the standard definition.]
النقض (al-naqd): Counterexample: a particular instance that shows the converse does not follow from the original. [Note: Here specifically a counterexample in conversion, not dialectical refutation.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Direct proof
B) Reductio ad absurdum
C) Induction
D) Analogy

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion is justified by a reductio proof: assuming the converse false leads to impossibility with the original.

Non-Converting Propositions and the Principle of the Most Specific
Author lists non-converting propositions and explains via temporal specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of propositions that do not undergo simple conversion and explain the reasoning using the most specific case.

Core Idea
The temporal proposition is the most specific non-converter; its failure generalizes to broader categories.

After defining conversion, the author turns to an important qualification: not all propositions convert. He lists four pairs of modal and absolute propositions that resist conversion. Then he offers a clever argument: the temporal proposition (waqtiyyah) is the most specific among them, and since it fails conversion, the others must also fail. This is a powerful application of the logical principle that if the specific fails, the general fails.

Guided Path
1
List the four pairs of non-converting propositions.
The author mentions temporal, existential, possible, and absolute general propositions. Understand each type: temporal has a determinate time, existential has indefinite time, possible asserts possibility, absolute general has no modal qualification.
These are all negative modal propositions.
2
Identify the key feature of the temporal proposition.
It is the most specific because it involves a determinate time. For example, 'Zayd is standing at noon' is true only at that moment. Its converse 'Something standing at noon is Zayd' may be false if others are also standing.
3
Understand the logical principle: if the most specific fails, the more general fails.
The author argues that because the temporal does not convert, the more general propositions (existential, possible, absolute) also do not convert. This is because they contain the temporal as a subset; if the subset fails, the superset cannot hold.
4
Apply the principle to a concrete example.
Consider 'Zayd is standing at noon' (temporal). Its converse is false if another person is also standing at noon. Therefore temporal propositions do not convert. Now consider 'Zayd is standing' (existential, no time specified). If temporal fails, existential must also fail because any counterexample for temporal is also a counterexample for existential.
5
Examine the exception: the proof only applies to negative modal propositions mentioned.
The passage restricts to 'what does not convert among negative modal propositions'. So the non-conversion is specifically for these types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي
وحاصلُهُ : أَنَّه لو لم يصدقِ العكسُ لَصَدَقَ نقيضُه، وهو مع الأصل الْمُحالَ ـ كما ذكرنا غيرَ مرَّةٍ - والمُحالُ ناشئ من نقيض العكس، فيلزم صِدقُ العكس.
ما لا ينعكس من الموجُهَاتِ السَّالِبَة
وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلبَوَاقِي من القضايا، وهي الوقتيتان، والوجوديتان، والممكِنَتانِ، والمطلقة العامة.
وإنَّما لم تنعكس هذه القضايا بِالنَّقْضِ؛ أي : بسببِ النَّقض الوارد على الانعكاس،
وذلك أنَّ الوقتيَّة أخصُّ تلك القضايا المذكورة، وهي لا تنعكس، فلا تنعكس القضايا المذكورة؛ لأنَّه إذا لم ينعكس الأخصُّ، لم ينعكس الأعم. أمَّا أنَّ الوقتيَّة أخصُّ القضايا المذكورة؛ فيظهرُ بأدنى تأمل.
Chapter: On simple conversion. Its summary: That if the converse were not true, its contradictory would be true, and it together with the original leads to absurdity — as we have mentioned many times — and the absurdity arises from the contradictory of the converse, so the truth of the converse follows. What does not convert among negative modal propositions: And there is no conversion for the remaining propositions, which are the two temporals, the two existentials, the two possibles, and the absolute general. These propositions do not convert because of the counterexample; that is, due to the counterexample that occurs to conversion. And that is because the temporal is the most specific of those mentioned propositions, and it does not convert, so the mentioned propositions do not convert; because if the more specific does not convert, the more general does not convert. As for the temporal being the most specific of the mentioned propositions, it becomes clear with minimal reflection.
Source pages p. 725

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion ('aks mustawi) and identifies which modal propositions do not convert. The author first presents the standard proof of conversion by reductio ad absurdum: if the converse were false, its contradictory would be true, which together with the original proposition leads to an impossibility, thereby proving the converse true. Then attention turns to negative modal propositions, listing four pairs that do not convert: temporal, existential, possible, and absolute general (i.e., absolute affirmative or negative that are not further qualified). The reason they do not convert is by counterexample (naqd): there exist instances where the original proposition is true but its converse is false. The explanation that follows focuses on the temporal proposition (waqtiyyah): it is the most specific among these, and since it does not convert, the more general propositions (which include it) also do not convert. The argument is that if the most specific case fails conversion, the broader categories that contain it must also fail. The passage then invites minimal reflection to see why the temporal is most specific—because it involves a determinate time, unlike others which either have no time or an indeterminate time.

Key Terms
الوقتية (al-waqtiyyah): Temporal proposition: a proposition whose truth is tied to a specific time, e.g., 'Zayd is standing at noon.' [Note: Standard term in modal logic; no significant shift.]
الوجودية (al-wujudiyyah): Existential proposition: a proposition that predicates existence or non-existence at some indefinite time. [Note: Distinct from absolute propositions.]
الممكنة (al-mumkinah): Possible proposition: a proposition that asserts possibility, e.g., 'Zayd may be writing.' [Note: Standard modal term.]
المطلقة العامة (al-mutlaqah al-'ammah): Absolute general proposition: a categorical without any temporal or modal qualification. [Note: Often simply called 'mutlaqah'.]
النقض (al-naqd): Counterexample: a particular instance that shows the converse does not follow. [Note: Here specifically a counterexample in conversion.]
الأخص (al-akhass): The more specific; here the temporal is the most specific among the listed propositions.
الأعم (al-a'amm): The more general; refers to the other propositions that are less specific than the temporal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, which proposition is the most specific among those that do not convert?
A) Temporal (waqtiyyah)
B) Existential (wujudiyyah)
C) Possible (mumkinah)
D) Absolute general (mutlaqah 'ammah)

Key Takeaway
Propositions with temporal, existential, possible, or absolute general modality do not convert; the temporal is the most specific and its failure proves the rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Provide a counterexample showing why the existential proposition 'Zayd is writing' (wujudiyyah) does not convert simply. Then use the principle of the most specific to justify that if temporal fails, existential fails.
?
Identify a proposition from the Qur'an or Hadith that is temporal or absolute and test whether its conversion would be valid. Explain your reasoning using the author's argument.
